4.4 BUILT-IN SAFETY SYSTEMS
Tobe systems include multiple layers of engineered safety aligned with NFPA 2, ASME, and ISA/IEC standards.
SYSTEM

FUNCTION

STANDARD

H₂ Gas Detection

Fixed detectors at all potential leak points, calibrated to 1% H₂

NFPA 2

Forced Ventilation

Continuous dilution airflow, interlocked with gas detection

NFPA 2 §7.1

Emergency Shutdown

Manual pushbuttons + automated PLC logic; depressurization, isolation

ISA 84

Pressure Relief

PRDs sized per ASME VIII, routed to vertical vent stacks

ASME, CGA S-1

Safety Instrumented System

SIL 2-capable PLC with segregated safety logic

IEC 61511

CAUSTIC-FREE ADVANTAGE
Unlike alkaline electrolyzers, Tobe systems require no caustic chemical handling. This eliminates secondary
containment requirements, chemical spill plans, and HAZMAT placarding.

4.5 ISO 22734 LISTING STATUS
CERTIFICATION STATUS (JAN 2026)
Current stage: In-process with NRTL (CSA/TÜV/UL pathway under evaluation).
Target completion: Q2-Q3 2026.
Scope: T-25, T-125, and T-2500 models per ISO 22734 (Hydrogen generators using water electrolysis).

Interim permitting path: For projects requiring NTP before certification completion, Tobe supports NRTL field
evaluation or special inspection by the AHJ. This approach provides an alternative compliance pathway per NFPA 2
§1.5 (equivalency provisions). Typical field evaluation scope and duration: 4-8 weeks; cost shared per project
agreement.

4.6 TOBE'S PERMITTING SUPPORT
• BIM-compatible 3D models — Revit format or IFC
• Equipment datasheets — Pressures, volumes, relief device sizing
• Code basis report — NFPA 2, 70, 55; IFC; ASME BPVC; ISO 22734
• Hazard narrative support — ESD philosophy, detection philosophy
• AHJ meeting participation — Code interpretation support

5. BALANCE OF PLANT INTEGRATION
A complete hydrogen production facility requires more than just the electrolyzer. This section covers
typical BOP systems.

5.1 WATER TREATMENT
Tobe-Supplied (Integrated)

RO membrane system

Raw water supply piping

EDI or DI polishing

Feed tank (optional buffer)

Product water distribution

RO reject handling/discharge

Controls and instrumentation

Pretreatment (if high TDS)

5.2 HYDROGEN GAS HANDLING
1. Dryer/Purifier — Tobe-supplied PSA achieves ≥99.9995% purity
2. Buffer Storage — Low-pressure storage to smooth production/demand
3. Compression — If storage or delivery pressure exceeds ~350 psig
4. High-Pressure Storage — Cascade banks, tube trailers, or pipeline

5.3 THERMAL MANAGEMENT
Tobe's isothermal design generates minimal waste heat. The system operates at <50°C with passive or minimal
active cooling. For most installations, integrated air cooling is sufficient—no external chiller required.

GRID BEHAVIOR

Why This Matters
• No warm-up energy wasted — Instant on/off during cloud transients or wind lulls
• No thermal cycling stress — Near-ambient operation eliminates hot/cold fatigue
• Wide turndown — Follow solar/wind curves without shutdown/restart cycles
• Grid-friendly — Capacitive load behavior reduces grid stress

granularity. This future-proofs 45V compliance over the 10-year credit period.

7. 45V COMPLIANCE & CARBON INTENSITY
The Section 45V Clean Hydrogen Production Tax Credit is the most significant incentive for U.S.
hydrogen projects.

7.1 THE 45V CREDIT AT A GLANCE

$0.60/kg

6%

Full rates require PWA compliance. Base rates (without PWA) are 20% of full rate.

7.2 CRITICAL DEADLINES & SAFE HARBOR
⚠ REGULATORY SNAPSHOT (JAN 2026)
Begin construction deadline: Verify the current deadline with qualified tax counsel. The original IRA deadline was Dec
31, 2032, but legislative action in 2025 may have accelerated this.
Safe harbor methods (per IRS guidance):
1. Physical Work Test — Begin significant physical work on-site or on custom equipment under binding contract.
2. 5% Safe Harbor — Incur ≥5% of total project cost under binding written agreements (most common for large projects).
Once begun, projects typically have 4 years to be placed in service to satisfy continuity requirements. Confirm all details
with counsel.

7.4 VERIFICATION REQUIREMENTS

• Lifecycle GHG emissions rate (kg CO₂e/kg H₂)
• Electricity consumption and source documentation
• EAC retirement records (if applicable)

Assumptions: Tobe CAPEX ($446/kW prototype), 90% CF, 8% WACC, 20-year life.

UNDERSTANDING "NEGATIVE LCOH"
With Tier 1 45V credits and low electricity costs, modeled net LCOH can be negative during the 10-year credit period.
This indicates strong project economics but does not guarantee profit at any hydrogen price. Actual returns depend on:
capacity factor achieved, credit tier qualification (verified annually), monetization pathway (direct use vs. credit transfer),
and O&M and other project costs not captured in LCOH. See sensitivity tables for impact of key variables.

TECHNICAL BRIEF

TESTING FRAMEWORK
Comprehensive Efficiency
This document provides a detailed analysis of Tobe Energy's methodology for
measuring and enhancing the efficiency of its novel hydrogen production
system. Through advanced instrumentation, real-time data acquisition, and
robust validation techniques, we ensure precision, transparency, and
repeatability in efficiency calculations. By showcasing our results and scaling
plans, this report highlights

Version 2.0 · February 2026

Confidential

T OBE ENERGY · TESTING FRAMEWORK

02

EFFICIENCY TESTING
FRAMEWORK
TECHNICAL REPORT
DISCOVER HOW TOBE ENERGY IS REDEFINING
HYDROGEN PRODUCTION THROUGH RIGOROUS
EFFICIENCY TESTING AND CUTTING-EDGE
INSTRUMENTATION.
This document provides a detailed analysis of Tobe Energy's methodology for measuring and
enhancing the efficiency of its novel hydrogen production system. Through advanced instrumentation,
real-time data acquisition, and robust validation techniques, we ensure precision, transparency, and
repeatability in efficiency calculations. By showcasing our results and scaling plans, this report
highlights Tobe Energy's commitment to delivering economically viable and environmentally
sustainable hydrogen solutions.
Optimizing Hydrogen Production: A
Comprehensive Efficiency Testing
Framework In the evolving landscape of renewable energy, hydrogen production plays a pivotal role in providing a
clean, sustainable alternative to fossil fuels. The efficiency of hydrogen production systems is critical, as it directly
impacts both the economic viability and environmental sustainability of hydrogen as a fuel source. At Tobe
Energy, we have developed a novel electrolysis-based hydrogen production system designed to maximize
efficiency by reducing energy losses, improving operational reliability, and minimizing waste. This paper outlines
the methodology used to measure the efficiency of our hydrogen production system, detailing the instrumentation
and calculations employed, and reviews results of some of our efficiency testing. By carefully selecting and
integrating sensors, we have developed a comprehensive testing framework that accurately captures the key
parameters affecting system performance. This approach ensures that the data collected is not only precise but
also easily replicable, allowing for consistent monitoring and improvement over time. The goal of this white paper
is to provide a clear, step-by-step guide on how our system's efficiency is calculated, from measuring power input
to quantifying hydrogen output. Each piece of instrumentation used, along with its unique tag number, will be
detailed in the following sections, along with how the collected data feeds into the overall efficiency calculation.
This methodology is designed to ensure transparency and repeatability, making it a valuable tool for internal
testing, external validation, and future system optimization. By laying out this process, we aim to showcase the
rigorous testing and validation procedures that underpin the performance of our hydrogen production system. As
efficiency remains one of the most important metrics in evaluating any energy system, our methodology reflects
our commitment to developing a solution that is both economically viable and environmentally responsible.
Circuit Block Flow Diagram Overview
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · TESTING FRAMEWORK

03

The Circuit Block Flow Diagram provides a high-level representation of the electrical components in the hydrogen
production system. This diagram outlines the flow of electrical power through the system, starting from the power
supply and flowing through various sensors and circuits before reaching the electrolysis unit. Key elements such
as power sensors (PoT-01A/B, PoT-02, PoT-03) and current clamps (AG-01) are annotated, allowing for real-time
monitoring of power consumption and input levels. Additionally, the diagram showcases the integration of the
signal processing and amplification units, ensuring that electrical power is optimized for efficient hydrogen
production. The layout of the circuit is designed to provide precise feedback on voltage, current, and power at
each critical stage, enabling accurate calculations of the total energy input into the system. By incorporating both
real-time monitoring and secondary validation points, the Circuit Block Flow Diagram ensures transparency in the
measurement of power consumption.
Simplified Piping & Instrumentation
Diagram The Piping & Instrumentation Diagram (P&ID) outlines the physical and chemical processes involved in
the hydrogen production system. It provides a detailed look at the flow of fluids, gases, and electrolytes
throughout the system, from the water supply tank to the electrolysis cell and the gas storage units. Critical
instrumentation, such as flow transmitters (FT-E4, FT-P1), pressure sensors (PT-E3, PT-B6), and level sensors (LTT2, LT-E1, LT-B4), are strategically placed to ensure continuous monitoring of the system's operational conditions.
The diagram also illustrates the flow of oxygen and hydrogen gases through the system, highlighting key
components like the bubbler, where the oxygen concentration is measured (OT-B3), and the hydrogen sensor (HTB2), which ensures safe operation and efficient gas separation. Each measurement point is designed to feed realtime data into the control system, enabling the precise tracking of gas production and water consumption. The
Process Flow Diagram ensures that the overall system remains balanced and that gas production rates can be
optimized for maximum efficiency.
Introduction to Efficiency
Calculation The efficiency of our hydrogen production system is determined by comparing the total energy input
to the system with the energy content of the hydrogen produced. Accurately measuring these values requires a
comprehensive instrumentation setup, ensuring that every relevant parameter - from electrical power to gas flow
and composition - is captured in real-time. By leveraging a combination of sensors and validation instruments, we
are able to achieve precise control over the data collection process, allowing for reliable and reproducible
efficiency calculations. The key components contributing to the calculation include:

1. TOTAL POWER INPUT
2. GAS PRODUCTION MEASUREMENT
3. GAS PRODUCTION & COMPOSITION VALIDATION
When demonstrating calculations throughout this paper, the tag numbers of each instrument will be referenced as
variables. This approach streamlines the calculations, making it easier to trace how each measurement is
integrated into the efficiency formula, while maintaining consistency with the system's instrumentation and
diagrams. The calculations outlined in the following sections detail how each of these measurements is used to
compute the overall efficiency of the system. Our approach accounts for both electrical and gas- related variables,
allowing us to present a complete picture of the system's energy conversion effectiveness.
Total Power Input
Measured using high-precision power sensors, the total electrical energy supplied to the electrolysis system is
carefully monitored. We also use secondary verification tools, such as current clamps and analog meters, to
validate these readings. Put very simply, the total power input to the system is as follows: To calculate the
switching losses, it is very simply: Both values are in watts.
Gas Production Measurement
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · TESTING FRAMEWORK

04

The volumetric flow rate of the gas produced, primarily hydrogen, is tracked through flow sensors, and corrections
are made for relative humidity and water vapor content. By accounting for these factors, we ensure the measured
gas volumes accurately reflect the dry gas output of hydrogen and oxygen.
Flow Rate Measurement
The volumetric flow rate of the gas produced, primarily hydrogen, is tracked through flow sensors, and corrections
are made for relative humidity and water vapor content. By accounting for these factors, we ensure the measured
gas volumes accurately reflect the dry gas output of hydrogen and oxygen.
Relative Humidity Correction
Given that the gas exiting the electrolysis cell is typically somewhat saturated with water vapor, we must correct
the measured gas flow for relative humidity. We use a temperature corrected humidity sensor, RHT-B1, positioned
in the bubbler, provides real-time data on the relative humidity of the gas. This allows us to determine the fraction
of the gas flow that is water vapor, which is subtracted from the total flow to yield the dry gas flow rate. To
mitigate the possibility of air present in the bubbler prior to system testing, it is important to note that we do start
the bubbler water full and manually drain to reduce the level once gas production has begun. In the following
formulas, the variable P represents pressure, unlike P representing power in the previous formulas. First, we have
the partial pressure of water vapor. Where, PH20 is the partial pressure of the water vapor RH (RHT-B1) is the
measure of the relative humidity (values of 0.0-1.0) measured by the sensor. PSAT is the saturation vapor
pressure of water at the gas temperature (from a reference table or more preferably a reference table built into an
excel macro).
After we have obtained the partial pressure of the water vapor. We are able to correct the volumetric gas flow
measurement. Where, QMEASURED (FT-E4) the volumetric flow measurement PTOTAL (PT-E3) is the pressure of
the gas produced in the electrolysis cell PH2O was calculated above Finally, the QDRY value, according the
stoichiometric ratio, is hydrogen by volume. So, the volumetric quantity of hydrogen gas produced, corrected for
relative humidity is: In liters per minute of hydrogen produced.
Gas Production & Composition Validation
While the above calculations are straightforward and easily verifiable through literature, and the sensors are
robust with analog gauges and meters to double-check and verify, we have implemented several additional layers
of validation. For gas composition, we utilize an oxygen sensor (OT-B3) capable of measuring the oxygen
concentration in the bubbler with an accuracy of less than 1%, across a range of 0-100%. Additionally, we
measure hydrogen concentration up to 10,000 ppm using a dedicated hydrogen sensor (HT-B2). To accurately
track the buildup of hydrogen in the system, we begin with air in the bubbler and monitor hydrogen levels as they
rise to 10,000 ppm. When this sensor is not used to verify hydrogen concentration in startup up systems, we
utilize this sensor to provide a method of leak detection Furthermore, by tracking the flow rate of water through
the dosing pump and monitoring water levels in each of the vessels - ranging from the water tank to the
electrolysis cell and the bubbler - we maintain a precise mass balance of the system. Temperature and pressure
measurements in both the electrolysis cell and the bubbler are also factored into these calculations, ensuring that
all "lost" mass matches the volume of gas produced. Finally, we track the pH of the system, which is particularly
important for efficiency tests where we simulate "sea brine." This is used as an additional verification check
against an analog TDS meter, providing another layer of accuracy in our efficiency testing.
Published Results from SBIR Grant Testing
Our efficiency testing has been validated through a series of experiments conducted as part of our SBIR grant.
These tests were carried out using both low and high TDS water, following the exact methodology outlined in this
paper, with one notable exception: the total power input was measured using a wall outlet plug power meter,
positioned between the 120VAC power source and our Siglent DC Power Supply. The reported efficiency values
inherently include the losses from the DC power supply, which is designed more for versatility than for efficiency.
The Siglent power supply used in our tests operates with an efficiency of approximately 79.1% to 79.8%, as
detailed in the manufacturer's efficiency report here. To account for this, when excluding the losses of the

laboratory power supply, we add the average observed efficiency difference (20.2% to 20.9%) to the reported
values, resulting in adjusted system efficiencies ranging from 89.6% to 92.8% on LHV.
Scaling of Hydrogen Production
Notably, our testing covered two system configurations:
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · TESTING FRAMEWORK

05

For the 2.5kW low TDS testing, the hydrogen mass produced, standardized over 24 hours of continuous operation
at full capacity, amounted to 7.7kg. This demonstrates that as the size and power applied increase, our hydrogen
production scales linearly with the power input and the anode+cathode surface area. Preliminary data indicate
confidence in scaling this process to 25kg/day of hydrogen production with no significant loss of efficiency.
Furthermore, we believe that the system can be scaled beyond this, given sufficient time and resources for further
testing.
• 1kW Unit: Designed specifically for high TDS contaminant testing.
• 2.5kW Flagship Prototype: Capable of producing more than 5kg+ H2 / Day

Future Results and Battery-Powered Operation In addition to the data published here, we have explored powering
the flagship prototype using more efficient AC/DC conversion methods, batteries, and solar arrays. While the
detailed data for these tests have not yet been released, we can report system efficiencies between 90% and 95%
on an LHV basis for hydrogen production exceeding 5kg/day. These results will be included in a forthcoming
report, which we plan to release in mid-October. We can currently confidently say we have observed an average
efficiency in a 10-hour test duration of our flagship 5kg/day prototype of 94.7%.
Instrumentation and System
Connectivity
Overview of Instrumentation
Accurate measurement is key to determining system efficiency, and to achieve this, we have carefully selected
and integrated a range of sensors throughout our hydrogen production system. Each sensor is tagged and
mapped to either the Process Diagram (P&ID) or the Circuit Diagram, ensuring clarity and traceability during
testing. The primary role of these instruments is to capture real-time data related to power input, hydrogen
output, and system conditions, all of which contribute to our efficiency calculations.
Integration and Monitoring with Arduino
Instrumentation The entire instrumentation setup is connected via an Arduino-based system, which acts as the
central hub for sensor data collection. The sensors are programmed to interface with the Arduino platform,
allowing for seamless data acquisition. To enable remote monitoring and enhance system visibility, we have built
a custom dashboard using the Arduino Cloud platform. This dashboard allows real-time monitoring of system
parameters such as voltage, current, temperature, and hydrogen flow rate, making the efficiency testing process
highly accessible and transparent.
List of Instruments and Specifications
For each instrument, we provide its specifications, its corresponding tag number from either the Process Diagram
or Circuit Diagram, and a brief description of what it measures.
Power Sensor
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · TESTING FRAMEWORK

06

· Tag Numbers: PoT-01A/B, PoT-02, PoT-03 (Block Flow Circuit Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: INA260 ·
Specifications: o Voltage Range: 0-36V DC o Current Range: ±15A o Power Range: Up to 540W (calculated based
on voltage and current ranges) o Accuracy: ±0.02% for voltage, ±0.1% for current · Measurement: Monitors
voltage, current, and power in real-time. · Purpose: The INA260 sensor is used to monitor the total electrical
power input into the electrolysis system. This sensor combines voltage and current readings to calculate the
power, ensuring accurate tracking of the energy supplied to the system. · Integration: I2C interface for real-time
data acquisition · Note: Since the voltages at PoT-03 exceed the measurement limits of the INA260 sensor, a
voltage divider circuit is used to step down the voltage. The Arduino code references the characteristics of the
divider to return the correct voltage values, ensuring safe and accurate measurement of high-voltage signals.
Temperature Sensor
· Tag Numbers: TT-E2, TT-B5 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: DS18B20 ·
Specifications: o Temperature Range: -55°C to +125°C o Accuracy: ±0.5°C from -10°C to +85°C o Resolution:
Programmable (9 to 12-bit) · Measurement: Measures the temperature of the electrolyte within the electrolysis
cell. · Purpose: This sensor is used to monitor the electrolyte temperature to track heat losses and thermal
behavior, which are important for analyzing system efficiency. · Integration: The DS18B20 is connected to the
Arduino via a One-Wire interface, allowing multiple sensors to be connected on the same data line for
temperature monitoring across the system.
Environmental Reference Sensor
· Tag Number: RefT-R1 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: BME280 ·
Specifications: o Temperature Range: -40°C to +85°C o Humidity Range: 0% to 100% RH o Pressure Range: 300
hPa to 1100 hPa · Accuracy: o Temperature: ±1.0°C o Humidity: ±3% o Pressure: ±1 hPa · Measurement: Measures
ambient temperature, humidity, and barometric pressure. · Purpose: The BME280 is used as a reference
transmitter to capture the environmental conditions during testing. Ambient temperature, pressure, and humidity
can affect hydrogen production, and this data helps in adjusting and contextualizing efficiency calculations. ·
Integration: Connected to the Arduino via the I2C interface. · Note: The BME280 provides critical environmental
data that can be used for correlating system performance with external conditions.
Pressure Sensor
· Tag Number: PT-E3 and PT-B6 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: DFRobot
Sen0257 · Specifications: o Pressure Range: 0-232 psig (16 bar) o Accuracy: ±0.5% of full scale o Interface:
Analog (0.5V to 4.5V output) · Measurement: Measures the pressure of gases or liquids in the system. · Purpose: o
PT-E3: Monitors the pressure at the electrolysis cell to ensure stable operating conditions. o PT-B6: Monitors the
pressure at the bubbler to verify safe and consistent gas flow. · Integration: Connected to the Arduino via analog
input pins, where the voltage output is converted to pressure using a calibration curve.
Level Sensors
· Tag Numbers: LT-T2, LT-E1, LT-B4 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: MaxBotix
HRLV-EZ1 · Specifications: o Measurement Range: 1 mm to 500 cm o Accuracy: ±1% of the detected range o
Resolution: 1 mm o Output Type: Pulse width, analog voltage, or serial output o Measurement: Measures the
distance from the sensor to the liquid surface, which is converted into the liquid level. · Purpose: o LT-T2: Monitors
the liquid level in the water tank to ensure proper filling and avoid overflow. o LT-E1: Tracks the water level within
the electrolysis cell for operational stability. o LT-B4: Monitors the bubbler water level to maintain safe operations.
· Integration: Connected to the Arduino using either the pulse width, analog, or serial output options, allowing for
flexible and precise liquid level measurement in the system. · Note: The MaxBotix HRLV-EZ1 provides enhanced
accuracy and noise filtering, ensuring stable, continuous liquid level readings suitable for real-world operational
environments.
Flow Transmitter
· Tag Number: FT-P1(Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: DFRobot Gravity Liquid
Flow Sensor · Specifications: o Flow Range: 0.15-1.5 L/min o Accuracy: ±3% o Output Type: Digital pulse output ·
Measurement: Measures the flow rate of liquid passing through the Atlas Scientific Dosing Pump. · Purpose: o FT-

P1: The flow transmitter monitors the real-time flow rate of the dosing pump, ensuring that the correct volume of
liquid is delivered during operation. This data is crucial for maintaining accurate dosing and flow control in the
system. · Integration: Connected to the Arduino via a digital input pin, the sensor's pulse output is used to
calculate the flow rate by counting the pulses per liter. The Arduino code processes this data to provide real-time
flow rate readings between 0.15 to 1.5 L/min.

· Tag Number: FT-E4 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: FS1015 ·

conditions and detect any shifts in chemical balance. · Integration: The Atlas Scientific pH sensors are connected
to the Arduino via an I2C interface, allowing real-time data acquisition.
Humidity and Temperature Transmitter
· Tag Number: RHT-B1 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: DHT22 ·
Specifications: o Humidity Range: 0% to 100% RH o Accuracy (Humidity): ±2% RH o Temperature Range: -40°C to
+80°C o Accuracy (Temperature): ±0.5°C o Output Type: Digital (single-wire) · Measurement: Measures the
relative humidity and ambient temperature in the system environment. · Purpose: o RHT-B1: Monitors the ambient
humidity in the vapor space of the bubbler to ensure accurate hydrogen and oxygen production calculations. ·
Integration: The DHT22 sensor is connected to the Arduino using a single-wire digital interface, allowing real-time
humidity and temperature monitoring.
Hydrogen Leak Detection Sensor
· Tag Number: HT-B2 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: Figaro TGS821 ·
Specifications: o Hydrogen Detection Range: 500-10,000 ppm o Accuracy: ±30% at 1,000 ppm H₂ o Output Type:
Analog voltage · Measurement: Detects the presence of hydrogen in the air to monitor potential leaks. · Purpose:
o HT-B2: Primarily used to detect hydrogen leaks and ensure that the system achieves a hydrogen capture rate of
greater than 99%. The sensor will provide an alert if hydrogen levels exceed safe thresholds, enabling immediate
action to prevent potential hazards. · Integration: Connected to the Arduino via an analog input pin, the sensor's
voltage output is monitored continuously to detect even small hydrogen leaks in the system.
Oxygen Sensor
· Tag Number: OT-B3 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Sensor Model Number: Figaro KE-25 ·
Specifications: o Oxygen Concentration Range: 0% to 100% O₂ o Accuracy: ±1% O₂ o Output Type: Analog voltage
output · Measurement: Measures the concentration of oxygen in the bubbler. · Purpose: o OT-B3: The sensor
monitors the oxygen concentration in the bubbler, which allows us to determine the remaining concentration of
hydrogen (since what is not oxygen is hydrogen). This data, along with the readings from the hydrogen sensor
(HT-B2), ensures that the system maintains the desired hydrogen concentration and validates the efficiency of gas
separation. · Integration: The KE-25 is connected to the Arduino via an analog input pin, providing real-time
oxygen concentration data to continuously monitor the gas composition in the bubbler. · Note: The sensor plays a
crucial role in validating the hydrogen capture rate and ensuring safe and efficient operation by measuring
oxygen levels as a proxy for hydrogen concentration.
Additional Verification Instruments
While the primary data acquisition for efficiency calculations is handled through Arduino-compatible sensors,
several standalone instruments are employed to double-check and verify key measurements. These instruments
serve as secondary verification points, ensuring that all data collected is accurate and consistent. AC Current
Measurement · Location: AG-01 (Circuit Block Flow Diagram) · Instruments: o AstroAI CM2KOR AC Clamp Meter: A
standalone clamp meter used to measure alternating current in the power supply lines. o Hantek CC-65 AC/DC
Current Clamp: An oscilloscope probe capable of measuring both AC and DC currents, providing detailed
waveform data. · Purpose: These tools are used to cross-check current measurements collected by the system's
Arduino sensors, ensuring precision in power calculations.

Gas Flow Measurement
· Location: FG-E5 (Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram) · Instrument: Welding Gas Acrylic Flowmeter ·
Range: 0-50 L/min · Purpose: This flowmeter is used to verify the flow rates of gases in the system, particularly
hydrogen and non-corrosive gases. It provides an additional manual check to ensure the accuracy of the Arduinomonitored flow rates.

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
Zeeco ARC Hydrogen Electrolysis Facility

Project Overview
Parameter

Value

Location

Zeeco Advanced Research Complex, Broken Arrow, OK

System

12× Tobe T-25 Electrolyzers

Capacity

300 kg H₂/day (600 kW connected)

Phase 2

+1× T-125 (125 kg/day, 250 kW) - provisions only

H₂ Disposition

Direct to Zeeco flare/burners (no storage Phase 1)

Scope Summary
Zeeco Provides:
480V 3Φ utility service (65 kAIC assumed) + MCC-001 with space for Tobe feeder breaker
Municipal water supply
RO/DI water treatment system (Culligan) - including skid installation, drains, and reject routing
Building/enclosure with HVAC (~10 kW cooling basis)
H₂ separation, purification, and dehydration system (between electrolyzers and flare)
H₂ piping from BL-H-01 through purification to flare - including supports, valves, and tie-in flange
work
Hydrogen flare system
O₂ vent stack from BL-O-01 - including stack height/location per code and any dilution
requirements
Site civil, foundations, permits (AHJ lead)
CAT6 network drop from facility switch to Tobe OT switch

Tobe Provides:
12× T-25 electrolyzer skids (factory-assembled)
Feeder breaker CB-TOBE-001 (spec + furnish; Zeeco installs in MCC bucket)
480V feeder cable/conduit from MCC-001 to Tobe equipment
480V→5kV step-up transformer (XFR-001)
5kV switchgear and distribution
Power electronics enclosures (EE-101, EE-102)
Master PLC and HMI
OPC-UA server + tag list + network requirements; Zeeco provides SCADA gateway/historian
OT network switch and firewall (OT/IT boundary)
Commissioning of Tobe equipment; Zeeco supports facility systems commissioning

Interface Options
Option 1: Hardwired (Preferred for Simplicity)
Junction box at battery limit BL-W-01
Multi-conductor cable to Tobe marshalling panel
4-20mA signals: shielded twisted pair

Option 2: EtherNet/IP (If Culligan PLC Available)
Single CAT6 cable to Tobe network switch
All signals via CIP messaging
Reduces wiring, adds diagnostics
Requires Culligan PLC configuration

have been updated to reflect that and will be meeting with Santi to discuss. S&P / Platts Hydrogen
Standard Pricing Breakdown
Our operational target to begin commercial hydrogen sales is Q3 2026. Quick context about Tobe Energy:
Feel free to attribute this information directly to Tobe Energy Corp. if desired. You're welcome to use or adapt the
provided data below as it best fits your typical reporting structure: PLATTS LOW-CARBON HYDROGEN: Renewablederived gaseous hydrogen indicative value at $7.05/kg delivered within 50 miles (compressed, ~200 bar tube
trailer), utilizing low-cost renewable electricity and sustainable water resources in Oklahoma. Price reflects a 30%
profit margin at full commercial scale (~900,000 kg/year). At-the-gate offer, excluding delivery and ongoing R&D
investments, is approximately $4.36/kg. Details:
• Delivered (within 50 miles, gaseous hydrogen at ~200 bar): $7.05/kg (30% profit margin)
• At-the-gate cost (excluding delivery, retaining 30% margin): $6.15/kg
• At-the-gate cost without R&D expenditures (30% margin): $4.36/kg
• Fully American-made, vertically integrated manufacturing - from power electronics to electrolysis stacks, all in-house.
• Location: Oklahoma, USA
• Production Method: Electrolysis (90% system efficiency), renewable-derived low-carbon hydrogen
• Delivery Method: Gaseous hydrogen via tube trailers (~200 bar), delivered up to 50 miles
• Price Basis: Indicative delivered cost at scale, year-three facility maturity, 30% margin
• Long-term Outlook: Pricing expected to trend lower with further scale-up and improved operational efficiencies beyond year
three
• Sustainability Credentials: Renewable energy sources, minimal environmental footprint, low-carbon hydrogen designation in
alignment with

Platts methodology

kWh) $1.47 Compression & Purification (3.5 kWh/kg at $0.035/kWh) $0.12 Transportation (50-mile delivery) $0.90

Methodology for Comparative Hydrogen-Price Benchmarking To ground our cost claims in an objective market
context we began with the complete set of S&P Global Commodity Insights / Platts hydrogen price "heards," bids,
and spot oEers listed in the appendix. After de-duplicating repeated entries and standardising the formatting
(currency, units, and spelling - e.g., correcting "Toby" to Tobe Energy), each price point was tagged along three
axes:
With those tags in place we applied the same commercial filters that govern Tobe Energy's own oEering. We kept
only prices that:
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · H2 PRIC ING

03

Any quote that satisfied every filter became Tier 1 Conventional - the precise incumbent product we intend to
undercut. OEers that passed the economic tests but lacked one or two metadata points (for example, the purity
spec was implied rather than stated, or the listing carried no expiry date) were relegated to Tier 2 Conventional. A
small handful of unsubsidized renewable or low-carbon quotes that met the same delivery criteria were grouped
under Tier G Renewable. All other entries - pump prices in the high-$20s, pipeline tariEs quoted in dollars per
million SCF, forward cost curves for 2035 mega-projects, and any figure built on IRA or LCFS credits - were
excluded from the comparative analysis because they do not represent costs our customers would face at the
point of purchase. This disciplined triage yields a market snapshot that aligns cleanly with Tobe Energy's
Oklahoma economics: today's fully on-spec SMR hydrogen collected at the plant gate trades in the $7-14 /kg
range; broader but slightly less documented SMR quotes cluster around $8-13 /kg; and truly green, unsubsidized
alternatives remain scarce and generally higher-priced, with the notable exception of Tobe Energy's own $7.77 /kg
at-gate indication. These tiers provide a transparent baseline against which our updated costs - $6.24 /kg at the
gate (dropping to $4.45 /kg as R&D amortizes) and $7.14 /kg delivered within 50 miles - can be compared. In
short, the methodology ensures that every number we cite is a like-for-like match on purity, compression, delivery
responsibility, and subsidy treatment, giving investors and customers a clear view of the discount our technology
unlocks.
• Supply type (grey SMR "Conventional," low-carbon, or renewable/green)
• Delivery mode (pick-up at plant gate, delivered by tube-trailer, dispensed at a public pump, or shipped by pipeline)
• Subsidy status (explicit mention of IRA 45V, LCFS, carbon credits, or other incentives)
• exclude any embedded subsidy;
• are quoted at the plant gate or for customer pick-up (no retail pump, no long-haul freight, no captive pipeline);
• already include liquefaction or ≥200 bar compression so downstream users incur no extra compression cost; and
• for "Conventional" hydrogen, explicitly state fuel-cell-grade 99.999 % purity if we want a perfect apples-to-apples
comparator.

1. INTRODUCTION
This document outlines a proposed organizational structure and leadership framework for Tobe Energy, reflecting
our vision for the coming years as we continue to secure funding, gain market traction, and scale our operations.
It represents an aspirational blueprint rather than an immediate snapshot, assuming we achieve the necessary
level of growth, capital, and customer demand to justify each role and team. While we are committed to building
toward this comprehensive structure as soon as reasonably possible, it is not expected that all the positions,
departments, and specialized teams will be fully staDed within 2025. Instead, this serves as a roadmap, guiding
our hiring priorities, talent development, and internal processes as our production volumes increase, our product
lines expand, and our customer base broadens. As we advance from pilot-scale units to industrial-scale hydrogen
production and integrated solutions, we will strategically add key roles and scale teams proportionally to our
progress. The ultimate goal is to ensure that Tobe Energy's organizational design supports eDicient decisionmaking, fosters innovation, meets regulatory standards, and delivers exceptional value to our customers.

CEO & Founder A visionary leader with a deep background in electrochemistry and renewable energy systems.
Combines technical expertise with strategic business acumen to guide Tobe Energy's growth CTO An
accomplished engineer with decades of experience in advanced power electronics and system design. Passionate
about pushing the boundaries of electrolysis efficiency. Previously led R&D teams at top-tier green tech firms,
published multiple peer-reviewed papers on resonant circuit designs. COO A seasoned operations professional
with a track record in scaling manufacturing lines and supply chains for complex industrial equipment. Has
spearheaded Lean manufacturing initiatives and previously managed operations at a large-scale energy
equipment manufacturer.
CFO A financial strategist with experience in venture-backed clean energy startups. Specializes in capital structure
optimization, investor relations, and financial modeling. Previously supported multiple successful funding rounds
and strategic partnerships. VP of Product & Engineering An engineering leader who excels at bridging the gap
between cutting-edge R&D and market-ready products. Prior leadership roles in product management at leading
energy storage firms, ensuring timely product launches that balance innovation with reliability. [VP of Sales &
Partnerships A business development expert with a strong network in hard-to-abate industries (fertilizer, steel,
refining). Skilled at translating customer needs into strategic sales pitches and building long-term partnerships
that drive market penetration. VP of Regulatory & Compliance A compliance specialist with extensive experience
navigating complex certification environments. Has successfully led teams through UL, CE, and ISO approvals in
multiple energy-related ventures, ensuring all products meet stringent global safety and environmental standards.

5. CULTURAL & LEADERSHIP ALIGNMENT (INSPIRED BY JENSEN HUANG)
Bold Vision & Technical Excellence: The CEO sets a technology-first vision - mirroring Jensen Huang's approach where engineering quality and innovation drive the company's competitive edge. Integrated Decision-Making:
Functional leaders (CTO, COO, CFO, VP of Product & Engineering) meet regularly, aligning technical feasibility,
operational efficiency, and financial viability. Customer-Centric Innovation: Sales & Partnerships and Regulatory
teams ensure that what's developed in R&D truly meets market needs and compliance requirements, reflecting a
holistic approach. Agile & Responsive Structure: The organizational chart is designed for quick iteration. Teams
can pivot fast as new market intelligence emerges, allowing for "fail fast, learn faster" product refinement.
Continuous Learning & Upgrading Skills: Encourage cross-functional knowledge sharing, regular training, and
industry event participation. Every leader and team member remains at the cutting edge of their field, much like
NVIDIA's culture of continuous innovation.

=== public/docs/legal/ip/Patent_Specification.pdf ===
ADVANCED RESONANT ELECTROLYSIS SYSTEM FOR HIGH-EFFICIENCY
HYDROGEN PRODUCTION

REFERENCE TO RELATED APPLICATIONS
This application is a continuation-in-part patent application which claims the benefit of
the filing date of U.S. Provisional Application Serial No. 19/088,007 titled “ADVANCED
RESONANT ELECTROLYSIS SYSTEM FOR HIGH-EFFICIENCY HYDROGEN
PRODUCTION” and filed 24 March 2025, the subject matter of which is incorporated
herein by reference.
U.S. Non-Provisional Application Serial No. 19/088,007 claims the benefit of the filing
date of U.S. Provisional Application Serial No. 63/570,102 titled “ADVANCED
ELECTROLYZER UTILIZING CAPACITIVELY COUPLED RESONANT EFFECTS FOR
ENHANCED HYDROGEN PRODUCTION EFFICIENCY” and filed 26 March 2024, the
subject matter of which is incorporated herein by reference.

CROSS-REFERENCES
Not applicable.

STATEMENT REGARDING FEDERALLY SPONSORED
RESEARCH OR DEVELOPMENT
Not applicable.

INCORPORATION BY REFERENCE OF MATERIAL
SUBMITTED ON A COMPACT DISK
Not applicable.

TECHNICAL FIELD
The present invention relates generally to electrochemical hydrogen production via
water electrolysis. More particularly, it pertains to high-efficiency resonant electrolysis
methods and apparatuses designed for industrial-scale operation, employing
advanced circuit topologies, real-time impedance matching, minimal-electrolyte
approaches, and safety features compliant with international regulations such as UL

2264A, NFPA 2, ASME Boiler & Pressure Vessel Code (BPVC), ISO 22734, and IEC
60079.

BACKGROUND OF THE INVENTION
Hydrogen production through water electrolysis is an increasingly critical technology,
driven by demand for clean, versatile energy carriers. Traditional electrolyzers—
whether alkaline or proton-exchange membrane (PEM)—commonly rely on direct
current (DC) power supplies and often suffer from substantial energy losses, high
operational costs, electrode fouling, and limited scalability.
In recent years, coil-based or capacitive methods have attempted to leverage resonant
effects to improve efficiency. For instance, McKane Lee’s “Tri-Coil System” and
Caplyzer’s “Capacitive Half-Cell System” address coil tuning and capacitive reactance,
respectively, but they do not provide comprehensive real-time impedance matching, or
they rely on large electrolyte volumes that limit industrial applicability.
Traditional alkaline or PEM electrolyzers require a large electrical input, leading to
lower overall efficiency due to high energy consumption. Additionally, systems that
emphasize resonance often suffer from inefficient reactance control, as they lack
adaptive impedance matching, resulting in instability and reduced performance under
varying conditions. Scalability remains a challenge, with many lab-scale resonant or
capacitive prototypes struggling to maintain efficiency when scaled to industrial
hydrogen production capacities. Furthermore, maintenance and longevity issues arise
as electrodes degrade over time due to fouling, while membranes become fatigued,
increasing operational costs and downtime. Lastly, some existing systems fail to fully
comply with global safety codes or lack integrated shutdown interlocks, posing risks in
terms of safety and regulatory adherence.
Another major limitation lies in the intrinsic nature of the electrochemical interface in
conventional electrolyzers. In systems that utilize bulk electrolytes, such as
concentrated alkaline or acidic solutions, ion transport is diffusion-limited and requires
high ionic strength to achieve practical current densities. This dependence on
conductive media introduces complexity in system design, limits material choices due
to corrosive environments, and demands frequent maintenance to manage scaling,
electrolyte degradation, and contamination. Moreover, many current systems do not
adequately address gas-phase management within the electrolyzer. The accumulation
of hydrogen and oxygen bubbles in electrode regions can significantly reduce reaction
surface area and increase internal resistance, further lowering system efficiency and
potentially introducing mechanical stresses over prolonged operation.

Additionally, the use of large liquid volumes introduces thermal management
challenges, as heat generated from resistive losses must be dissipated effectively to
maintain consistent operating conditions and prevent thermal degradation of
membranes or structural components. Many prior art systems lack integrated thermal
regulation, which limits their operational life and introduces variability in performance
metrics. The absence of precise control over dielectric properties and electrical field
distribution across the reaction medium leads to inconsistent breakdown behavior in
plasma-assisted systems, making reproducibility and scalability problematic.
Furthermore, power delivery architectures in existing electrolysis platforms often lack
the granularity and waveform control required to initiate and sustain efficient nonFaradaic or field-assisted processes, particularly under high-impedance startup
conditions. Without precise control of rise time, peak voltage, and waveform repetition
rate, systems designed to exploit dielectric breakdown or microdischarge phenomena
struggle to achieve stable and repeatable operation. As a result, the potential
advantages of plasma-assisted or dielectric-based electrolysis remain underutilized in
commercial applications.
Accordingly, there remains a need for an electrolysis system that supports highly
controlled electrical excitation, minimizes reliance on bulk electrolytes, reduces
maintenance requirements, and enables scalable, safe hydrogen production suitable
for both distributed and industrial deployment.

BRIEF SUMMARY OF THE INVENTION
A system and method for hydrolysis is disclosed. This Summary is provided to
introduce a selection of disclosed concepts in a simplified form that are further
described below in the Detailed Description including the drawings provided. This
Summary is not intended to identify key features or essential features of the claimed
subject matter. Nor is this Summary intended to be used to limit the claimed subject
matter's scope.
In one embodiment, a hydrolysis system is disclosed. The hydrolysis system
comprises a plurality of coaxial electrode pairs, each coaxial electrode pair comprising
a hollow cylindrical outer electrode, a solid cylindrical inner electrode disposed
concentrically within the outer electrode, an insulating annular spacer positioned
between the inner electrode and the outer electrode defining a uniform radial
microgap, and a dielectric fluid disposed in a microgap defined by the spacer. A first
end cap and a second end cap enclose the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs. The first
end cap and the second end cap each include a first terminal electrically connected to
the inner electrodes. The second end cap includes a second terminal electrically
connected to the outer electrodes. A power supply comprises a full bridge inverter, a

resonant tank including at least one inductor and a capacitor, a transformer connected
to the resonant tank, a high-frequency rectifier, a filter capacitor, and a half-bridge
pulse driver connected to the coaxial electrode pairs. A series impedance element is
disposed between the half-bridge pulse driver and the coaxial electrode pairs. A fluid
connection is coupled to the coaxial electrode pairs. A proton exchange membrane
hydrogen pump is fluidly connected to the fluid connection. The dielectric fluid
comprises deionized water having a total dissolved solids concentration below 1 part
per million. The dielectric fluid is substantially free of added electrolytes or ionic
species. The uniform radial microgap has a spacing between 0.1 millimeters and 2.0
millimeters. Each coaxial electrode pair defines a cylindrical capacitor having a
capacitance between 10 picofarads and 100 picofarads. The resonant tank is
configured as an LLC resonant converter and the transformer provides galvanic
isolation and voltage step-up from the full bridge inverter to the high-frequency rectifier.
The half-bridge pulse driver is configured to apply a pulsed DC waveform having a
peak voltage greater than 1 kilovolt and a rise time shorter than a dielectric relaxation
time of the dielectric fluid. The series impedance element comprises an inductive or
resistive component having a quality factor between 0.5 and 5 at a carrier frequency of
the pulsed DC waveform. The fluid connection is configured to convey a two-phase
mixture comprising hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, and water vapor. The proton exchange
membrane hydrogen pump is configured to separate the hydrogen gas from the twophase mixture and deliver a purified hydrogen product stream at elevated pressure.
The proton exchange membrane hydrogen pump further delivers an oxygen-rich
byproduct stream from an anode-side outlet.

DETAILED DESCRIPTION
The following detailed description refers to the accompanying drawings. Whenever
possible, the same reference numbers are used in the drawings and the following
description to refer to the same or similar elements. While disclosed embodiments may
be described, modifications, adaptations, and other implementations are possible. For
example, substitutions, additions, or modifications may be made to the elements
illustrated in the drawings, and the methods described herein may be modified by
substituting reordering or adding additional stages or components to the disclosed
methods and devices. Accordingly, the following detailed description does not limit the
disclosed embodiments. Instead, the proper scope of the disclosed embodiments is
defined by the appended claims.

The disclosed embodiments improve upon the problems with the prior art by providing
a system that optimizes energy efficiency, enhances scalability, and ensures
operational stability in hydrogen electrolysis. Unlike traditional alkaline or PEM
electrolyzers that require high energy input and suffer from inefficiencies, the disclosed
system leverages resonant power transfer and adaptive impedance matching to
minimize energy losses while maintaining stable operation under varying load
conditions.
By incorporating a primary coil inductively coupled to at least one secondary coil, the
system efficiently delivers alternating current to the electrolysis cell stack without the
need for excessive electrical input. The inclusion of an adaptive impedance matching
network allows the system to dynamically adjust inductive and capacitive elements,
preventing performance degradation due to fluctuations in water purity, temperature, or
electrode wear. This real-time adaptability ensures that the system remains in optimal
resonance, unlike conventional electrolysis setups that often struggle with instability
and inefficiency.
Furthermore, the disclosed system is designed with scalability in mind. Traditional labscale resonant or capacitive prototypes often fail to maintain efficiency at industrial
production levels, whereas this system allows for multi-megawatt expansion by
incorporating additional secondary coils and parallel cell stacks without significant
redesign. The modular electrolysis cell stack includes electrodes coated with catalyst
materials and integrated coolant channels for effective thermal management,
extending electrode longevity and reducing maintenance costs.
In addition to performance improvements, the system addresses critical safety and
regulatory challenges. It includes real-time monitoring of voltage, current, temperature,
and pressure, enabling proactive adjustments that enhance operational reliability. The
control unit also provides closed-loop feedback to maintain optimal current density,
ensuring efficient hydrogen production while minimizing system stress. Additionally,
compliance with global safety standards, including UL 2264A, NFPA 2, ASME BPVC,
and ISO 22734, is embedded within the design, ensuring a robust and regulationcompliant hydrogen production solution.
By integrating these advancements, the disclosed system not only resolves the
limitations of existing electrolysis technologies but also establishes a more efficient,
scalable, and safer platform for sustainable hydrogen generation.
The transition to clean energy has highlighted hydrogen as a crucial component of
sustainable energy solutions. Electrolysis, the process of splitting water into hydrogen
and oxygen using electricity, remains a fundamental method of hydrogen production.
However, conventional electrolysis technologies face challenges related to efficiency
and cost, which hinder widespread adoption. The disclosed system introduces an

innovative approach referred to as cold electrolysis, which eliminates waste heat
generation, a primary inefficiency in traditional systems. This novel process
incorporates advanced power electronics, a unique anode and cathode configuration,
and a high-voltage, low-current design to achieve significant efficiency gains and cost
reductions. Unlike existing electrolysis technologies such as Alkaline, Proton Exchange
Membrane (PEM), Solid Oxide, and Anion Exchange systems, the disclosed system is
engineered without exotic materials and provides a cost-effective solution.
The disclosed system achieves a cost of approximately $446 per kilowatt (kW), which
is 45% lower than the least expensive conventional electrolysis technology. The cost
figures for traditional electrolysis systems in existing literature are typically based on
large, megawatt-scale deployments and calculated on a per-kilowatt basis. In contrast,
the disclosed system, while currently designed for smaller-scale applications, is
expected to achieve further cost reductions as production scales. As electrolysis
systems increase in size, cost reductions tend to follow a greater than linear decline,
making the disclosed technology even more attractive for large-scale hydrogen
production. Even at the prototype stage, comparative analysis indicates that the
disclosed system offers a compelling alternative to industry standards, with the
expectation that its per-kilowatt cost will further decline as scalability is realized.
To compare the cost-effectiveness of the disclosed system against established
electrolysis technologies, multiple statistical methodologies were employed, including
midpoint averaging, bootstrap sampling, and trimmed means. These approaches were
used to account for variability in reported costs and ensure a balanced, fair
comparison. The analysis revealed that the results across these statistical methods
were closely aligned, leading to the selection of midpoint averaging as the primary
comparative metric due to its accuracy in capturing the central tendency of cost data.
Presently, the cost per kilowatt for conventional electrolysis systems is significantly
higher than that of the disclosed system. Traditional Alkaline electrolysis systems cost
approximately $821 per kW, PEM electrolysis systems at $1,196 per kW, Solid Oxide
electrolysis at $4,341 per kW, and Anion Exchange systems exceed $931 per kW. In
contrast, the disclosed system, at $446 per kW, provides a substantial cost reduction,
even before scalability is considered.
As scaling occurs, production costs follow the six-tenths rule, commonly used in
industrial scaling projections. This formula, which models the cost reduction trajectory
of scaling up process equipment, suggests that as the disclosed system moves from
the prototype phase to larger-scale production, its cost per kilowatt will continue to
decrease. Based on this scaling model, at the megawatt scale, the expected cost of
the disclosed system is projected to drop to approximately $177 per kW. This would

position it as the most cost-effective electrolysis technology available, reinforcing its
potential for commercial and industrial adoption.
Electrical efficiency is a key performance indicator in electrolysis, measuring the
proportion of electrical energy converted into chemical energy stored in hydrogen.
Conventional electrolysis technologies exhibit a wide range of efficiencies depending
on system design and operating conditions. The disclosed system achieves an
electrical efficiency of 94.7%, significantly outperforming other systems. This efficiency
level is due to the cold electrolysis process, which eliminates waste heat losses and
optimizes power electronics and electrode configurations to minimize energy
dissipation.
Comparative analysis indicates that Alkaline electrolysis systems typically operate at
62.0% efficiency, PEM systems at 61.5%, and Solid Oxide electrolysis—which requires
extreme operating temperatures exceeding 400°C—at 78.33% efficiency. Most
conventional electrolyzers suffer substantial energy losses due to heat generation,
which also necessitates additional cooling mechanisms. In contrast, the disclosed
system operates at near-ambient temperatures, eliminating these inefficiencies and
significantly increasing energy conversion rates.
Beyond electrical efficiency, overall system efficiency must account for auxiliary power
consumption, gas capture rates, and other operational losses. The disclosed system,
with a 99% gas capture rate, achieves an overall system efficiency of 93.75%,
consuming approximately 42.23 kWh per kilogram of hydrogen produced. These
efficiency levels are competitive with even the most advanced commercial
electrolyzers, positioning the system as a leader in energy conversion effectiveness.
A critical factor in the long-term viability of electrolysis technology is stack durability
and replacement costs. In conventional electrolysis systems, electrode and membrane
degradation present significant operational challenges, leading to increased
maintenance costs and downtime. PEM and Solid Oxide electrolysis systems, in
particular, rely on components that degrade over time, requiring frequent replacement
and increasing long-term operational expenses.
In contrast, the disclosed system employs a unique stack design that does not rely on
consumable materials. Currently tested for over 1,000 hours, the stack exhibits
minimal wear, with an expected lifetime of 80,000+ hours. The system’s stack is
constructed entirely from 304 stainless steel, a widely available and corrosion-resistant
material that enhances durability while reducing replacement costs. Unlike other
electrolysis systems that depend on specialized or exotic materials, the disclosed
system offers an economically sustainable alternative, with dramatically lower
maintenance and stack replacement expenses.

Comparative analysis of replacement costs confirms that Alkaline electrolysis stacks
typically require replacement after approximately 79,400 hours of operation, PEM
systems after 62,500 hours, and Solid Oxide systems after 33,333 hours, while Anion
Exchange stacks have the shortest operational lifespan at approximately 7,000 hours.
The disclosed system’s stainless steel-based stack construction ensures a far longer
lifespan while maintaining low replacement costs, further improving its economic
feasibility.
The disclosed system represents a significant advancement in electrolysis technology,
combining unprecedented efficiency, low cost, and long-term durability. The proprietary
cold electrolysis process enables high efficiency and cost savings by eliminating waste
heat production, minimizing operational expenses, and maximizing energy conversion.
Even in its prototype phase, the disclosed system is already more cost-effective than
leading electrolysis technologies, with a per-kilowatt cost of $446, which is lower than
any conventional electrolysis system currently available. As production scales, this
cost is projected to fall below $200 per kW, making it the most cost-efficient
electrolysis solution in the market.
With 94.7% electrical efficiency, a system efficiency of 93.75%, and a stack lifetime
exceeding 80,000 hours, the disclosed system sets a new benchmark for hydrogen
production technology. Unlike conventional electrolyzers that require frequent
component replacement and use expensive materials, the disclosed system is
engineered for longevity and affordability.
As the demand for hydrogen continues to grow within the global energy transition, the
disclosed system offers a commercially viable and scalable solution, redefining the
electrolysis industry. By addressing the fundamental challenges of cost, efficiency, and
system longevity, this technology is positioned to drive the widespread adoption of
clean hydrogen production worldwide.
The disclosed hydrolysis system addresses numerous longstanding challenges in the
field of water electrolysis by introducing a fundamentally different architecture that
departs from conventional Faradaic and bulk-electrolyte-dependent systems. Unlike
traditional alkaline or PEM electrolyzers that rely on continuous ionic conduction
through conductive liquid media, the disclosed system utilizes a capacitive discharge
mechanism across microgap-separated coaxial electrodes immersed in a highresistivity dielectric fluid. This configuration enables the initiation of non-thermal
plasma within a precisely controlled microenvironment, resulting in localized dielectric
breakdown that reduces the effective resistance across the electrodes and facilitates
efficient energy transfer to the water molecules without requiring the presence of
added ionic species or catalysts.

The system significantly improves upon prior capacitive and resonant cell architectures
by incorporating real-time impedance control through a carefully tuned power delivery
network. The inclusion of a series impedance element between the half-bridge pulse
driver and the coaxial capacitive array permits waveform shaping and energy buffering
that ensure the electrical excitation remains synchronized with the dielectric relaxation
properties of the fluid. This mitigates overshoot, prevents arcing, and allows
repeatable, high-voltage pulsing with fast rise times—conditions that are difficult to
achieve using legacy coil-based resonant systems or simple capacitive circuits. The
power electronics topology, particularly the use of an LLC resonant converter with
galvanic isolation, further ensures efficient voltage transformation, system protection,
and compatibility with grid-tied or off-grid power sources.
In contrast to prior systems that employ large electrolyte reservoirs prone to fouling
and thermal instability, the microgap geometry and ultra-pure dielectric medium reduce
fluid volume, lower thermal mass, and allow for precise control of breakdown
conditions. The use of solid dielectric spacers to maintain microgap uniformity across
all electrode pairs enables consistent field distribution, contributing to high repeatability
and low degradation rates across the electrode surfaces. Electrode fouling is
substantially mitigated because the process does not rely on continuous ionic
conduction through a bulk solution, and because the fluid used is substantially free of
contaminants or additives.
Moreover, integration with a proton exchange membrane hydrogen pump allows in situ
gas separation and pressurization, eliminating the need for post-processing
compression stages and enabling the production of high-purity hydrogen streams with
minimal parasitic energy losses. The system architecture supports modular scaling, as
multiple coaxial electrode pairs can be arranged in parallel and uniformly driven by a
single power system without the complex impedance tuning required in coil-based
systems. This modularity, combined with the robust mechanical design, such as
stainless steel and acrylic pressure vessel components, facilitates deployment in both
centralized and distributed hydrogen production contexts.
Overall, the disclosed system advances beyond the limitations of existing technologies
by offering a low-maintenance, high-efficiency, field-assisted electrolysis method that
eliminates dependence on corrosive electrolytes, enables precise waveform and
impedance control, and supports compact, scalable designs suitable for industrial
hydrogen generation.
Referring now to the Figures, FIGS. 1 through 1C illustrate various views of a
hydrolysis system 100, according to an example embodiment. The system 100
includes a plurality of coaxial electrode pairs 102. Shown in FIGS. 2 through 2C, which
illustrate detailed views of the coaxial electrode pairs, each coaxial electrode pair 102

includes a hollow cylindrical outer electrode 202, a solid cylindrical inner electrode 204
disposed concentrically within the outer electrode, an insulating annular spacer 206
positioned between the inner electrode and the outer electrode defining a uniform
radial microgap, and a dielectric fluid disposed in a microgap 208 defined by the
spacer. The coaxial electrode pairs refer to multiple electrode structures, each
configured with a specific concentric geometry that facilitates controlled electric field
distribution and dielectric breakdown within a confined region. Each individual coaxial
electrode pair comprises a hollow cylindrical outer electrode and a solid cylindrical
inner electrode disposed concentrically within the outer electrode such that the
longitudinal axes of both electrodes are aligned to ensure radial symmetry. This coaxial
arrangement allows for the establishment of a uniform and highly controlled electric
field between the two electrodes when a voltage potential is applied across them.
An insulating annular spacer 206 is positioned between the inner 204 and outer 202
electrodes to maintain a precise radial separation, thereby defining a uniform radial
microgap that encircles the inner electrode. This microgap 208 contributes to ensuring
predictable dielectric behavior and consistent breakdown thresholds across the entire
electrode surface. The spacer material is non-conductive and dimensionally stable,
and in one example embodiment, may be fabricated from high-dielectric-strength
polymers such as PTFE, PEEK, or polyimide. The thickness of the spacer determines
the width of the microgap and is typically in the range of 0.1 mm to 2.0 mm, allowing
the structure to behave as a coaxial cylindrical capacitor under high voltage excitation.
In some embodiments, a thermal management component configured to regulate the
temperature of the dielectric fluid within the uniform radial microgap is provided to
control thermal conditions within the electrolysis cell system during operation. A
thermal management component is a system or device configured to remove,
dissipate, or redistribute heat generated during electrical operation in order to maintain
components within a desired temperature range. In the present system, heat may be
generated within the dielectric fluid due to dielectric losses, plasma formation, resistive
heating during breakdown events, and conduction through the electrodes. Without
regulation, localized temperature rise within the uniform radial microgap could alter
dielectric properties, reduce fluid resistivity, affect plasma stability, and increase
mechanical stress within the pressure vessel. In one embodiment, the thermal
management component comprises a closed-loop fluid circulation system configured
to continuously circulate the purified dielectric fluid through the coaxial electrode pairs
and through an external heat exchanger. The heat exchanger may include an aircooled radiator, liquid-to-liquid heat exchanger, or thermoelectric cooling assembly
configured to remove thermal energy from the dielectric fluid before recirculation into
the feed manifold. A pump may be included to maintain controlled flow rate through the
internal fluid channels and microgaps.

In another embodiment, the thermal management component includes passive thermal
dissipation features such as heat sinks integrated into the stainless steel end caps,
thermal conduction paths through the acrylic pressure vessel, or external cooling fins.
Temperature sensors positioned within the dielectric fluid loop or on the end caps may
provide feedback to a control system that adjusts flow rate, pulse parameters, or
cooling capacity in response to measured temperature. The thermal management
component regulates the temperature of the dielectric fluid within the uniform radial
microgap to maintain stable dielectric properties, consistent breakdown thresholds,
and predictable plasma behavior. By controlling fluid temperature, the system
preserves high resistivity of the purified water, limits thermal expansion effects on the
microgap geometry, reduces component degradation, and enhances operational
efficiency and durability during sustained high-power pulsed operation.
In an example embodiment, shown in FIGS. 3 through 3B, the insulating annular
spacer 206 is configured as a generally ring-shaped structural component
dimensioned to be positioned concentrically between the solid cylindrical inner
electrode and the hollow cylindrical outer electrode. The insulating annular spacer
functions as a dielectric structural separator that maintains a fixed and uniform radial
distance between two conductive elements while electrically isolating them from one
another. The spacer defines the uniform radial microgap that is occupied by the
dielectric fluid and across which the electric field is established during operation. As
illustrated, the spacer 206 includes an outer annular body 302 having a polygonal or
multi-faceted external perimeter and a substantially circular internal opening. The
internal opening is sized to receive the solid cylindrical inner electrode, while the
external perimeter is configured to fit within the hollow cylindrical outer electrode. A
plurality of inwardly projecting support features 304, lobes, or teeth are formed along
the inner circumference of the spacer. These support features are circumferentially
spaced and extend radially inward to contact or closely interface with the outer surface
of the solid cylindrical inner electrode. The support features maintain concentric
alignment of the inner electrode relative to the outer electrode while minimizing
material volume within the microgap region, thereby preserving fluid volume and
electric field uniformity. The axial thickness of the spacer corresponds to the
longitudinal spacing between adjacent electrode sections and contributes to defining
the axial length of the uniform radial microgap. The geometry of the spacer ensures
that the radial spacing between the inner electrode and the outer electrode remains
consistent around the full circumference, preventing localized narrowing that could
otherwise result in uneven electric field concentration or premature dielectric
breakdown.
In one embodiment, the spacer 206 is manufactured from a high-dielectric-strength,
chemically inert material such as polytetrafluoroethylene, polyether ether ketone,
polyimide, ceramic, or another electrically insulating polymer capable of withstanding

high electric fields and elevated temperatures. The spacer may be machined, injection
molded, or additively manufactured to achieve tight dimensional tolerances and
consistent concentricity. The inwardly projecting support features may be formed
integrally with the annular body to ensure structural rigidity and alignment stability
under axial compression applied by the first end cap and the second end cap.
Functionally, the spacer 206 performs multiple roles within the system. It electrically
isolates the inner electrode from the outer electrode under high-voltage conditions,
mechanically stabilizes the coaxial electrode pair, and precisely defines the uniform
radial microgap that governs capacitive behavior and dielectric breakdown
characteristics. By maintaining accurate concentric alignment and uniform spacing, the
spacer contributes to predictable cylindrical capacitance values and consistent plasma
formation across all electrode pairs. This structural precision improves operational
repeatability, reduces the likelihood of localized arcing, and supports scalable
assembly of multiple coaxial electrode pairs within the electrolysis cell system.
The microgap is filled with a dielectric fluid, which occupies the volume between the
inner and outer electrodes and is confined by the annular spacer. In one example
embodiment, the dielectric fluid is ultra-pure deionized water with a total dissolved
solids concentration below 1 part per million, selected for its high resistivity and
capacity to undergo dielectric breakdown under high electric fields. This configuration
enables the electrode pair to function initially as a high-impedance capacitive structure
until sufficient voltage induces dielectric breakdown in the fluid, resulting in localized
plasma formation within the microgap.
Within the broader system, the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs is typically arranged
in parallel within a common vessel, allowing each pair to operate under identical
electrical and fluidic conditions. This uniformity ensures consistent plasma generation
across all electrode pairs and enhances the system’s ability to scale without
introducing significant variability. Furthermore, the coaxial geometry provides high
surface area exposure within a compact volume and supports efficient electric field
confinement, which in turn improves energy transfer efficiency and reduces unwanted
stray capacitance or field leakage.
By maintaining controlled microgap spacing, using stable dielectric spacers, and
selecting appropriate dielectric fluids, each coaxial electrode pair operates with a
predictable capacitive behavior that transitions to plasma conduction at a defined
threshold. This structure provides significant advantages over traditional planar or
plate-based electrodes by enabling non-Faradaic electrolysis mechanisms, improving
field uniformity, and reducing electrode degradation, while offering scalability and
reliability suitable for high-efficiency hydrogen production systems.

In some embodiments, the uniform radial microgap 208 has a spacing between 0.1
millimeters and 2.0 millimeters. This spacing refers to the precisely controlled annular
region separating the solid cylindrical inner electrode from the hollow cylindrical outer
electrode within each coaxial electrode pair. This microgap is defined by the radial
distance between the outer surface of the inner electrode and the inner surface of the
outer electrode, and it is maintained uniformly along the full length of the electrodes by
an insulating annular spacer. The specified range of 0.1 mm to 2.0 mm directly
influences the electrical field strength, dielectric behavior, and plasma formation
characteristics within the electrolysis cell. Generally, the microgap acts as a dielectric
region where charge accumulates during the capacitive phase of operation. When a
high-voltage pulse is applied across the electrodes, the electric field strength in this
region becomes inversely proportional to the gap width. A narrower gap results in a
higher field strength for a given voltage, which is advantageous for initiating dielectric
breakdown in the ultra-pure fluid. Conversely, a slightly wider gap provides greater
fluid volume for heat dissipation and gas evolution, and may offer improved tolerance
against microfabrication imperfections or misalignment.
Specifically, the 0.1 mm to 2.0 mm range is selected to balance the need for strong
electric fields with manufacturability, fluid dynamics, and system robustness. A
microgap near 0.1 mm allows for very high field intensities at relatively moderate
voltages, supporting rapid and efficient plasma initiation with lower energy input. A gap
near 2.0 mm, while requiring higher voltage to reach breakdown thresholds, can
accommodate higher throughput fluid flow and may be more suitable for systems with
active thermal regulation or large-scale implementations. The uniformity of the radial
microgap contributes to the reliable function of the system. Any variation in the gap
width would result in non-uniform electric fields, potentially leading to uneven plasma
formation, localized arcing, or premature failure of the dielectric medium. The
insulating annular spacer, which is fabricated with high dimensional precision from a
material with excellent dielectric and thermal properties, ensures that the radial
spacing remains consistent throughout each electrode pair. Common materials for the
spacer include PTFE, PEEK, or other high-performance polymers capable of
maintaining structural integrity under thermal and electrical stress. Electrically, the
uniform microgap geometry allows each coaxial electrode pair to function as a
predictable cylindrical capacitor, with capacitance values falling within a known range
based on the dielectric constant of the fluid, the electrode dimensions, and the gap
spacing. This predictable behavior supports precise control over the energy stored and
released during each pulse cycle, which is essential for synchronizing plasma
formation across the full array of electrode pairs.
The controlled microgap spacing also contributes to fluid dynamics within the system.
The annular region supports laminar flow of the dielectric fluid, which assists in heat
removal and in the transport of gas-phase reaction products away from the electrode

surfaces. The gap size is optimized to minimize fluid resistance while still enabling
efficient plasma interaction across the dielectric medium. The uniform radial microgap
spacing between 0.1 millimeters and 2.0 millimeters enables the high-field, capacitive
behavior that underpins the system’s plasma-assisted electrolysis process. It ensures
consistent dielectric performance, predictable electrical response, and scalable
integration of multiple electrode pairs, while providing sufficient structural and fluidic
control to maintain long-term stability and system reliability.
Each of the plurality of electrode pairs defines a coaxial cylindrical capacitor having a
capacitance between 10 nanofarads and 100 picofarads. A coaxial cylindrical capacitor
is formed by two concentric conductive cylinders separated by a dielectric medium.
The capacitance of such a structure is determined by the permittivity of the dielectric
fluid, the axial length of overlap between the inner and outer electrodes, and the radial
spacing defined by the uniform radial microgap. In the present system, the solid
cylindrical inner electrode and the hollow cylindrical outer electrode define the
conductive elements of the capacitor, and the purified dielectric fluid disposed within
the microgap defines the dielectric medium. The capacitance of each coaxial electrode
pair is selected and dimensioned to fall within a range between approximately 10
nanofarads and 100 picofarads, depending on electrode length, surface area, and
microgap spacing. In preferred embodiments, the capacitance of each individual
microgap is ideally between approximately 10 picofarads and 100 picofarads. For
example, with a radial spacing of approximately 1.59 millimeters and typical electrode
surface area used in a prototype configuration, the capacitance of a single coaxial pair
may be on the order of several tens of picofarads. The capacitance is inversely related
to the natural logarithm of the ratio of the outer electrode radius to the inner electrode
