radius and directly proportional to the axial length and dielectric constant of the purified
water. Because purified deionized water exhibits a high relative permittivity, the
resulting cylindrical geometry produces a measurable and predictable capacitance
even with millimeter-scale gap spacing. When multiple coaxial electrode pairs are
electrically connected in parallel, the individual capacitances sum to define an
aggregate capacitance for the module, thereby scaling the effective energy storage
capability and influencing pulse energy deposition characteristics during high-voltage
operation. By configuring each coaxial electrode pair as a defined cylindrical capacitor
within the stated capacitance range, the system establishes predictable capacitive
behavior prior to dielectric breakdown and enables controlled interaction with the
resonant and pulse-driving circuitry.
In some embodiments, the dielectric fluid includes deionized water with a total
dissolved solids (TDS) concentration below 1 part per million. This influences the
electrical, chemical, and plasma-forming characteristics within the coaxial electrode
microgaps. Deionized water, also referred to as ultra-pure water, is water that has
been processed to remove virtually all ionic species, organic contaminants, and

particulate matter. A TDS level below 1 ppm signifies an extremely high degree of
purity, where the remaining dissolved substances are negligible from an electrical
conductivity standpoint.
In this configuration, the use of ultra-pure deionized water serves two key roles: as a
dielectric medium and as the source of hydrogen and oxygen atoms for electrolysis.
Electrically, the low ionic content ensures that the fluid exhibits very high resistivity,
often exceeding 18 megaohm-centimeters. This high resistivity prevents conventional
Faradaic conduction at lower voltages, allowing the coaxial electrode pairs to function
primarily as capacitive structures until a sufficiently high electric field is applied. Once
the applied voltage across the uniform radial microgap exceeds the dielectric
breakdown threshold of the deionized water, localized plasma channels form, enabling
field-driven dissociation of water molecules through non-thermal mechanisms rather
than classical ionic conduction.
Chemically, maintaining a TDS concentration below 1 ppm ensures that no extraneous
ions interfere with the breakdown dynamics or introduce parasitic reactions at the
electrode surfaces. This condition minimizes the risk of electrode fouling, deposition, or
corrosion, which are common challenges in traditional electrolyzers that rely on saline,
acidic, or alkaline electrolytes. The absence of added ionic species also prevents ion
migration across the system, thereby enhancing the long-term stability and
repeatability of the dielectric breakdown process. The deionized water may be
produced and maintained using reverse osmosis followed by mixed-bed ion exchange
or electrodeionization to ensure the required level of purity. In some embodiments, an
inline purification system is used to continuously regenerate the dielectric fluid,
countering the effects of trace contamination introduced during operation. Materials in
contact with the dielectric fluid, including the annular spacer and fluidic channels, must
be selected to avoid leaching of ions or particulates that could degrade water purity
over time. Examples include perfluorinated polymers such as PTFE or PFA, and highgrade stainless steel.
Functionally, the use of ultra-pure deionized water enables a capacitive discharge
mechanism that is fundamentally distinct from conventional electrolysis. Instead of
relying on continuous ionic current, the system exploits the delayed onset of
conduction in the dielectric fluid to store electrical energy across the microgap and
then release it rapidly in the form of a dielectric breakdown event. This process
initiates non-thermal plasma formation, which leads to high-efficiency molecular
dissociation with lower overall energy input compared to traditional approaches. The
selection of deionized water with TDS below 1 ppm is therefore essential to achieving
controlled, repeatable plasma initiation, maintaining electrode integrity, and sustaining
long-term system performance. It supports a non-Faradaic electrolysis mechanism that

overcomes the limitations of conventional systems, such as electrolyte degradation,
unwanted side reactions, and low field utilization efficiency.
In some embodiments, the dielectric fluid is substantially free of added electrolytes or
ionic species. This refers to the deliberate exclusion of conductive additives that are
commonly used in traditional electrolysis systems to enhance electrical conductivity. In
this system, the dielectric fluid, typically ultra-pure deionized water, is maintained in a
chemically pure state, containing only trace levels of naturally occurring ions, with no
intentional introduction of salts, acids, bases, or other dissociable compounds. This
design choice is central to achieving and preserving the high resistivity necessary for
capacitive energy storage and controlled dielectric breakdown across the coaxial
electrode microgaps.
By avoiding the addition of electrolytes such as sodium hydroxide, potassium
hydroxide, sulfuric acid, or similar ionic substances, the fluid retains its dielectric
properties, characterized by minimal electrical conductivity and high dielectric strength.
This condition is essential for the fluid to function not as a conductive path for
continuous current flow, but as an insulating medium that supports charge
accumulation until breakdown voltage is reached. In the absence of ionic species, the
fluid exhibits behavior more akin to that of a capacitor’s dielectric layer than a
conventional electrolytic solution, allowing the system to operate under non-Faradaic
conditions prior to breakdown.
Operationally, the absence of added ions eliminates unwanted electrochemical
reactions at the electrode surfaces during the capacitive charging phase. This
minimizes electrode degradation mechanisms such as oxidation, pitting, and material
dissolution that typically result from continuous Faradaic current flow in conventional
electrolysis. It also reduces the formation of solid precipitates or scale, which can
interfere with microgap geometry and degrade system performance over time.
Furthermore, without added electrolytes, the chemical composition of the fluid remains
more stable during operation, reducing the need for frequent replacement or complex
reconditioning of the working fluid.
The purity of the dielectric fluid is maintained through the use of materials and system
components that do not introduce contaminants. Components such as the annular
spacers, fluid channels, and containment vessels are constructed from materials with
low ionic leach rates, including high-performance polymers like PTFE, PFA, or PVDF,
and corrosion-resistant metals such as 316L stainless steel. In some implementations,
the fluid circuit includes inline purification modules such as mixed-bed ion exchange
cartridges or continuous electrodeionization units to remove any trace ionic buildup
caused by environmental exposure or long-term use.

The condition of being substantially free of added electrolytes contributes to enabling
the system’s unique electrohydrodynamic behavior. When high-voltage pulses are
applied to the coaxial electrodes, the fluid initially resists conduction until the electric
field strength exceeds the breakdown threshold. At that point, localized plasma
channels form, allowing for energy-efficient water molecule dissociation through highfield effects rather than ionic migration. This mechanism provides a sharp transition
from insulating to conducting behavior, improving control over the onset of hydrogen
and oxygen generation while limiting parasitic energy losses.
Maintaining the dielectric fluid in a state substantially free of added electrolytes or ionic
species ensures high electrical resistivity, preserves system stability, protects electrode
integrity, and enables the non-thermal plasma generation required for efficient
hydrogen production in a field-assisted electrolysis system. This characteristic
distinguishes the system from conventional electrolysis approaches and is essential to
the controlled and repeatable operation of the microgap breakdown mechanism.
Each coaxial electrode pair defines a cylindrical capacitor, with a capacitance falling
within the range of 10 picofarads to 100 picofarads, based on its precise geometric
and material configuration. In this structure, the solid cylindrical inner electrode and the
hollow cylindrical outer electrode function as the two conductive plates of the capacitor,
while the uniform radial microgap between them, filled with a high-resistivity dielectric
fluid, serves as the dielectric medium. This coaxial geometry inherently lends itself to
cylindrical capacitance, where the electric field is radially symmetric and confined
within the annular microgap, allowing for predictable energy storage characteristics.
The capacitance of a cylindrical capacitor is determined the length of the electrodes,
the radius of the inner and outer conductors, and the dielectric constant of the fluid
occupying the gap. The specified capacitance range of 10 to 100 picofarads reflects a
balance between compact physical dimensions and sufficient energy storage per pulse
cycle. This range allows for effective accumulation of electrical energy during the
charging phase of each high-voltage pulse and rapid energy release during the
breakdown event that initiates non-thermal plasma formation in the dielectric fluid.
The use of a dielectric medium such as ultra-pure deionized water, with a relatively
high dielectric constant and extremely low ionic conductivity, supports capacitive
behavior without introducing significant leakage current or premature conduction. The
low parasitic conductance ensures that the coaxial electrode pair maintains its
capacitive identity until the applied voltage reaches the breakdown threshold of the
dielectric fluid. At that point, the stored energy is rapidly discharged, creating a
transient conductive plasma path through the microgap that facilitates water
dissociation without the need for added electrolytes. Functionally, the capacitive nature
of each electrode pair allows the system to operate in a field-assisted mode rather
than a continuous Faradaic conduction regime. This permits sharp transitions from

high-impedance to low-impedance states, which contributes to initiating and sustaining
dielectric breakdown in a controlled manner. The ability to precisely tailor the
capacitance within each pair provides design flexibility, allowing the system to be tuned
for different pulse energies, frequencies, and breakdown characteristics.
Manufacturing consistency is essential to ensure that all coaxial pairs within the
system fall within the desired capacitance range. Variations in electrode diameter,
microgap spacing, or dielectric purity can significantly alter the capacitance and,
consequently, the electrical behavior during operation. To maintain uniformity, the
electrode components are machined or formed to tight tolerances, and the dielectric
fluid is filtered and monitored to maintain consistent permittivity. Each coaxial electrode
pair functions as a cylindrical capacitor with a capacitance between 10 and 100
picofarads, providing the foundation for the system’s capacitive energy storage and
pulsed plasma discharge mechanism. This configuration enables high-field, nonthermal water splitting with minimal energy losses, supports predictable system
behavior, and ensures compatibility with high-frequency pulsed power delivery
systems.
In some embodiments, the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs is housed inside an
acrylic pressure vessel. A pressure vessel is an enclosure designed to contain fluids
under pressure while maintaining structural integrity and isolating the internal
environment from external surroundings. In the present system, the pressure vessel
defines a sealed chamber that encloses the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs, retains
the dielectric fluid within the electrolysis cell, and contains the generated gases during
operation. The pressure vessel provides mechanical support, fluid containment, and
electrical insulation between the high-voltage internal components and the external
environment. Specifically, the pressure vessel is fabricated from acrylic, which is a
transparent, electrically insulating polymer material possessing suitable mechanical
strength and chemical resistance for exposure to purified water and gas mixtures. The
acrylic body may be formed as a cylindrical or prismatic shell extending axially
between the first end cap and the second end cap. The end caps are mechanically
secured to opposing ends of the acrylic vessel using compression fasteners, threaded
rods, or flange connections, thereby forming a sealed internal volume. Sealing
elements such as O-rings or gaskets may be positioned at the interface between the
acrylic vessel and the end caps to prevent leakage of fluid or gas.
The plurality of coaxial electrode pairs is arranged within the interior of the acrylic
pressure vessel in a parallel array. The vessel maintains alignment of the electrode
assembly and withstands internal pressures generated by gas evolution within the
uniform radial microgaps. Because acrylic is electrically non-conductive, the pressure
vessel provides inherent dielectric isolation, reducing the risk of unintended current
paths or external arcing under high-voltage pulsed operation. The transparency of

acrylic additionally permits visual inspection of fluid flow, bubble formation, and general
operating conditions during testing or maintenance. The acrylic pressure vessel may
be made from solid stock, fabricated from extruded tubing, or assembled from bonded
panels depending on the desired geometry and pressure rating. The wall thickness is
selected based on anticipated operating pressure, safety factors, and mechanical load
from end cap compression. By housing the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs within an
acrylic pressure vessel, the system achieves controlled fluid containment, electrical
isolation, structural stability under pressurized operation, and improved safety during
high-field plasma-assisted electrolysis.
With reference to FIGS. 1 through 1C, a first end cap 104 and a second end cap 106
enclose the coaxial electrode pairs, providing both mechanical containment and
electrical connectivity for the electrolysis cell system. These end caps are positioned at
opposite ends of the array of coaxial electrode pairs and serve to seal the internal
volume of the electrolysis cell, maintain electrode alignment, and support electrical
interfacing with external power electronics. In one example embodiment, each end cap
is fabricated from a corrosion-resistant and electrically conductive material, such as
stainless steel, and is precision-machined to ensure uniform compression and
alignment of the electrode array when assembled within a pressure-rated vessel.
Each of the first end cap and the second end cap includes a first terminal that 108 is
electrically connected to the solid cylindrical inner electrodes of the plurality of coaxial
electrode pairs. The first terminal on each end cap establishes a common electrical
connection for the inner electrodes, enabling the inner electrodes of all coaxial
electrode pairs to be electrically coupled together in a parallel configuration. By
providing the first terminal at both axial ends of the assembly, consistent electrical
continuity and uniform current distribution are maintained throughout the capacitive
array. In one embodiment, the first terminal is integrated into an innermost portion of
each respective end cap and extends through an insulated feedthrough to permit highvoltage electrical connection from outside the pressure vessel while maintaining
electrical and fluidic isolation from surrounding structural components. The solid
cylindrical inner electrodes are physically supported and electrically interconnected
through conductive elements such as rods, bus bars, threaded studs, or contact rings
that are embedded within, welded to, or mechanically fastened to the end caps. This
configuration secures the inner electrodes in axial alignment, ensures low-resistance
electrical contact, and preserves the concentric geometry required to maintain the
uniform radial microgap within each coaxial electrode pair.
Additionally, each of the first end cap and the second end cap includes a second
terminal 110 that is electrically connected to the hollow cylindrical outer electrodes of
the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs. The second terminal on each end cap
cooperates with the corresponding first terminal connected to the solid cylindrical inner

electrodes to enable application of a voltage potential across each coaxial electrode
pair. By providing a second terminal at both axial ends of the electrode assembly, the
outer electrodes are electrically commoned and mechanically secured in a manner that
supports uniform voltage distribution and reduced contact resistance across the
capacitive array. In one embodiment, the second terminal is positioned within an
outermost portion of each respective end cap and may extend through an insulated
high-voltage feedthrough to facilitate electrical connection to the power supply while
maintaining dielectric isolation from surrounding structural components. The hollow
cylindrical outer electrodes are electrically coupled together through conductive
mechanical features formed as part of, or secured to, the end caps. Such features may
include concentric collars, conductive sleeves, clamping rings, or machined contact
surfaces that align the outer electrode bodies concentrically and maintain consistent
electrical contact under axial compression. This configuration ensures reliable
electrical continuity to the outer electrodes while simultaneously contributing to
structural stability and precise maintenance of the uniform radial microgap geometry.
Within the overall assembly, the first and second end caps not only provide electrical
connectivity but also ensure mechanical rigidity and fluidic containment. The spacing
and alignment of the coaxial electrode pairs are preserved by the end caps, which
apply uniform axial compression to the array, preventing mechanical shifting or
loosening over time. The electrical connections are configured to support high-voltage
pulsed signals without arcing, and insulation is strategically applied to isolate
conductive paths from the dielectric fluid and structural housing. This dual-purpose
design of the end caps, combining structural, fluidic, and electrical functions, offers a
compact and modular solution that facilitates reliable operation under high voltage
conditions. It improves over prior systems by enabling efficient parallel electrical
distribution to multiple coaxial electrodes while minimizing the need for complex wiring
or distributed contacts, and by simplifying assembly and maintenance of the electrode
array within high-pressure electrolysis systems.
An innermost end cap portion 112 includes the first terminal in connection with the
hollow cylindrical outer electrode. The innermost end cap portion having the first
terminal in connection with the hollow cylindrical outer electrode is configured as a
conductive structural plate that forms the radially central region of the end cap
assembly and provides direct electrical and mechanical interface to each hollow
cylindrical outer electrode of the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs. As shown in FIG. 4,
the innermost end cap portion includes a generally planar body 402 having a plurality
of centrally arranged terminal features corresponding in number and spacing to the
plurality of coaxial electrode pairs. The body may have rounded corners and a
generally rectangular outer perimeter to mate with a corresponding pressure vessel or
outer end cap portion. The centrally positioned openings 404 of the channels visible in
FIG. 4 are aligned in a grid pattern and are configured to receive or support the hollow

cylindrical outer electrodes. The body includes a pattern of threaded through-holes
408, or bore holes, distributed around its perimeter for fastening and structural
attachment, such as to the threaded bore holes 508 outermost end cap portion.
FIG. 4A, which is a top view, illustrates the spatial arrangement of the first terminals
across the surface of the innermost end cap portion. Each circular feature corresponds
to a location at which a hollow cylindrical outer electrode is mechanically seated and
electrically coupled. The terminals may comprise threaded bores, cylindrical recesses,
or conductive posts that extend axially to engage the outer electrodes. The distributed
pattern ensures uniform electrical path length and balanced current distribution across
the capacitive array. FIG. 4B, which is a cross-sectional side view, illustrates the axial
extension of the terminal structures from the base of the innermost end cap portion. In
one embodiment, each first terminal opening 406 includes an upwardly extending
conductive channel that projects into the hollow interior of the corresponding hollow
cylindrical outer electrode, or alternatively receives an end of the outer electrode within
a counterbored recess. Mechanical fastening may be achieved through threaded
engagement, interference fit, brazing, welding, or conductive adhesive bonding. The
cross-sectional view further demonstrates that the innermost end cap portion provides
a common conductive base interconnecting all first terminals, thereby electrically
commoning the plurality of hollow cylindrical outer electrodes. Electrically commoning
means electrically connecting the plurality of hollow cylindrical outer electrodes
together such that they share a common electrical potential and function as a single
electrical node within the circuit.
The innermost end cap portion may be made from a conductive and corrosionresistant material, such as stainless steel, copper alloy, or plated aluminum, capable of
withstanding repeated high-voltage pulsed operation and exposure to humidified
process gases. The thickness of the plate and the geometry of the terminal channels
are selected to minimize electrical resistance and mechanical deflection under axial
compression applied between opposing end caps. Insulating elements may be
incorporated at the interface between the innermost end cap portion and any outer end
cap portion to maintain electrical isolation between the first terminal and the second
terminal associated with the solid cylindrical inner electrodes. Functionally, the
innermost end cap portion having the first terminal establishes a low-resistance,
structurally rigid electrical node that interconnects all hollow cylindrical outer electrodes
in parallel. This configuration promotes uniform voltage distribution across the plurality
of coaxial electrode pairs, reduces parasitic resistance and inductance within the
current path, and enhances stability during rapid voltage rise and dielectric breakdown
events within the uniform radial microgaps. By integrating mechanical alignment and
electrical interconnection into a single machined component, the innermost end cap
portion improves assembly precision, durability, and repeatability of high-field
electrolysis operation.

An outermost end cap portion 114 includes the second terminal in connection with the
solid cylindrical inner electrode. The outermost end cap portion 114 is configured as
the radially exterior structural and conductive section of the end cap assembly that
provides mechanical support, electrical interconnection, and fluid sealing for the
plurality of solid cylindrical inner electrodes. As shown in FIG. 5, the outermost end cap
portion 114 comprises a generally planar body 502 with a rounded rectangular
perimeter configured to interface with a pressure vessel or housing. The body 502
includes a pattern of threaded through-holes 408, or bore holes, distributed around its
perimeter for fastening and structural attachment, such as to the threaded bore holes
408 of the innermost end cap portion. Illustrated in FIGS. 6A and 6B, which are cross
sections of a side view of the end cap, the pattern of the bore holes 408, 508 are
matched and aligned to create an end cap with a flush perimeter between the
innermost and outermost portions. The outermost end cap portion forms the external
face of the end cap assembly and is dimensioned to withstand axial compression
forces applied during assembly of the electrolysis cell system.
FIG. 5A, which is a bottom view, illustrates an interior recessed portion 504 formed
within the outermost end cap portion 114. The solid cylindrical inner electrodes are
disposed within the recessed portion 504 of the outermost end cap portion 114. This
recessed cavity 504 is shaped to receive the upper ends of the solid cylindrical inner
electrodes. A plurality of circular openings or counterbores are arranged within the
recessed portion 504, each aligned with a corresponding solid cylindrical inner
electrode. These openings define conductive seating surfaces or terminal interfaces
that establish electrical continuity between the outermost end cap portion 114 and the
solid cylindrical inner electrodes. FIG. 5B, which is a bottom perspective view, further
illustrates the recessed geometry and the arrangement of terminal engagement
features. The second terminal may be formed integrally as part of the conductive body
of the outermost end cap portion 114, such that the entire recessed conductive region
serves as a common electrical node. In one embodiment, each solid cylindrical inner
electrode is received into a corresponding bore or pocket within recessed portion 504
and mechanically secured by press-fit engagement, threaded coupling, brazing,
welding, or conductive fasteners. The conductive material of the outermost end cap
portion 114 electrically common connects all of the solid cylindrical inner electrodes in
parallel.
FIG. 5C, which is a cross-sectional side view, illustrates how the solid cylindrical inner
electrodes extend axially into the recessed portion 504 of the outermost end cap
portion 114. Fluid from the feed manifold and outlet manifold flows through the space
defined between adjacent electrodes, with access to the flow region occurring within
the recessed portion 504. The second terminal is received within a channel formed in
the outermost end cap portion 114. The channel includes an opening 506 configured to
receive and support the second terminal. The channel 602 (shown in FIG. 6) provides

a passage through which the second terminal extends, allowing the second terminal to
be adjustably positioned to contact the solid cylindrical inner electrode and establish
electrical connection therewith. The cross-section also shows that the outermost end
cap portion 114 is structurally distinct from the innermost end cap portion, with
insulating elements positioned between them to maintain electrical isolation between
the first terminal and the second terminal.
Referring now to FIG. 6, a cross-sectional view of the assembled terminal interface is
illustrated, showing the inner end cap portion 112 and the outer end cap portion 114
arranged in stacked relation and mechanically secured together. FIG. 6 depicts the
adjustable terminal configuration in which the threaded conductive screws themselves
define the electrical terminals that interface directly with the electrodes of the coaxial
electrode pairs. The first terminal 108 is formed by a threaded conductive screw that
extends laterally through the outer end cap portion 114 and through an electrically
isolated passage 602, or channel, toward the solid cylindrical inner electrode. The
screw is aligned radially so that, upon advancement, its distal end makes direct
mechanical and electrical contact with the outer surface of the solid cylindrical inner
electrode. Because the screw is fabricated from a conductive material such as
stainless steel, copper alloy, or plated steel, and because it directly contacts the inner
electrode body, it establishes a low-resistance electrical pathway between the external
connection point and the inner electrode. The threaded engagement permits fine axial
adjustment of the screw position, allowing the installer to compensate for dimensional
tolerances, thermal expansion, electrode stack height variation, and compression
changes over time. Once properly positioned, the screw may be locked in place using
a jam nut, thread-locking compound, or other retention mechanism to prevent
loosening during high-frequency pulsed operation.
Similarly, the second terminal 110 is defined by a separate threaded conductive screw
positioned at a different radial location and configured to engage the hollow cylindrical
outer electrode. This screw extends laterally through the end cap structure and is
oriented such that, when advanced, it contacts either the outer surface or an
accessible conductive region of the hollow cylindrical outer electrode. As with the first
terminal 108, the second terminal 110 serves both as a mechanical adjustment
member and as the electrical interface to the external power circuitry. The independent
adjustability of the second terminal allows controlled contact pressure to be applied to
the outer electrode, ensuring reliable electrical continuity without deforming or
misaligning the coaxial structure.
The inner end cap 112 and the outer end cap 114 are secured together by a plurality of
axially oriented fasteners that apply compressive force across the electrode assembly.
This compression maintains concentric alignment of the solid cylindrical inner
electrodes relative to the hollow cylindrical outer electrodes and preserves the uniform

radial microgap defined by the insulating annular spacers. Electrically insulating
features are provided between the inner end cap 112 and the outer end cap 114 to
ensure that the first terminal 108 remains electrically isolated from the second terminal
110, thereby preventing short-circuit conditions.
The configuration illustrated in FIG. 6 provides several functional advantages. Because
the threaded screws themselves define the electrical terminals, no separate internal
bus bars or welded connections are required at the point of contact. The adjustability
allows precise tuning of electrical contact resistance and facilitates maintenance or
electrode replacement without disassembling the entire end cap structure. Additionally,
the lateral orientation of the terminals enables external electrical connections to be
made without interfering with axial compression members or fluid pathways within the
electrolysis cell. This arrangement improves serviceability, electrical reliability, and
mechanical stability under high-voltage pulsed conditions while maintaining clear
electrical separation between the inner and outer electrode circuits.
In some embodiments, the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs are electrically connected
in parallel via the terminals to define a capacitive array. An electrical parallel
connection is a configuration in which multiple electrical elements share common
connection points such that each element is subjected to the same applied voltage
while the total current is distributed among the elements. In the present system, the
solid cylindrical inner electrodes of the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs are
electrically interconnected through the first terminal, and the hollow cylindrical outer
electrodes are electrically interconnected through the second terminal. These
terminals establish common electrical nodes at opposing polarity points, thereby
placing each coaxial electrode pair in parallel with the others. Because each coaxial
electrode pair defines a cylindrical capacitor formed by the inner electrode, the outer
electrode, and the dielectric fluid disposed within the uniform radial microgap, the
parallel interconnection of multiple pairs results in an aggregate capacitance equal to
the sum of the individual capacitances. Each microgap therefore contributes its
individual capacitance to a combined capacitive array. The total effective capacitance
of the system increases proportionally with the number of coaxial electrode pairs
connected in parallel. Functionally, this parallel configuration ensures that substantially
the same voltage is applied across each uniform radial microgap during pulsed
operation, promoting uniform electric field distribution and consistent dielectric
breakdown behavior across the array. The distributed current sharing among multiple
electrode pairs reduces localized thermal stress, improves scalability to higher power
levels, and allows the overall module to achieve higher effective energy throughput
while maintaining controlled electric field conditions within each individual microgap.
By electrically connecting the plurality of coaxial electrode pairs in parallel via the
terminals, the system defines a modular, scalable capacitive array suitable for highvoltage pulsed plasma-assisted hydrolysis.

Referring now to FIG. 7, a detailed perspective view of the hydrolysis system is shown,
according to an example embodiment. A fluid connection is coupled to the coaxial
electrode pairs. The fluid connection functions as an integrated pathway for introducing
and extracting fluids from within the electrode array, ensuring proper interaction
between the dielectric medium and the electrode surfaces, as well as facilitating
removal of reaction byproducts. This fluid connection is configured to interface directly
with the internal structure of the electrolysis cell where the coaxial electrode pairs are
housed. It serves both as an inlet for the dielectric fluid and as an outlet for the
gaseous or vapor-phase products generated during operation. Structurally, the fluid
connection may be integrated into one or both of the end caps that enclose the
electrode array. In one example embodiment, an inlet manifold is incorporated into the
first end cap, allowing purified water to be introduced into the annular microgaps
between the inner and outer electrodes through internal fluid channels that align with
each electrode pair. The geometry of the fluid pathways is designed to ensure uniform
distribution of fluid across all microgaps, preventing stagnation and ensuring consistent
dielectric behavior throughout the array. In a complementary configuration, the second
end cap may include an outlet manifold that collects the fluid and any entrained gases
after interaction with the energized electrodes.
During operation, high-voltage pulsing across the microgaps results in dielectric
breakdown of the fluid, leading to localized plasma formation and the dissociation of
water molecules into hydrogen and oxygen. These gases, along with water vapor
generated by localized heating, are entrained within the fluid volume and carried
toward the outlet via the fluid connection. As such, the fluid connection is designed to
convey a two-phase mixture comprising liquid water, hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, and
water vapor. Materials selected for the fluid conduit, such as fluoropolymer or stainless
steel tubing, must be chemically inert and pressure-rated to handle both the dielectric
fluid and gaseous byproducts. The fluid connection also supports system-level
functions such as recirculation, filtration, or cooling. In one embodiment, a pump
circulates the dielectric fluid through an external reservoir or heat exchanger before
reintroducing it into the cell, allowing for thermal regulation and removal of any
accumulated impurities. Inline sensors may be positioned along the fluid path to
monitor flow rate, temperature, or gas composition, providing real-time feedback to the
control system. By coupling the fluid connection directly to the coaxial electrode pairs,
the system achieves efficient management of both the dielectric environment and
reaction products. This design eliminates the need for large bulk fluid volumes or
separate gas collection chambers, supporting compact integration and reducing
system complexity. Furthermore, the ability to direct fluid flow across each microgap
ensures uniform dielectric conditioning, effective gas removal, and long-term stability
of the electrochemical environment.

In some embodiments, the fluid connection is configured to convey a two-phase
mixture comprising hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, and water vapor. A fluid connection is a
conduit or manifold structure that enables controlled transport of process media
between internal components of a system and downstream subsystems. In the present
system, the fluid connection provides a dedicated pathway for removing reaction
products generated within the uniform radial microgaps of the coaxial electrode pairs
during pulsed high-voltage operation. More specifically, application of high-voltage
pulses across the dielectric-filled microgaps causes localized dielectric breakdown and
formation of non-thermal plasma channels, resulting in dissociation of water molecules
into hydrogen and oxygen. These gaseous species nucleate within the annular
microgap and mix with residual liquid water and water vapor formed due to localized
heating. The fluid connection is therefore dimensioned and positioned to receive and
transport this multiphase stream, which includes discrete gas bubbles suspended in
liquid water as well as vapor-phase components. Internal passages within one or both
end caps may form a collection manifold that communicates directly with each coaxial
electrode pair, ensuring uniform evacuation of reaction products and preventing
localized gas accumulation that could disrupt electric field distribution. In one example
embodiment, the fluid connection comprises corrosion-resistant tubing or integrated
stainless steel or fluoropolymer channels capable of withstanding elevated pressure,
temperature fluctuations, and exposure to reactive oxygen species. Flow geometry
may be configured to promote laminar transport while minimizing pressure drop and
preventing backflow into the microgaps. Sealing interfaces are designed to maintain
dielectric isolation between electrical terminals and fluid passages. By actively
conveying the two-phase mixture away from the electrode region, the fluid connection
supports stable plasma formation, maintains consistent dielectric conditions within the
microgaps, and enables continuous operation. Removal of hydrogen and oxygen as
they are formed reduces bubble-induced field distortion and prevents recombination
within the cell volume. This configuration improves operational stability and scalability
compared to systems that rely on passive gas accumulation or bulk electrolyte
reservoirs, where gas entrapment can significantly impair efficiency and repeatability.
FIG. 7 further illustrates structural and fluidic integration of a feed manifold 702, an
outlet manifold 704, and a valve assembly 706 with an end cap of the electrolysis cell
system. The figure further depicts structural compression members, electrode
penetrations, and external fluid fittings associated with operation of the coaxial
electrode array. At least one of the first end cap and the second end cap includes the
feed manifold 702 mounted to an exterior face of the end cap. The feed manifold 702
includes one or more threaded or compression fittings configured to couple to an
external purified water supply line. The purified water stream has a total dissolved
solids concentration below 1 ppm and is substantially free of added electrolytes or
ionic species. The purified water stream defines the dielectric fluid introduced into the

electrolysis cell. The feed manifold 702 is contiguous with at least one internal fluid
channel formed within the end cap body. That fluid channel extends from the feed
manifold 702 into the interior region of the end cap and is placed in fluid
communication with the insulating annular spacer of each coaxial electrode pair.
Through this channeling arrangement, purified water is directed into the uniform radial
microgap defined between the solid cylindrical inner electrode and the hollow
cylindrical outer electrode. The geometry of the internal passages distributes the
dielectric fluid to the plurality of electrode pairs while maintaining isolation between the
electrical terminals and the fluid network.
FIG. 7 also illustrates an outlet manifold 704 integrated with the same or an opposing
end cap. The outlet manifold 704 is configured to collect a two-phase mixture including
hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, and water vapor generated within the microgaps during
pulsed operation. Internal passages within the end cap direct the gas-liquid mixture
from the electrode region into the outlet manifold 704. The outlet manifold 704 includes
external fittings configured to couple to downstream tubing that fluidly connects to a
proton exchange membrane hydrogen pump. The proton exchange membrane
hydrogen pump receives the mixture from the outlet manifold 704 and separates
hydrogen from the stream to produce a purified hydrogen product at elevated
pressure.
Additionally, a valve assembly 706 is shown coupled to the manifold structure. The
valve assembly 706 may function as a pressure relief valve, vent valve, purge valve, or
check valve. In one embodiment, the valve assembly 706 provides controlled release
of accumulated gas to prevent over-pressurization of the internal manifold network. In
another embodiment, the valve assembly 706 enables controlled purging of air during
startup or maintenance. The valve assembly 706 therefore enhances safety, facilitates
pressure regulation, and contributes to stable fluid flow within the hydrolysis system.
This configuration demonstrates how the feed manifold 702, the contiguous internal
fluid channels, the outlet manifold 704, and the valve assembly 706 are integrated
within at least one of the first end cap and the second end cap to enable controlled
introduction of purified dielectric fluid, removal of reaction products, pressure
management, and continuous operation of the hydrolysis system.
A proton exchange membrane (“PEM”) hydrogen pump is fluidly connected to the fluid
connection. The PEM hydrogen pump serves as a downstream gas-handling
component that performs selective separation, purification, and pressurization of the
hydrogen gas produced within the electrolysis system. This pump is connected to the
outlet side of the fluid connection that collects the two-phase mixture comprising
hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, water vapor, and residual dielectric fluid from the coaxial

electrode pairs. Its primary function is to extract hydrogen selectively from this mixture
and deliver it as a high-purity, pressurized product stream.
The PEM hydrogen pump operates based on electrochemical principles, using a solid
polymer electrolyte membrane that is selectively permeable to protons. The membrane
is sandwiched between two electrodes—an anode and a cathode—forming a
membrane-electrode assembly (MEA). When a low voltage is applied across the MEA,
hydrogen molecules at the anode dissociate into protons and electrons. The protons
are conducted through the membrane to the cathode side, while the electrons travel
through an external circuit and recombine with the protons at the cathode to form
molecular hydrogen. Oxygen, water vapor, and other non-hydrogen species do not
pass through the membrane and are expelled as a byproduct stream, typically through
an anode-side outlet. The PEM hydrogen pump is positioned immediately downstream
of the fluid connection, receiving the gas-laden fluid mixture. A gas-liquid separator
may optionally precede the pump to remove excess liquid water and allow only the
gaseous components to enter the pump chamber. The hydrogen pump then selectively
extracts hydrogen, compresses it electrochemically, and delivers it at elevated
pressure to a storage vessel or direct-use application such as fuel cells or industrial
gas systems.
An important advantage of integrating a PEM hydrogen pump into the system is the
ability to produce high-purity hydrogen without mechanical compression or
downstream purification stages. The pump eliminates the need for post-electrolysis
membrane separators or PSA (pressure swing adsorption) units, reducing overall
system complexity, footprint, and energy consumption. Additionally, the use of solidstate components in the PEM pump ensures minimal moving parts, leading to higher
reliability and lower maintenance requirements. The design of the PEM hydrogen
pump must accommodate the specific conditions of the upstream fluid connection,
including the presence of water vapor and trace oxygen. In one embodiment, the pump
includes integrated sensors and feedback control to regulate applied voltage, flow rate,
and pressure, ensuring optimal performance across a range of operating conditions.
Materials in contact with process gases are selected for chemical compatibility and
durability, including perfluorinated membranes, carbon-supported catalysts such as
platinum, and corrosion-resistant enclosures. By fluidly connecting the PEM hydrogen
pump to the output of the electrolysis cell, the system achieves direct, inline hydrogen
separation and pressurization. This configuration enhances overall process efficiency,
reduces component count, and supports modular, scalable deployment of hydrogen
production units in both centralized and distributed energy systems.
In some embodiments, the proton exchange membrane hydrogen pump is configured
to separate the hydrogen gas from the two-phase mixture and deliver a purified
hydrogen product stream at elevated pressure. A proton exchange membrane

hydrogen pump is an electrochemical device that selectively transports hydrogen
across a solid polymer electrolyte membrane while rejecting non-hydrogen species.
The device operates by dissociating molecular hydrogen into protons and electrons at
an anode interface, conducting the protons through a proton-conductive membrane,
and recombining the protons with electrons at a cathode interface to reform molecular
hydrogen. This mechanism enables both purification and compression without
mechanical moving components. In the present system, the proton exchange
membrane hydrogen pump is fluidly connected to the fluid connection that conveys the
two-phase mixture comprising hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, and water vapor generated
by dielectric breakdown within the coaxial electrode pairs. Prior to entering the
membrane assembly, bulk liquid may be separated to ensure predominantly gaseous
feed conditions. At the anode side of the membrane, hydrogen molecules from the
mixture are oxidized into protons, which migrate selectively through the membrane
material. Oxygen and other non-hydrogen constituents do not permeate the membrane
and are discharged separately. On the cathode side, the transported protons
recombine with electrons supplied through an external circuit to form high-purity
hydrogen gas. Because hydrogen is transported electrochemically against a pressure
gradient, the device is capable of delivering the hydrogen product stream at elevated
pressure relative to the inlet. In one example embodiment, the membrane comprises a
perfluorosulfonic acid polymer, and the electrode layers include platinum-based
catalysts supported on carbon substrates. Flow fields and current collectors are
fabricated from corrosion-resistant metals or coated alloys to withstand humidified
hydrogen and oxygen exposure. The stack may be modular to accommodate varying
production rates and pressure targets. By integrating electrochemical separation and
compression into a single unit, the proton exchange membrane hydrogen pump
eliminates the need for downstream pressure swing adsorption systems or mechanical
compressors. This reduces parasitic energy consumption, simplifies system
architecture, and enables direct production of high-purity, pressurized hydrogen
suitable for storage or immediate use.
In some embodiments, the proton exchange membrane hydrogen pump further
delivers an oxygen-rich byproduct stream from an anode-side outlet. The proton
exchange membrane hydrogen pump not only transports and compresses hydrogen
but also separates and discharges non-permeating gaseous species through a
dedicated exhaust pathway. Because the membrane selectively conducts protons
derived from hydrogen while blocking oxygen and other gases, species that do not
permeate the membrane remain on the anode side of the device and are directed
toward an outlet port. In the present system, the two-phase mixture conveyed from the
coaxial electrode pairs includes hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, and water vapor. Upon
entry into the hydrogen pump, hydrogen is selectively dissociated and transported
across the membrane to the cathode side, while oxygen and residual water vapor

remain confined to the anode chamber. The anode-side outlet is fluidly configured to
continuously discharge this oxygen-rich stream, thereby preventing pressure buildup
and maintaining steady-state separation conditions. The discharged stream may
include minor traces of hydrogen that were not electrochemically processed,
depending on operating parameters. Structurally, the anode-side outlet is integrated
into the housing of the membrane-electrode assembly and may include corrosionresistant flow channels fabricated from coated stainless steel, titanium, or conductive
composite materials. Sealing elements and gaskets are selected to withstand
exposure to humidified oxygen and elevated temperatures. In certain embodiments,
the oxygen-rich stream may be directed to a collection manifold for reuse, venting, or
secondary processing. By providing a controlled anode-side discharge of oxygen-rich
gas, the hydrogen pump ensures effective phase separation, supports continuous
hydrogen purification, and maintains pressure differentials necessary for
electrochemical compression. This configuration enables simultaneous recovery of
hydrogen at elevated pressure and management of oxygen byproducts within a
compact, integrated architecture.
Referring now to FIG. 8, the power supply 800 is shown, according to an example
embodiment. The power supply 800 is configured to deliver controlled, high-voltage,
high-frequency pulsed waveforms to the coaxial electrode pairs, enabling precise
excitation of the dielectric fluid for plasma-assisted electrolysis. This power supply
architecture includes a sequence of power conversion and conditioning stages,
beginning with a full bridge inverter 802. The full bridge inverter operates by switching
a DC input, typically from a regulated DC power source or rectified AC line input, into a
high-frequency AC output. This is accomplished through coordinated switching of four
power transistors, such as insulated gate bipolar transistors (IGBTs) or metal-oxidesemiconductor field-effect transistors (MOSFETs), arranged in an H-bridge
configuration. The high switching frequency, often in the tens to hundreds of kilohertz,
permits efficient coupling to downstream resonant components. Following the inverter,
a resonant tank circuit 804 is employed. This resonant tank includes at least one
inductor and one capacitor arranged to form a series or parallel resonance network,
often configured as an LLC resonant topology. The purpose of the resonant tank is to
shape the waveform generated by the inverter, enabling soft-switching of the power
devices and optimizing power transfer efficiency. The resonant behavior of this circuit
ensures that the voltage and current waveforms are synchronized and sinusoidal,
minimizing switching losses and electromagnetic interference. The resonant frequency
is tuned to match the dielectric and load characteristics of the downstream electrolysis
cell, allowing precise control over energy delivery to the system.
Connected to the resonant tank is a transformer 806, which provides galvanic isolation
and voltage transformation between the inverter stage and the high-voltage output
stage. In one embodiment, the transformer steps up the voltage to a level suitable for

initiating dielectric breakdown in the microgap of the coaxial electrode pairs. The
transformer is designed with a high-frequency ferrite core material to minimize core
losses and provide efficient magnetic coupling under rapid switching conditions. The
isolation afforded by the transformer contributes to operator safety and preventing
electrical noise from feeding back into the upstream power stages. Downstream of the
transformer, a high-frequency rectifier 808 converts the high-frequency AC output into
pulsed DC. This rectifier typically employs ultrafast recovery diodes or synchronous
rectification using high-speed MOSFETs to accommodate the fast switching nature of
the input waveform. The rectified output is then smoothed by a filter capacitor 809,
which provides energy buffering and suppresses voltage ripple. The filter capacitor is
selected based on its voltage rating, dielectric strength, and low equivalent series
resistance (ESR) to handle the transient loads presented by the pulsed discharge in
the electrode microgaps.
A half-bridge pulse driver 810 is connected between the power supply output and the
coaxial electrode pairs. This driver is responsible for generating high-voltage pulsed
DC waveforms with controlled timing, rise time, and amplitude. The half-bridge
configuration allows two switches to alternately drive the electrode array while
referencing a midpoint voltage, effectively modulating the output across the coaxial
electrodes. The pulse driver is designed to deliver peak voltages exceeding one
kilovolt, with rise times shorter than the dielectric relaxation time of the fluid, a critical
parameter to ensure non-thermal plasma formation rather than traditional ionic
conduction. Timing control circuitry governs the pulse width, frequency, and repetition
rate, enabling fine-tuned plasma initiation and sustainment. This multi-stage power
supply architecture provides a tightly controlled, high-efficiency energy delivery system
capable of matching the complex impedance characteristics of the electrode array and
the dielectric medium. It offers several advantages over traditional electrolysis power
systems by enabling real-time tuning, soft-switching operation, and waveform shaping
tailored for microdischarge and field-assisted water splitting, thereby increasing energy
efficiency, reducing thermal losses, and enhancing system reliability.
The resonant tank is configured as an LLC resonant converter 812, enabling efficient,
soft-switched power transfer from the full bridge inverter to the transformer. This
topology includes a series inductor (L), a parallel inductor (L), and a capacitor (C),
forming a frequency-selective network that minimizes switching losses and enables
high-frequency operation with reduced electromagnetic interference. The resonant
frequency of the tank is carefully selected to match the electrical characteristics of the
downstream load, allowing the system to operate in either inductive or capacitive mode
depending on the desired output conditions. The transformer, positioned after the
resonant tank, provides galvanic isolation between the primary side connected to the
inverter and the secondary side connected to the high-frequency rectifier. This isolation
ensures safety by electrically separating the input power source from the high-voltage

components downstream. The transformer also performs voltage step-up, converting
the moderate-voltage output of the resonant tank to a higher voltage level suitable for
initiating dielectric breakdown in the electrode array. The transformer’s turns ratio is
selected to achieve the required output voltage range while maintaining efficient
energy transfer and preserving waveform integrity across the isolation boundary.
The half-bridge pulse driver is configured to apply a pulsed DC waveform having a
peak voltage greater than 1 kilovolt and a rise time shorter than a dielectric relaxation
time of the dielectric fluid. A half-bridge pulse driver is a power electronic switching
stage that generates controlled high-voltage pulses by alternately switching two
semiconductor devices in a complementary manner. This topology enables rapid
transitions between voltage states and precise control over pulse amplitude, width, and
repetition frequency. The driver is designed to interface with capacitive loads and to
deliver transient energy with minimal distortion or delay. In the present system, the
half-bridge pulse driver is electrically connected to the coaxial electrode pairs, which
collectively behave as a capacitive array. The driver applies a pulsed DC waveform
exceeding 1 kilovolt across the solid cylindrical inner electrodes and the hollow
cylindrical outer electrodes. The peak voltage is selected to generate an electric field
intensity across the uniform radial microgap sufficient to exceed the dielectric
breakdown threshold of the ultra-pure fluid contained therein. Equally significant is the
rise time of the applied pulse, which is engineered to be shorter than the dielectric
relaxation time of the dielectric fluid. Because ultra-pure deionized water exhibits very
low ionic conductivity, its dielectric relaxation time is relatively long. By delivering a
voltage transition faster than charge carriers can redistribute within the fluid, the
system ensures that the initial response is dominated by electric field effects rather
than ionic conduction. This promotes field-driven dielectric breakdown and non-thermal
plasma formation instead of conventional Faradaic current flow. In one example
embodiment, the half-bridge driver employs high-speed switching devices such as
silicon carbide or gallium nitride transistors, enabling nanosecond-scale switching
performance and high voltage tolerance. Gate driver circuitry is configured to minimize
switching losses and prevent cross-conduction between devices. Snubber networks
and layout optimization are used to reduce parasitic inductance and ringing, thereby
preserving waveform integrity at the electrode terminals. By combining a peak voltage
greater than 1 kilovolt with a rise time shorter than the dielectric relaxation time, the
half-bridge pulse driver facilitates controlled dielectric breakdown within the microgap,
reduces energy loss through ionic migration, and enhances repeatability of plasma
initiation across the plurality of electrode pairs. This controlled excitation approach
improves efficiency, stability, and scalability relative to conventional low-frequency or
slowly rising voltage electrolysis systems.
In some embodiments, each coaxial electrode pair is in electrical connectivity with the
half-bridge pulse driver through the first terminal and the second terminal, such that

the pulsed voltage generated by the half-bridge is applied directly across the solid
cylindrical inner electrode and the hollow cylindrical outer electrode. In this
configuration, the applied pulsed voltage has a peak amplitude greater than 1 kilovolt
and is delivered with sufficient rise time and field intensity to establish an electric field
within the uniform radial microgap that exceeds the dielectric strength of the purified
dielectric fluid. For example, with a radial spacing in the range of approximately 0.1
millimeters to 2.0 millimeters, a peak voltage of 1 kilovolt corresponds to an average
electric field on the order of 0.5 to 10 megavolts per meter, depending on the selected
gap dimension. In embodiments utilizing a radial spacing of approximately 1.59
millimeters, a 1 kilovolt pulse produces an average field of approximately 0.63
megavolts per meter, while higher peak amplitudes proportionally increase the field
strength to approach or exceed localized breakdown thresholds, particularly in the
presence of field enhancement and microbubble formation. Upon application of the
high-voltage pulse, localized dielectric breakdown occurs within the uniform radial
microgap. This breakdown is spatially confined within the annular region defined by the
insulating annular spacer and the concentric electrode geometry. The breakdown
event results in the formation of a non-thermal plasma channel extending between the
solid cylindrical inner electrode and the hollow cylindrical outer electrode. The plasma
channel provides a transient conductive pathway through the previously high-resistivity
dielectric fluid. The non-thermal plasma is characterized by high electron energy
relative to bulk fluid temperature, enabling molecular dissociation and ionization
processes without requiring bulk heating of the dielectric fluid.
Prior to breakdown, the effective resistance across each coaxial electrode pair is
greater than approximately 1 megohm, and in certain embodiments may be
substantially higher depending on water purity and temperature. For example, purified
water having a total dissolved solids concentration below 1 ppm and bulk conductivity
on the order of 10⁻⁴ to 10⁻⁵ siemens per meter may exhibit resistances across the
defined microgap that extend into the multi-megaohm range under low-field conditions.
Following plasma channel formation, the effective resistance across the electrode pair
is reduced into a range between approximately 1 kilohm and 10 kilohms. In one target
operating condition, the effective plasma resistance is approximately 5 kilohms. This
represents a dynamic resistance reduction of several orders of magnitude, for example
from greater than 1,000,000 ohms to approximately 1,000 to 10,000 ohms, while the
intrinsic cylindrical capacitance of the microgap remains present in parallel. The
confinement of the breakdown event within the uniform radial microgap promotes
repeatable plasma initiation, limits arc propagation beyond the defined annular region,
and preserves structural integrity of the electrode assembly during repetitive highvoltage pulsing. The controlled transition between a high-resistance capacitive state
and a lower-resistance plasma-assisted state enables predictable energy deposition

per pulse and scalable aggregation of multiple coaxial electrode pairs in parallel to
achieve higher overall power levels.
A series impedance element 814 is disposed between the half-bridge pulse driver and
the coaxial electrode pairs, electrically in line with the output of the pulse driver and the
input terminals of the electrode array. The series impedance element manages
electrical behavior during high-voltage pulsed operation by regulating current flow,
buffering energy delivery, and shaping the pulse waveform applied across the
microgap between the coaxial electrodes. Introduction of a defined impedance into the
circuit improves control over pulse rise time, suppresses overshoot, and governs
energy dissipation during dielectric breakdown events. In one embodiment, the series
impedance element comprises an inductive component such as an air-core or ferritecore inductor. The inductive component stores energy in a magnetic field when current
passes through a conductive winding and opposes rapid changes in current according
to its inductance value. This moderates the rate at which charge accumulates across
the coaxial capacitive structures, which is significant due to the rapid transition from
high resistance to low resistance that occurs in the dielectric fluid during plasma
formation. The inductive impedance limits current, prevents voltage collapse, and
ensures that energy stored in the filter capacitor is delivered in a controlled and nondestructive manner. In another embodiment, the series impedance element comprises
a resistive component, which limits current by dissipating electrical energy as heat
according to its resistance value. Suitable implementations include high-voltage
wirewound resistors, carbon film resistors, or other dissipative elements capable of
handling transient power loads. The resistive component smooths rapid fluctuations
and absorbs excess energy that could otherwise produce circuit instability or electrical
noise.
The inductive or resistive component is selected to provide a quality factor between
0.5 and 5 at a carrier frequency of the pulsed DC waveform. The quality factor is a
dimensionless parameter representing the ratio of reactive energy stored in the
component to energy dissipated per cycle at a specified operating frequency. The
carrier frequency of the pulsed DC waveform corresponds to the fundamental
switching frequency of the half-bridge pulse driver that generates the high-voltage
pulses applied to the coaxial electrode pairs. The plurality of coaxial electrode pairs
collectively define a capacitive array due to the cylindrical capacitor geometry formed
by the inner and outer electrodes and the intervening dielectric fluid. Within the
specified quality factor range, the series impedance element provides sufficient
reactive behavior to moderate transient current during rapid voltage rise while
introducing controlled damping to suppress oscillation and overshoot associated with
parasitic inductance and capacitance in the circuit.

In certain configurations, the series impedance element combines inductive and
resistive characteristics in a composite network that balances energy buffering with
damping. Such a configuration permits tuning of circuit response to match the complex
impedance of the coaxial electrode array and the dielectric environment, which may
vary as a function of temperature, fluid purity, or pulse repetition rate. When
implemented as an inductor, the component may comprise a wound conductive coil
formed on an air core or a magnetic core constructed from ferrite or powdered iron
materials suitable for high-frequency operation. When implemented as a resistor, the
component may comprise a high-voltage wirewound, metal film, or thick-film structure
designed to withstand pulsed energy loads.
The series impedance element also protects the half-bridge pulse driver by reducing
reflected energy and mitigating sudden load collapse that could otherwise damage
switching components. The element acts as a passive filter that stabilizes the system
against transient behavior associated with dielectric breakdown or plasma collapse
within the microgaps. In systems employing fast rise time pulses and peak voltages
exceeding one kilovolt, the series impedance element ensures that energy is delivered
with temporal precision sufficient to initiate controlled non-thermal plasma formation
while limiting premature arcing and component stress. In an ideal embodiment, the
quality factor is maintained above 1 at the carrier frequency of the pulsed DC
waveform. A quality factor greater than 1 indicates that reactive energy storage
exceeds resistive dissipation, promoting efficient transfer of pulse energy into the
capacitive electrode pairs while maintaining adequate damping for stable operation.
This improves pulse fidelity, enhances repeatability of dielectric breakdown within the
uniform radial microgap, and reduces electrical stress on switching components
relative to configurations lacking controlled impedance characteristics.
Referring now to FIGS. 9 - 12, an example system and methods for hydrogen
production using an electrolyzer stack in a resonant circuit is shown. The circuit block
flow diagram of FIG. 9 provides a high-level representation of the electrical
components in the hydrogen production system. This diagram outlines the flow of
electrical power through the system, starting from the power supply and flowing
through various sensors and circuits before reaching the electrolysis unit. Key
elements such as power sensors (PoT-01A/B, PoT-02, PoT-03) and current clamps
(AG-01) are annotated, allowing for real-time monitoring of power consumption and
input levels.
Additionally, the diagram showcases the integration of the signal processing and
amplification units, ensuring that electrical power is optimized for efficient hydrogen
production. The layout of the circuit is designed to provide precise feedback on
voltage, current, and power at each critical stage, enabling accurate calculations of the
total energy input into the system. By incorporating both real-time monitoring and

secondary validation points, the Circuit Block Flow Diagram ensures transparency in
the measurement of power consumption.
The piping and instrumentation diagram of FIG. 10 outlines the physical and chemical
processes involved in the hydrogen production system. It provides a detailed look at
the flow of fluids, gases, and electrolytes throughout the system, from the water supply
tank to the electrolysis cell and the gas storage units. Critical instrumentation, such as
flow transmitters (FT-E4, FT-P1), pressure sensors (PT-E3, PT-B6), and level sensors
(LT-T2, LTE1, LT-B4), are strategically placed to ensure continuous monitoring of the
system’s operational conditions. The diagram also illustrates the flow of oxygen and
hydrogen gases through the system, highlighting key components like the bubbler,
where the oxygen concentration is measured (OT-B3), and the hydrogen sensor (HTB2), which ensures safe operation and efficient gas separation. Each measurement
point is designed to feed real-time data into the control system, enabling the precise
tracking of gas production and water consumption. The process flow ensures that the
overall system remains balanced and that gas production rates can be optimized for
maximum efficiency.
The efficiency of the hydrogen production system is determined by comparing the total
energy input to the system with the energy content of the hydrogen produced.
Accurately measuring these values requires a comprehensive instrumentation setup,
ensuring that every relevant parameter, from electrical power to gas flow and
composition, is captured in real-time. By leveraging a combination of sensors and
validation instruments, we are able to achieve precise control over the data collection
process, allowing for reliable and reproducible efficiency calculations. The key
components contributing to the calculation include total power input, gas production
measurement, and gas production & composition validation.
When demonstrating calculations throughout this paper, the tag numbers of each
instrument will be referenced as variables. This approach streamlines the calculations,
making it easier to trace how each measurement is integrated into the efficiency
formula while maintaining consistency with the system’s instrumentation and diagrams.
The calculations outlined in the following sections detail how each of these
measurements is used to compute the overall efficiency of the system. The present
invention’s approach accounts for both electrical and gas-related variables, allowing us
to present a complete picture of the system’s energy conversion effectiveness.
Total power input is measured using high-precision power sensors, carefully
monitoring the total electrical energy supplied to the electrolysis system. Secondary
verification tools, such as current clamps and analog meters, are used to validate
these readings. Put simply, the total power input to the system is given by:

Both values are expressed in watts.
Gas production measurement involves tracking the volumetric flow rate of the gas
produced, primarily hydrogen, through flow sensors, with corrections made for relative
humidity and water vapor content. This ensures that the measured gas volumes
accurately reflect the dry gas output of hydrogen and oxygen. The gas volumetric flow
rate exiting the electrolysis cell is measured directly via FT-E4, which is validated
against the analog flowmeter FG-E5. Given that the gas exiting the electrolysis cell is
typically saturated with water vapor, a temperature-corrected humidity sensor (RHTB1) positioned in the bubbler provides real-time data on the relative humidity of the
gas. This allows us to determine the fraction of the gas flow that is water vapor, which
is subtracted from the total flow to yield the dry gas flow rate.
To mitigate the possibility of air in the bubbler prior to system testing, the bubbler is
initially filled with water and then manually drained to reduce the level once gas
production begins. In the following formulas, the variable P represents pressure, unlike
its previous use for power. The partial pressure of water vapor is given by:
