Gas production and composition validation involve additional layers of verification.
While the above calculations are straightforward and verified through literature, robust
sensors with analog gauges and meters are used to double-check measurements. For
gas composition, an oxygen sensor (OT-B3) measures the oxygen concentration in the
bubbler with an accuracy of less than 1% across a 0-100% range. A hydrogen sensor
(HT-B2) is also used to measure hydrogen concentration up to 10,000 ppm. To track
hydrogen buildup in the system, the bubbler is initially filled with air, and hydrogen
levels are monitored as they rise to 10,000 ppm. When the hydrogen sensor is not
used for concentration verification, it serves as a leak detection mechanism.

Furthermore, by tracking the flow rate of water through the dosing pump and
monitoring water levels across the system, from the water tank to the electrolysis cell
and bubbler, a precise mass balance is maintained. Temperature and pressure
measurements in both the electrolysis cell and the bubbler are factored into
calculations to ensure that all “lost” mass aligns with the gas produced. Additionally,
the system’s pH is tracked, particularly when simulating “sea brine” for efficiency tests.
A total dissolved solids (TDS) meter is used as an analog verification check, adding
another layer of accuracy to the efficiency testing.
Results from testing validate these efficiency calculations through a series of
experiments. Conducted using both low and high TDS water, these tests followed the
methodology outlined in this paper, with one exception: total power input was
measured using a wall outlet plug power meter positioned between the 120VAC power
source and the Siglent DC power supply. The reported efficiency values inherently
include losses from the DC power supply, which operates with an efficiency of
approximately 79.1% to 79.8%. To account for this, when excluding laboratory power
supply losses, an efficiency adjustment is applied, resulting in reported system
efficiencies ranging from 89.6% to 92.8% on the lower heating value (LHV) basis.
Hydrogen production scaling was tested in two configurations: a 1kW unit for high TDS
contaminant testing and a 2.5kW flagship prototype capable of producing more than
5kg of hydrogen per day. In low TDS testing, the flagship prototype produced 5.76kg of
hydrogen over 24 hours of continuous operation at full capacity, demonstrating that
hydrogen production scales linearly with power input and anode+cathode surface
area. Preliminary data suggest that scaling the system to 25kg/day can be achieved
without significant efficiency losses, and with further testing, even greater production
capacities may be attainable.
Future results and battery-powered operation testing indicate that powering the
flagship prototype with more efficient AC/DC conversion methods, batteries, and solar
arrays yields system efficiencies between 90% and 95% on an LHV basis for hydrogen
production exceeding 5kg/day. However, in a 10-hour test, an average efficiency of
94.7% was observed for the flagship 5kg/day prototype.
Instrumentation and system connectivity play a crucial role in efficiency determination.
Each sensor is tagged and mapped to either the Process Diagram (P&ID) or the
Circuit Diagram, ensuring clarity and traceability during testing. An Arduino-based
system serves as the central hub for sensor data collection, interfacing with sensors
for seamless data acquisition. A custom dashboard built using the Arduino Cloud
platform enables real-time monitoring of system parameters, such as voltage, current,
temperature, and hydrogen flow rate, enhancing accessibility and transparency.

A detailed list of instruments and specifications, including power sensors, temperature
sensors, environmental reference sensors, pressure sensors, level sensors, flow
transmitters, gas flow transmitters, pH transmitters, humidity sensors, and hydrogen
and oxygen sensors, ensures the accuracy of measurements. Additional verification
instruments, such as AC current measurement tools, gas flow meters, and power
supply analog gauges, serve as secondary validation points, ensuring the reliability
and consistency of the collected data.
The power sensor used in the system is the INA260, identified with tag numbers
PoT-01A/B, PoT-02, and PoT-03 in the Block Flow Circuit Diagram. It operates within a
voltage range of 0-36V DC, a current range of ±15A, and a power capacity of up to
540W, with an accuracy of ±0.02% for voltage and ±0.1% for current. This sensor is
responsible for real-time monitoring of voltage, current, and power input to the
electrolysis system, ensuring accurate energy tracking. Integrated via an I2C interface,
the INA260 allows for real-time data acquisition. However, since the voltage at PoT-03
exceeds its measurement limits, a voltage divider circuit is employed to step down the
voltage, with Arduino code referencing the divider’s characteristics to return accurate
voltage values safely.
The system also incorporates temperature sensors, specifically the DS18B20, tagged
as TT-E2 and TT-B5 in the Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram. This sensor
measures electrolyte temperature within the electrolysis cell, with a range from -55°C
to +125°C and an accuracy of ±0.5°C within -10°C to +85°C. Temperature monitoring
is crucial for tracking heat losses and thermal behavior, both of which are essential for
assessing system efficiency. The DS18B20 connects to the Arduino using a One-Wire
interface, allowing multiple sensors to operate on the same data line for
comprehensive temperature monitoring.
An environmental reference sensor, the BME280, tagged as RefT-R1, is employed to
measure ambient temperature, humidity, and barometric pressure. With a temperature
range of -40°C to +85°C, a humidity range of 0% to 100% RH, and a pressure range of
300 hPa to 1100 hPa, it provides crucial environmental data that may impact hydrogen
production efficiency. It offers an accuracy of ±1.0°C for temperature, ±3% for humidity,
and ±1 hPa for pressure measurements. Integrated via an I2C interface, the BME280
helps contextualize efficiency calculations by correlating system performance with
environmental conditions.
Pressure sensors, including the DFRobot Sen0257, are designated under tag numbers
PT-E3 and PT-B6 in the Simplified Piping & Instrumentation Diagram. They operate
within a pressure range of 0-232 psig (16 bar) and have an accuracy of ±0.5% of the
full scale. PT-E3 monitors the pressure at the electrolysis cell, ensuring stable
operating conditions, while PT-B6 measures pressure at the bubbler to verify safe and

consistent gas flow. These sensors integrate with the Arduino via analog input pins,
where voltage outputs are converted to pressure values using a calibration curve.
For liquid level monitoring, the system uses MaxBotix HRLV-EZ1 sensors, tagged as
LT-T2, LT-E1, and LT-B4. These sensors measure liquid levels within a range of 1 mm
to 500 cm, with an accuracy of ±1% and a resolution of 1 mm. LT-T2 tracks the water
level in the storage tank to prevent overflow, LT-E1 ensures stable water levels within
the electrolysis cell, and LT-B4 monitors the bubbler’s water level for safe operation.
These sensors integrate with the Arduino through pulse width, analog voltage, or serial
output interfaces, ensuring precise liquid level readings.
Liquid flow measurement is conducted using the DFRobot Gravity Liquid Flow Sensor,
tagged as FT-P1, with a flow range of 0.15-1.5 L/min and an accuracy of ±3%. This
sensor monitors the real-time flow rate of the dosing pump, ensuring that the correct
volume of liquid is delivered for optimal system performance. It connects to the Arduino
via a digital input pin, with its pulse output used to calculate flow rate by counting
pulses per liter. The Arduino code then processes this data to provide real-time flow
rate readings.
Gas flow is monitored using the FS1015 sensor, tagged as FT-E4, with a flow range of
0-50 L/min and an accuracy of ±2%. This sensor measures the flow rate of hydrogen
and oxygen produced in the electrolysis cell. Integrated via an analog input pin, its
voltage output corresponds to the gas flow rate, which is converted into liters per
minute using calibration data in the Arduino code.
For pH monitoring, the system employs Atlas Scientific pH sensors, tagged as PhT-T1
and PhT-B7. These sensors measure pH levels within a range of 0-14, with an
accuracy of ±0.002 pH. PhT-T1 monitors the pH of the water in the storage tank to
ensure it remains optimal for electrolysis, while PhT-B7 tracks pH levels in the bubbler
to maintain stable operating conditions and detect chemical shifts. These sensors
integrate with the Arduino via an I2C interface, enabling real-time pH monitoring.
Humidity and temperature within the bubbler environment are measured using the
DHT22 sensor, tagged as RHT-B1. It operates with a humidity range of 0% to 100%
RH and a temperature range of -40°C to +80°C, with an accuracy of ±2% RH and
±0.5°C, respectively. This sensor ensures accurate hydrogen and oxygen production
calculations by monitoring ambient humidity in the vapor space of the bubbler. The
DHT22 integrates with the Arduino via a single-wire digital interface for real-time
monitoring.
Hydrogen leak detection is handled by the Figaro TGS821 sensor, tagged as HT-B2,
with a detection range of 500-10,000 ppm and an accuracy of ±30% at 1,000 ppm H₂.
This sensor continuously monitors hydrogen levels in the air to detect potential leaks,

ensuring a hydrogen capture rate of greater than 99%. Integrated via an analog input
pin, its voltage output is continuously monitored to detect even minor hydrogen leaks,
triggering alerts if hydrogen levels exceed safe thresholds.
Oxygen concentration in the bubbler is measured using the Figaro KE-25 sensor,
tagged as OT-B3. It operates within a range of 0% to 100% O₂, with an accuracy of
±1%. This sensor helps determine the remaining hydrogen concentration by
measuring the oxygen levels in the bubbler. Integrated via an analog input pin, it
provides real-time oxygen concentration data, ensuring accurate gas separation
efficiency calculations.
To further enhance measurement accuracy, additional verification instruments are
employed. AC current measurements are validated using standalone clamp meters
such as the AstroAI CM2KOR AC Clamp Meter and the Hantek CC-65 AC/DC Current
Clamp. These tools cross-check current measurements captured by Arduino sensors,
ensuring precise power calculations. Gas flow measurement is manually verified using
the FG-E5 Welding Gas Acrylic Flowmeter, which provides an independent reference
for hydrogen and oxygen flow rates.
Power supply analog gauges, mounted on the Resonant Tank Circuit, provide real-time
voltage and current readings for operational monitoring. These analog gauges serve
as a quick, manual reference alongside digital measurements captured by the Arduino
system. By integrating these various sensors and verification instruments, the
hydrogen production system maintains high accuracy, ensuring reliable efficiency
calculations and safe system operation.
By integrating these measurement, validation, and monitoring methods, the hydrogen
production system achieves a highly accurate and reproducible efficiency evaluation,
providing a comprehensive framework for sustainable and scalable hydrogen
generation.
Referring now to FIG. 12, a flow diagram illustrating a method of operating the
hydrolysis system is shown, according to an example embodiment. The method
comprises a sequence of coordinated electrical, fluidic, and control steps for
generating hydrogen gas using a resonant power supply and a capacitive electrolysis
cell stack. FIG. 12 illustrates a method of operating the hydrolysis system including
mechanical preparation, electrical energization, active tuning of resonant parameters,
controlled plasma-assisted water dissociation, and membrane-based hydrogen
purification and delivery.
At step 1205, an electrolyzer stack is provided. Providing the electrolyzer stack
includes mechanically assembling a plurality of coaxial electrode pairs within a
pressure vessel, electrically connecting the inner electrodes and outer electrodes in

parallel via corresponding terminals, sealing the assembly with first and second end
caps, and fluidly coupling the stack to a purified water supply and downstream gas
handling components. Providing the stack may further include filling the uniform radial
microgaps with purified dielectric fluid and verifying electrical isolation between
terminals prior to energization.
At step 1210, a resonant circuit is operated to generate a high-voltage supply.
Operating the resonant circuit includes rectifying an alternating current input to
produce a bulk direct current bus, switching a full-bridge inverter to generate a highfrequency waveform, driving a resonant tank comprising inductive and capacitive
elements, transferring energy through a transformer to provide galvanic isolation and
voltage transformation, and rectifying and filtering the secondary waveform to establish
a controlled high-voltage direct current bus suitable for energizing the electrolyzer
stack.
At step 1215, circuit parameters are monitored. Monitoring includes sensing highvoltage bus magnitude, electrolyzer stack voltage, stack current, switching frequency,
and phase relationships within the resonant converter. Monitoring may further include
measuring dielectric fluid conductivity, temperature of the dielectric fluid, temperature
of power electronics components, internal vessel pressure, and outlet gas pressure.
These measurements may be continuously sampled and digitized by a controller to
determine whether operating conditions remain within predefined thresholds.
At step 1220, inductance and/or capacitance parameters of the resonant circuit are
adjusted. Adjusting inductance and/or capacitance parameters includes modifying the
effective operating point of the resonant tank by varying switching frequency relative to
the tank’s resonant frequency, selecting different tap positions or transformer
configurations, or incorporating adjustable reactive components. Such adjustment
alters the effective impedance seen by the power stage and controls the magnitude
and stability of energy transfer to the electrolyzer stack.
At step 1225, operating frequency is adjusted. Adjusting the operating frequency
includes modifying the switching frequency of the full-bridge inverter and/or the
modulation frequency of the half-bridge pulse driver connected to the electrolyzer load.
This may include varying carrier frequency, duty cycle, burst repetition rate, or pulse
polarity. Frequency adjustment enables control over electric field rise time, energy per
pulse, repetition rate of dielectric breakdown events, and overall power delivered to the
microgaps.
At step 1230, hydrogen is generated at the electrodes. Generating hydrogen at the
electrodes includes applying high-voltage pulsed signals across the solid cylindrical
inner electrode and the hollow cylindrical outer electrode of each coaxial pair,
establishing an electric field within the uniform radial microgap that exceeds the

dielectric strength of the purified dielectric fluid, initiating localized dielectric
breakdown, forming a non-thermal plasma channel, and dissociating water molecules
into hydrogen and oxygen species within the microgap region.
At step 1235, hydrogen gas is generated from the electrolyzer stack. Hydrogen formed
within the microgaps coalesces into gas bubbles or entrained gas regions within the
flowing dielectric fluid and is transported out of the pressure vessel through an outlet
manifold. This step includes collecting the gas-liquid mixture and directing it toward
downstream separation components.
At step 1240, hydrogen gas is further generated or processed from the electrolyzer
stack. Processing includes delivering the gas-containing stream to a proton exchange
membrane hydrogen pump configured to selectively transport protons across a
membrane, thereby recombining protons to form molecular hydrogen on a designated
side of the membrane. This step may also include compressing the hydrogen to an
elevated pressure as part of the membrane-based pumping process.
At step 1245, gas is passed through a membrane. Passing gas through the membrane
includes selectively permeating hydrogen ions through a proton exchange membrane
while restricting oxygen and other species, thereby producing a purified hydrogen
product stream and a secondary stream enriched in oxygen and water vapor. The
hydrogen stream may be collected, dried, stored, or delivered to an external
application, while the oxygen-rich stream may be vented, captured, or further
processed.
Referring now to FIG. 13, an electrical one-line diagram of a power delivery and
control architecture for a hydrolysis system is illustrated, according to an example
embodiment, the diagram depicting electrical energy flow from a utility service 1302
through intermediate conversion stages to a plurality of high-voltage pulsed
electrolysis units. The incoming utility service provides alternating current power at
approximately 480 volts, three-phase, the service being a 480VAC, three-phase, fourwire, 60 Hz, solidly grounded wye system with an available fault current on the order of
65 kAIC, which is delivered through a main circuit breaker 1304, the main circuit
breaker comprising a low-voltage power breaker rated at approximately 800 ampere
frame with LSIG protection and approximately 65 kAIC interrupting capacity. The main
circuit breaker is configured to provide overcurrent protection and isolation for
downstream electrical components, and is further associated with a metering device
1306 configured to monitor electrical parameters including kilowatts, kilovolt-amperes
reactive, power factor, and total harmonic distortion.
The utility service is electrically connected to a motor control center 1308, the motor
control center being a 480V, approximately 65 kAIC rated, solidly grounded wye
distribution assembly, which distributes power to auxiliary loads including, in one

embodiment, water treatment subsystems, cooling systems, and compressed air
systems, such auxiliary loads including, by way of example, reverse osmosis
equipment on the order of approximately 15 kW, cooling loads on the order of
approximately 10 kW, and air systems on the order of approximately 5 kW, with
additional spare capacity provided. These auxiliary loads, which may collectively
represent on the order of approximately 50 kW including HVAC and UPS loads,
support operation of the hydrolysis system but are electrically distinct from the primary
high-voltage electrolysis power path.
A handoff interface 1310 is provided between a first electrical scope and a second
electrical scope, the handoff interface comprising a feeder connection configured to
transfer power from the motor control center to downstream high-voltage distribution
equipment, the feeder being defined in accordance with applicable electrical code
requirements and installed via cable and conduit. A feeder breaker 1312, including a
molded case circuit breaker rated at approximately 800 ampere frame with LSIG
protection and approximately 65 kAIC interrupting capacity, is positioned downstream
of the handoff and is configured to provide additional protection and switching
capability for the 480VAC feeder.
Electrical power is supplied to a step-up transformer 1314, the transformer being
configured to convert the incoming medium-voltage alternating current to a higher
voltage level, for example from approximately 480 volts delta to approximately 5
kilovolts wye, the transformer having a rating on the order of approximately 750 kVA
with an impedance on the order of approximately 5.75 percent. The transformer
provides galvanic isolation and establishes a high-voltage distribution bus, and in one
embodiment is sized relative to an electrical load on the order of approximately 576
kW for electrolysis stacks plus approximately 50 kW auxiliary load, corresponding to
approximately 626 kW total and approximately 638 kVA at a power factor of
approximately 0.95, thereby operating at approximately 85 percent of transformer
capacity.
A high-voltage switchgear assembly 1316 is electrically connected to the secondary
side of the transformer, the switchgear assembly comprising a 5 kV class switchgear
with a bus rating on the order of approximately 600 amperes and approximately 25
kAIC interrupting capacity. The switchgear assembly is configured to distribute power
across a high-voltage bus identified as a 5 kV bus having approximately 600 kW
connected load, provide fault detection, and implement protective functions including
high-resistance grounding (HRG), wherein a first ground fault condition produces an
alarm and a second ground fault condition produces a trip, with ground fault current
limited to less than approximately 10 amperes.

The high-voltage bus distributes electrical power to a plurality of feeder breakers,
including breakers 1318 and 1320, each having ratings on the order of approximately
125 amperes and approximately 5 kAIC interrupting capacity, with additional reserved
breaker space 1322 for future expansion. Each feeder breaker 1318 and 1320 is
electrically connected to a respective power electronics enclosure identified as
enclosure 1324 and enclosure 1326, each enclosure being configured as a NEMA 4X
enclosure and housing a plurality of power electronics units.
The power electronics enclosures house rectification and conversion components
configured to convert the high-voltage alternating current into a high-voltage direct
current bus. In one embodiment, each enclosure includes a rectifier stage 1328 or
1330 configured to convert approximately 5 kV AC to approximately 7 kV DC at a
power level on the order of approximately 288 kW. The resulting DC bus, identified as
a DC bus of approximately 7 kVDC, includes associated DC protection comprising
fast-acting fuses and/or DC circuit breakers configured to interrupt fault currents.
Within each power electronics enclosure, a DC link is established, the DC link
comprising energy storage and filtering elements configured to stabilize the highvoltage DC bus. Downstream of the DC link, a plurality of power electronic modules
are provided, each module 1332 and 1334 being identified, for example, as PE-01
through PE-06 in a first enclosure and PE-07 through PE-12 in a second enclosure,
each module having a power rating on the order of approximately 48 kW and
comprising switching devices, such as silicon carbide MOSFETs, arranged to form
high-frequency switching circuits operating at frequencies less than approximately 100
kHz. In one embodiment, each module implements an LLC resonant converter
topology and a pulsed output stage, configured to generate high-frequency, highvoltage pulsed DC signals. Each power electronic module is electrically coupled to a
corresponding electrolysis stack, indicated as stacks E101 through E112, the
electrolysis stacks comprising the plurality of coaxial capacitive electrode assemblies
described elsewhere in the specification. The power electronic modules are configured
to deliver pulsed electrical energy to the electrolysis stacks, with controllable
parameters including pulse frequency, duty cycle, and amplitude.
A DC protection subsystem is associated with each DC bus and is configured to
provide fault interruption and protection using devices such as fast-acting fuses or DC
circuit breakers. These protection elements are positioned to interrupt fault currents
and protect both the power electronics and the electrolysis stacks. In some
embodiments, feeder conductors and associated equipment are sized in accordance
with applicable electrical codes with appropriate derating.
A control power subsystem is provided to supply low-voltage power to control and
instrumentation components. The control power subsystem includes an uninterruptible

power supply 1336 having a capacity on the order of approximately 10 kVA and
configured as an online double-conversion system with ride-through capability on the
order of approximately 30 minutes, as well as regulated distribution panels 1338 and
1340. Control components include a programmable logic controller 1342, a humanmachine interface (HMI), network switches, and gas detection systems, and may
further include a safety architecture implementing either PLC-based emergency
shutdown or dedicated safety systems.
A control network 1344 interconnects the control components using industrial
communication protocols, including Ethernet/IP over an operational technology virtual
local area network and OPC-UA communication to a supervisory control system 1346,
with network infrastructure including managed switches 1348, enclosure switches
1350, and firewall interfaces 1352 between operational technology and information
technology domains. These components are configured to monitor system parameters,
execute control algorithms, and provide operator interaction.
Grounding arrangements are provided to ensure safe operation, including solid
grounding for lower-voltage systems and high-resistance grounding for the highvoltage system, with equipment grounding implemented as isolated ground
configurations and cable shielding grounded at a single point. The grounding
configuration is selected to limit fault currents, for example to less than approximately
10 amperes in the high-voltage system, and to enable detection of ground faults.
Additional engineering considerations may include protection coordination studies, arc
flash analysis including zone selective interlocking, harmonic studies in accordance
with IEEE 519, and grounding and electromagnetic compatibility planning.
In operation, electrical power is received from the utility service, stepped up to a higher
voltage, distributed through switchgear, converted to high-voltage direct current, and
processed by high-frequency power electronic modules to produce pulsed electrical
signals. These signals are applied to electrolysis stacks, thereby driving the hydrolysis
process. FIG. 13 therefore illustrates an integrated electrical architecture that enables
controlled delivery of high-voltage pulsed power to a plurality of electrolysis units, while
incorporating protection, control, auxiliary systems, defined electrical ratings, and
expansion provisions necessary for safe and efficient operation.
Referring now to FIG. 14, a piping and instrumentation diagram of a hydrolysis system
is illustrated, according to an example embodiment, the diagram depicting a fluid
handling architecture configured to supply a dielectric fluid to an electrolyzer module,
separate generated gases, and recirculate liquid within a controlled process loop, the
illustrated system being bounded within a skid boundary defining a modular assembly.
As shown, a deionized water feed is introduced from a supply header 1402 identified
as a deionized water header. The deionized water defining the dielectric fluid and

being supplied at a pressure on the order of approximately 30 psig and ambient
temperature conditions. The dielectric fluid is characterized by a total dissolved solids
concentration below approximately 1 part per million, such that the dielectric fluid
exhibits low bulk conductivity and minimal ionic content. The feed stream is conveyed
through a series of isolation valves, including valves 1404 and 1406, and through flow
instrumentation including a flow transmitter 1408 configured to measure volumetric
flow rate. A control valve, for example a flow control valve 1410 associated with an
actuator or signal input 1412, is provided in the feed path, and an emergency
shutdown valve 1414 is positioned in the feed line and is configured to isolate the
electrolyzer module in response to a fault condition or control signal, including loss of
power or detection of hazardous gas conditions.
The dielectric fluid is delivered to an electrolyzer module 1416, which is the disclosed
electrolyzer module having a capacity on the order of approximately 50 kW and
approximately 1.25 kg/day hydrogen production, which in one example embodiment
comprises a plurality of coaxial electrode pairs arranged within an enclosed housing.
The electrolyzer module is configured to receive electrical energy from a power supply,
for example 480VAC three-phase input, and apply a pulsed electric field across the
dielectric fluid disposed within uniform radial microgaps defined between inner and
outer electrodes. Instrumentation associated with the electrolyzer module includes a
temperature switch 1418 configured to provide alarm and trip functionality, as well as
internal measurements of stack voltage, current, and temperature communicated to a
programmable logic controller via package instrumentation. A nitrogen supply line 1420
may be provided to introduce nitrogen from a header for startup or shutdown inerting,
with associated valve 1422.
The electrolyzer module thereby generates a two-phase mixture comprising hydrogen
gas, oxygen gas, and water vapor, which exits the module through separate outlet
lines. A hydrogen gas-liquid separator 1424 is positioned downstream of the
electrolyzer module and is fluidly connected to receive a hydrogen-rich portion of the
two-phase mixture. Within the hydrogen gas-liquid separator, liquid droplets coalesce
and settle, while hydrogen gas accumulates in an upper region of the vessel, the
separator being rated, for example, at approximately 50 psig maximum allowable
working pressure and having a volume on the order of approximately 2 gallons. The
separated hydrogen gas is conveyed through an outlet line including a check valve
1426 and isolation valve 1428 to a hydrogen collection header identified as 1430, for
example at approximately 1/15 psig and less than approximately 104°F. The hydrogen
line includes a pressure gauge 1432 configured to measure internal pressure, a
pressure safety valve 1434, for example, at approximately 50 psig and configured to
vent gas to a safe location, and a plurality of analyzers and transmitters including
analyzers 1436 configured to measure hydrogen purity, oxygen content (O2 CR),
moisture or dew point (dew point analyzer), and other gas composition parameters, as

well as a pressure transmitter 1438. A flame arrestor or detonation protection device
may also be included in the hydrogen line. A pressure switch high-high device 1440 is
associated with the hydrogen-side process line, configured to detect when system
pressure exceeds a defined upper safety threshold. Upon reaching the high-high
setpoint, pressure switch high-high device 1440 is operatively coupled to initiate a
protective response, such as triggering an alarm and/or causing an automatic
shutdown of the electrolyzer module, including actuation of the emergency shutdown
valve 1414 to prevent overpressure conditions and ensure safe operation.
An oxygen gas-liquid separator 1442 is fluidly connected to receive an oxygen-rich
portion of the two-phase mixture, the separator being rated, for example, at
approximately 10 psig maximum allowable working pressure and having a volume on
the order of approximately 2 gallons. The oxygen gas-liquid separator operates
similarly, allowing liquid to separate from oxygen gas. The oxygen gas is conveyed
through an outlet line including a check valve 1444 and a pressure safety valve 1446,
for example, at approximately 10 psig, to a vent or collection system identified as
1448, which may vent to a safe location or be connected to a future oxygen collection
system. Condensate collected in the separators is directed downward through drain
lines, for example via gravity, and may include level gauges 1450 and 1452 for
monitoring liquid levels within the separators. A condensate return pathway is provided
between the gas-liquid separators and a water balance tank 1454. The water balance
tank is a vessel configured to maintain a controlled inventory of the dielectric fluid
within the system and includes instrumentation such as a level transmitter 1456, a
temperature transmitter 1458, and an analyzer 1460 configured to measure water
quality parameters such as dissolved oxygen. High-level and low-level switches 1462
and 1464 are provided to detect threshold conditions and initiate control actions. The
tank further includes isolation valves such as 1466 and 1468 and may include drain
connections.
A recirculation pump identified as 1470 is fluidly coupled to the water balance tank and
is configured to circulate the dielectric fluid through the system, the pump being
configured as a recirculation pump capable of maintaining continuous flow. The pump
draws fluid from the water balance tank and delivers the fluid through a recycle line
including check valves 1472 and 1474 and isolation valves 1476 and 1478 back
toward the electrolyzer module or to a recycle header identified as 1480, for example
at approximately 1/2 psig and ambient temperature. The recycle line further includes
analyzers 1482 and 1484 configured to measure parameters such as electrical
conductivity (EC) and pH. In one example embodiment, a drain line 1486 is provided to
remove excess fluid from the system, the drain line including a flow transmitter 1488
and a valve configured to selectively discharge fluid to a drain location. Additionally,
nitrogen purging may be used during startup and shutdown operations, and certain

valves, including emergency shutdown valve 1414, may be configured to fail closed
upon loss of power or signal.
The diagram further illustrates a plurality of instrumentation elements distributed
throughout the system, including pressure transmitters, temperature transmitters, flow
transmitters, and gas analyzers operatively connected to a control system. Instrument
signals may be transmitted as electronic signals, for example 4–20 mA signals, to a
master programmable logic controller. Safety components include pressure safety
valves configured to relieve overpressure conditions, check valves configured to
prevent reverse flow, and emergency shutdown valves configured to isolate sections of
the system. All piping may be constructed of materials such as stainless steel, for
example ASTM A316, and all hand valves may be ball valves unless otherwise
specified. The piping network interconnecting the components comprises conduits,
fittings, and junctions configured to convey fluid between the various elements, with
line sizes, for example 1/2-inch A316 piping, indicated throughout the diagram. Arrows
within the diagram indicate the direction of flow. Dashed boundary lines define the skid
boundary, indicating components contained within a modular assembly.
In operation, the dielectric fluid is supplied to the electrolyzer module, where electrical
excitation produces hydrogen and oxygen gases within the fluid. The resulting twophase mixture is separated into hydrogen-rich and oxygen-rich streams, with gases
conveyed to respective outlets and liquids returned via condensate pathways to the
water balance tank. The recirculation pump maintains continuous flow of the dielectric
fluid, while instrumentation and control systems ensure safe and efficient operation,
including gas detection, emergency shutdown functionality, and pressure relief. FIG.
14 therefore illustrates a complete fluid handling and gas management subsystem that
supports operation of the hydrolysis system, including dielectric fluid delivery, gas
separation, fluid recirculation, defined instrumentation, and integrated monitoring and
safety functions.
Although the subject matter has been described in language specific to structural
features and/or methodological acts, it is to be understood that the subject matter
defined in the appended claims is not necessarily limited to the specific features or
acts described above. Rather, the specific features and acts described above are
disclosed as example forms of implementing the claims.

connected to the inner electrodes, and the second end cap including a second terminal
electrically connected to the outer electrodes;

a high-frequency rectifier;
a filter capacitor; and

connection.

from the full bridge inverter to the high-frequency rectifier.

The hydrolysis system of claim 1, wherein the half-bridge pulse driver is configured to

time shorter than a dielectric relaxation time of the dielectric fluid.

disposed between the half-bridge pulse driver and the coaxial electrode pairs;
wherein the series impedance element comprises an inductive or resistive component
having a quality factor between 0.5 and 5 at a carrier frequency of the pulsed DC
waveform.
The hydrolysis system of claim 1, wherein the fluid connection is configured to convey
a two-phase mixture comprising hydrogen gas, oxygen gas, and water vapor.
The hydrolysis system of claim 9, wherein the proton exchange membrane hydrogen
pump is configured to separate the hydrogen gas from the two-phase mixture and
deliver a purified hydrogen product stream at elevated pressure.
The hydrolysis system of claim 10, wherein the proton exchange membrane hydrogen
pump further delivers an oxygen-rich byproduct stream from an anode-side outlet.

comprising:
a first end cap;

electrically connectivity with a half-bridge pulse driver that applies a pulsed voltage
having a peak amplitude greater than 1 kilovolt across the solid cylindrical inner
electrode and the hollow cylindrical outer electrode causing a localized dielectric
breakdown of the dielectric fluid to occur within the uniform radial microgap, resulting

in a formation of a non-thermal plasma channel and a reduction in effective resistance
across each of the plurality of electrode pairs from greater than 1 megohm to an
effective range between 1 kilohm and 10 kilohms.

Abstract
A hydrolysis system is disclosed comprising a plurality of coaxial electrode pairs, each
including a hollow cylindrical outer electrode and a solid cylindrical inner electrode
separated by an insulating annular spacer that defines a uniform radial microgap filled
with a dielectric fluid. The system includes end caps with electrical terminals for
connection to the electrodes, and a power supply featuring a full bridge inverter, an
LLC resonant tank, a transformer, a high-frequency rectifier, and a half-bridge pulse
driver. A series impedance element is coupled between the pulse driver and the
electrodes to shape high-voltage pulsed waveforms. The system further includes a
fluid connection conveying a two-phase mixture and a proton exchange membrane
hydrogen pump configured to separate hydrogen gas and deliver it at elevated
pressure. The electrode configuration enables controlled dielectric breakdown and
non-thermal plasma formation within the fluid, resulting in efficient hydrogen
generation without the need for added electrolytes.

=== public/docs/legal/regulatory/01_LIFECYCLE_GHG_ANALYSIS.pdf ===
COMPLETE LIFECYCLE GHG EMISSIONS ANALYSIS
FOR TOBE ENERGY HYDROGEN SYSTEM
Following DOE 45VH2-GREET Methodology (Rev. December 2025)
Target Year: 2026 | Location: Oklahoma (Plains NEEDS Region)

1. ELECTRICITY FOR ELECTROLYSIS
Stack electricity consumption: 42 kWh/kg H₂ (HHV basis)
Electricity source: 100% renewable PPA (wind, Oklahoma)
Critical Assumption & Emission Factor Selection
• The GREET 2025 extract shows wind as "~0 gCO₂eq/kWh" for operational
emissions only.
• Per 45VH2-GREET methodology (Section 3.2.1), renewable electricity must
include full lifecycle emissions (manufacturing, construction,
decommissioning).
• GREET 2025 default for onshore wind (2026): 14.0 gCO₂eq/kWh (conservative
value; GREET v2020 = 13.1, but 2025 update accounts for supply chain
emissions. Source: GREET 2025 Workbook, "Electricity Generation" tab, "Wind Onshore" pathway).
• Why conservative? Industry data shows 11–14 gCO₂eq/kWh for 2026 wind
(NREL 2023). Using 14.0 gCO₂eq/kWh aligns with DOE's "overestimate where
uncertain" directive.
Calculation
$$ \text{Emissions} = 42 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2} \times 14.0 \frac{\text{gCO}
_2\text{eq}}{\text{kWh}} \times \frac{1 \text{kg}}{1,000 \text{g}} = 0.588
\frac{\text{kgCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$

2. COMPRESSION ENERGY (1 bar → 200 bar)
System: Reciprocating multi-stage compressor with intercooling (200 bar output)
Compression Work Calculation (First Principles)
• Pressure ratio: $ \frac{P_2}{P_1} = \frac{200 \text{bar}}{1 \text{bar}} = 200 $

• Optimal staging: For reciprocating compressors with intercooling, use
polytropic compression ($n = 1.2$) and 3 stages (industry standard for 200 bar).
• Stage pressure ratio: $ r = 200^{1/3} \approx 5.85 $
• Work per stage (polytropic):
$$ W_{\text{stage}} = \left( \frac{n}{n-1} \right) \times \frac{R \times T_{\text{in}}}
{\text{MW}{\text{H}_2}} \times \left[ r^{\frac{n-1}{n}} - 1 \right] \times \frac{1}
{\eta{\text{comp}}} $$
Where:
• $ R = 8.314 \frac{\text{kJ}}{\text{kmol} \cdot \text{K}} $, $ \text{MW}{\text{H}_2} =
2.016 \frac{\text{kg}}{\text{kmol}} $ → $ \frac{R}{\text{MW}{\text{H}_2}} = 4.124
\frac{\text{kJ}}{\text{kg} \cdot \text{K}} $
• $ T_{\text{in}} = 298 \text{K} $ (standard inlet temp)
• $ \eta_{\text{comp}} = 0.85 $ (conservative compressor efficiency, NREL 2022)
• $ r^{\frac{n-1}{n}} = 5.85^{\frac{0.2}{1.2}} = 5.85^{0.1667} \approx 1.38 $
$$ W_{\text{stage}} = \left( \frac{1.2}{0.2} \right) \times 4.124 \times 298 \times
\left[ 1.38 - 1 \right] \times \frac{1}{0.85} = 6 \times 1,229 \times 0.38 \times 1.176
= 3,270 \frac{\text{kJ}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$
• Total work (3 stages):
$$ W_{\text{total}} = 3 \times 3,270 \frac{\text{kJ}}{\text{kg H}_2} = 9,810
\frac{\text{kJ}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$
• Convert to kWh/kg H₂:
$$ 9,810 \frac{\text{kJ}}{\text{kg H}_2} \times \frac{1 \text{kWh}}{3,600 \text{kJ}}
= 2.725 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$
• Add motor & electrical losses (conservative):
• Motor efficiency: 95% → $ \frac{2.725}{0.95} = 2.868 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg
H}_2} $
• Electrical losses (transformers, wiring): +5% → $ 2.868 \times 1.05 =
\boxed{3.01 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2}} $
Validation: NREL 2022 reports 2.5–3.5 kWh/kg for 200-bar compression. 3.01
kWh/kg is conservative.
Emissions Calculation
• Electricity source: 100% renewable PPA (same as electrolysis).
$$ \text{Emissions} = 3.01 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2} \times 14.0
\frac{\text{gCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kWh}} \times \frac{1 \text{kg}}{1,000 \text{g}} =
0.0421 \frac{\text{kgCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$

3. WATER TREATMENT (RO SYSTEM)
System: Small RO unit with 3–5 HP pump (use 5 HP for conservatism)

Energy Consumption Calculation
• Pump power: $ 5 \text{HP} \times 0.746 \frac{\text{kW}}{\text{HP}} = 3.73
\text{kW} $
• Water consumption: 10 L/kg H₂ (high-purity electrolysis, conservative end of
9–10 L/kg range)
• RO recovery rate: 75% (industry standard) → Feedwater required = $ \frac{10
\text{L}}{0.75} = 13.33 \text{L/kg H}_2 $
• Annual production: 900,000 kg H₂/year (nameplate)
• Annual feedwater: $ 900,000 \text{kg H}_2 \times 13.33 \frac{\text{L}}{\text{kg
H}_2} = 12,000,000 \text{L/year} $
• RO runtime: Assumes continuous operation with electrolyzer (8,760 hours/year
at 100% capacity factor).
• Annual RO energy: $ 3.73 \text{kW} \times 8,760 \text{h} = 32,675 \text{kWh/
year} $
• Energy per kg H₂:
$$ \frac{32,675 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{year}}}{900,000 \frac{\text{kg H}_2}
{\text{year}}} = 0.0363 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$
Emissions Calculation
• Electricity source: 100% renewable PPA.
$$ \text{Emissions} = 0.0363 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2} \times 14.0
\frac{\text{gCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kWh}} \times \frac{1 \text{kg}}{1,000 \text{g}} =
0.0005 \frac{\text{kgCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$

4. AUXILIARY LOADS
Components: Control systems (negligible), winter heating (marginal contribution)
Energy Consumption Calculation
• Winter heating: 2 kW average (conservative), Oklahoma heating season = 180
days → $ 180 \times 24 = 4,320 \text{h/year} $.
• Conservative adjustment: Assume 50% derating → $ 2 \text{kW} \times 2,160
\text{h} = 4,320 \text{kWh/year} $.
• Energy per kg H₂:
$$ \frac{4,320 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{year}}}{900,000 \frac{\text{kg H}_2}
{\text{year}}} = 0.0048 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$
• Control systems: 5 Arduino boards (total <0.1 kW) → negligible (<0.0001 kWh/
kg H₂).

Emissions Calculation
• Electricity source: 100% renewable PPA.
$$ \text{Emissions} = 0.0048 \frac{\text{kWh}}{\text{kg H}_2} \times 14.0
\frac{\text{gCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kWh}} \times \frac{1 \text{kg}}{1,000 \text{g}} =
0.0001 \frac{\text{kgCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$

5. WATER SUPPLY CHAIN EMISSIONS
Source: Municipal water supply (per GREET methodology)
Emission Factor & Calculation
• GREET 2025 default for municipal water: 110 gCO₂eq/m³ (conservative; Source:
GREET 2025 Workbook, "Water Supply" tab).
• Water consumption: 10 L/kg H₂ = 0.010 m³/kg H₂.
$$ \text{Emissions} = 0.010 \frac{\text{m}^3}{\text{kg H}_2} \times 110
\frac{\text{gCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{m}^3} \times \frac{1 \text{kg}}{1,000 \text{g}} =
0.0011 \frac{\text{kgCO}_2\text{eq}}{\text{kg H}_2} $$

• Threshold analysis:
• Maximum allowable for $3.00/kg tier: 0.45 kgCO₂eq/kg H₂.
• Tobe's emissions (0.6318) exceed this by 40.4%.
• Even with optimistic assumptions (0.562), emissions remain 24.9% above

CONCLUSION & RECOMMENDATIONS
1. Total lifecycle GHG emissions: 0.632 kgCO₂eq/kg H₂ (well-to-gate,
conservative estimate).
2. 45V qualification:
3. ❌ Does NOT qualify for $3.00/kg tier (emissions > 0.45 kgCO₂eq/kg H₂).
4. ✅ Qualifies for $1.00/kg tier (emissions < 1.5 kgCO₂eq/kg H₂).
5. Financial impact vs. preliminary analysis:
6. Preliminary analysis incorrectly assumed wind PPA = 0 gCO₂eq/kWh
(operational only).
7. Actual annual PTC revenue: $900,000/facility (vs. claimed $2.7M).
8. Revised 10-year net benefit (PPA @ $0.030/kWh):
◦ PPA cost: $1.13M/year
◦ PTC revenue: $0.9M/year
◦ Net loss: -$230,000/year (vs. claimed +$1.57M/year).
Critical Path Forward
• To qualify for $3.00/kg tier: Reduce emissions by 28.8% (to ≤0.45 kgCO₂eq/kg
H₂).
• Option 1: Use wind PPA with certified low-impact manufacturing (e.g., <10.7
gCO₂eq/kWh).
• Option 2: Add carbon-free compression (e.g., waste-heat recovery to cut
compression energy by 30%).
• Immediate action: Re-run GREET model with facility-specific PPA
documentation (e.g., RECs with embodied carbon data).
Final determination: Tobe Energy's system does not qualify for the $3.00/kg 45V
credit tier under current specifications and GREET 2025 methodology. Rigorous
accounting of renewable lifecycle emissions is non-negotiable for 45V compliance.

- All scenarios use 100% renewable PPA → Non-electricity emissions (RO water
treatment, manufacturing) = 0.01 kgCO₂e/kg H₂ (GREET baseline for electrolysis
pathways)

Calculation

= 13.45% → 56.5% (see note below)

Critical Notes:
1. $1/kg and $0.75/kg tiers require grid % > 100% under Plains grid mix
(impossible).
2. Correction: IRS rules allow blending with lower-emission sources (e.g., biogas,
nuclear) to meet thresholds. For pure grid electricity:
- $1/kg tier: Max grid % = (1.5 - 0.01) × 1,000 / (440.8 × 42) = 8.05% (not 33.3%)
- $0.75/kg tier: Max grid % = (2.5 - 0.01) × 1,000 / (440.8 × 42) = 13.45% (not 56.5%)
3. Why the discrepancy? The 33.3% and 56.5% values assume a hypothetical grid
with emissions matching the tier threshold. For Plains grid (440.8 gCO₂e/kWh), the
actual max grid % is 8.05% for $1/kg and 13.45% for $0.75/kg.

Key Insight:
- No U.S. grid region qualifies for any 45V tier with grid-only electricity.
- The cleanest grid (Northwest, 186.7 gCO₂e/kWh) still yields 7.85 kgCO₂e/kg H₂ (17×
above $3/kg threshold).
- User Defined is the only path to qualification (requires grid factor < 10.7 gCO₂e/kWh,
achievable only with 100% renewables/nuclear).

Key Insights for Investors
1. Single Biggest Lever for 45V Qualification
• Electricity carbon intensity dominates (>99% of emissions).
◦ Grid electricity at 440.8 gCO₂e/kWh → 18.51 kgCO₂e/kg H₂ (41× above
$3/kg threshold).
◦ Non-electricity factors (compression, stack efficiency) contribute < 0.1% of
total emissions under grid power.
• Action: Secure >97.5% renewable PPA (max 2.43% grid bleed) for $3/kg tier.
2. Robustness of $3/kg Qualification
• Extremely fragile under realistic conditions:
◦ 2.43% grid bleed is the cliff edge (e.g., 1 hour of grid outage per month at
100 MW plant = ~2.5% grid usage).
◦ Stack efficiency degradation (e.g., 42 → 45 kWh/kg) has zero impact
under 100% PPA but pushes grid-based projects further from thresholds.
• Investor Risk: PPA counterparty default or grid outage of >1% duration voids
$3/kg eligibility.
3. Cliff Edges for Each Tier

4. Strategic Recommendation
• Prioritize PPA structure over all else:
◦ Target >99% PPA coverage (e.g., 99% PPA + 1% grid = 0.185 kgCO₂e/kg
H₂, safely below 0.45 threshold).
◦ Avoid "grid-only" projects – even the cleanest U.S. grid (Northwest) is 17×
too dirty for $3/kg tier.
• Compression/stack efficiency are irrelevant for decarbonization (only impact
OPEX, not emissions).
Bottom Line: Tobe Energy can only qualify for $3/kg with near-100%
renewable PPA. Grid bleed tolerance is <2.5% – requiring ironclad PPA

contracts with penalties for curtailment. Regional grid differences are
negligible; the Plains grid is representative of most U.S. regions for 45V
analysis.

factors reflect 2024 NEEDS regional data.

TOBE ENERGY
COMPLIANCE MEMORANDUM: 45V PRODUCTION TAX CREDIT QUALIFICATION
Prepared for Investor Data Room | October 26, 2025

1. EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
Tobe Energy qualifies for the maximum $3.00/kg 45V Production Tax Credit
(PTC) under the Inflation Reduction Act (IRA), achieving a lifecycle greenhouse gas
(GHG) intensity of 0.03 kgCO₂e/kg H₂—93% below the $3.00/kg threshold of 0.45
kgCO₂e/kg H₂. This qualifies Tobe for Tier 1 (the highest credit tier) with a robust 0.42
kgCO₂e/kg margin of safety. The qualification is driven by:
- 100% renewable wind PPA in Oklahoma (emissions: ~0 gCO₂e/kWh),
- Industry-leading efficiency (42 kWh/kg H₂ vs. industry standard 50–55 kWh/kg for
PEM),
- Minimal auxiliary emissions (no cooling system, membrane-free design).
This PTC transforms Tobe’s unit economics, reducing hydrogen production costs from
$5.20/kg to $2.20/kg (net of credit).

2. 45V TAX CREDIT OVERVIEW
Per IRS Notice 2023-44 and Final 45V Rules (26 CFR §1.45V-1)
- IRA Section 45V provides a production tax credit for clean hydrogen produced after
2022.
- Credit Tiers (based on lifecycle GHG intensity):
- Tier 1 ($3.00/kg): ≤0.45 kgCO₂e/kg H₂
- Tier 2 ($2.00/kg): ≤1.00 kgCO₂e/kg H₂
- Tier 3 ($1.00/kg): ≤2.50 kgCO₂e/kg H₂
- 10-Year Window: Credits claimed annually for 10 years from facility commencement.
- Monetization:
- Transferability: 100% transferable to third parties (e.g., tax equity investors) at
closing.
- Direct Pay: Available for tax-exempt entities (e.g., municipalities) through 2032.
Investor Note: $3.00/kg is the highest achievable credit; no inflation adjustment
applies.

3. GREET METHODOLOGY COMPLIANCE
Per IRS-Approved 45VH2-GREET Model (Rev. December 2025)
- Model Requirement: All 45V applicants must use the DOE’s 45VH2-GREET tool for
GHG calculations.
- Tobe’s Pathway: Classified as Low-Temperature Electrolysis (LTE)—the only
applicable pathway for grid-powered electrolyzers.
- Scope: Well-to-Gate (WTG), as mandated by 45V rules. Excludes end-use (Well-toWheel).
- Boundary Includes:
- Electricity generation & transmission,
- Electrolyzer operation (including auxiliaries),
- Hydrogen compression, drying, and storage,
- Water treatment and supply.
Excludes manufacturing emissions (per IRS guidance), focusing solely on operational
lifecycle.

8. COMPETITIVE ADVANTAGE
Tobe’s technology delivers unmatched GHG performance vs. competitors:
- 42 kWh/kg vs. PEM’s 50–55 kWh/kg: 15–23% lower energy use → direct 15–23%

Tobe Energy’s membrane-free electrolyzer technology, powered by a 100% renewable

COST ANALYSIS
On-Site Hydrogen Production Advantage
The prevailing market price for hydrogen, particularly for small to mid-volume
consumers, significantly exceeds the production cost of the commodity itself.
This differential is largely attributable to the substantial overheads associated
with packaging, transportation, and last-mile delivery of cylindered hydrogen.
This document provides a quantitative analysis of these costs based on
publicly av

Version 2.0 · February 2026

Non-Confidential

T OBE ENERGY · C OST ANALYSIS

02

Date: May 3, 2025

1. OVERVIEW
The prevailing market price for hydrogen, particularly for small to mid-volume consumers, significantly
exceeds the production cost of the commodity itself. This differential is largely attributable to the
substantial overheads associated with packaging, transportation, and last-mile delivery of cylindered
hydrogen. This document provides a quantitative analysis of these costs based on publicly available
data, illustrating the market opportunity for Tobe Energy's on-site hydrogen generation systems.

2. COST ANALYSIS: DELIVERED HIGH-PRESSURE HYDROGEN CYLINDER
To quantify the effective cost of delivered hydrogen for small-volume users, Tobe Energy obtained a standard
commercial quote for a Size 300 hydrogen cylinder delivered to Tulsa, Oklahoma. 2.1. Cylinder Specifications and
Quoted Cost:
adjusted for real gas behavior at specified pressure and ambient temperature)
Airgas.com for Tulsa, OK delivery) 2.2. Effective Cost per Kilogram: Based on the quoted price and calculated
hydrogen mass:
• Supplier: Airgas (an Air Liquide company)
• Product: Standard Size 300 High-Pressure Hydrogen Cylinder
• Fill Pressure: 2,500 psi (approximately 172 bar)
• Internal Volume: ~49.5 liters
• Calculated Hydrogen Mass: Approximately 0.693 kg (derived using the Ideal Gas Law,
• Quoted Cost (Excluding Cylinder Rental/Lease): $84.05 (as of February 2026, via

The quoted cost of
$84.05 for approximately 0.693 kg of hydrogen results in an effective cost of $121.31 per kg. ($84.05 / 0.693 kg =
$121.31/kg)
This analysis indicates an effective delivered cost exceeding $120/kg for hydrogen supplied in standard highpressure cylinders. This figure represents the actual price point encountered by many commercial and industrial
entities for low-volume hydrogen procurement.

C A S E S T U DY

C A S E S T U DY

HYDROGEN FOR
HOSPITALS
Integration Case Study
Executive Summary: Large hospitals face increasing pressure to decarbonize
operations, enhance energy resilience, and manage costs effectively, all while
maintaining the highest standards of patient care. This document presents an
investor-focused use case for integrating on-site green hydrogen production
and utilization within a large hospital setting. Green hydrogen, produced via
renewable-power

Version 2.0 · February 2026

Non-Confidential

T OBE ENERGY · HYDROGEN FOR HOSPITALS

02

Executive Summary: Large hospitals face increasing pressure to decarbonize operations, enhance
energy resilience, and manage costs effectively, all while maintaining the highest standards of patient
care. This document presents an investor-focused use case for integrating on-site green hydrogen
production and utilization within a large hospital setting. Green hydrogen, produced via renewablepowered electrolysis, offers a unique, multifaceted solution by simultaneously generating medicalgrade oxygen, providing emissions-free backup power, enabling energy cost optimization through peak
shaving, supporting advanced medical research, and potentially offering novel therapeutic
applications. This integrated approach not only addresses environmental mandates (the hospital
sector contributes ~4.4% of global CO₂ emissions [1]) but also offers significant operational cost
savings, enhanced supply chain security for critical resources like oxygen, and improved overall
resilience against grid instability and commodity price volatility. For investors, this represents an
opportunity to fund a transformative infrastructure upgrade that yields tangible financial returns,
mitigates operational risks, and aligns with compelling ESG (Environmental, Social, and Governance)
objectives within the vital healthcare sector.

1. INTRODUCTION: THE IMPERATIVE FOR SUSTAINABLE AND RESILIENT
Healthcare The global healthcare sector, responsible for approximately 4.4% of net global carbon emissions [1], is
at a critical juncture. Hospitals are increasingly mandated to reduce their environmental footprint, with over
14,000 facilities worldwide pledging to halve emissions by 2030 [1]. Simultaneously, the absolute necessity for
uninterrupted operations and resource availability (power, medical gases) demands robust and resilient
infrastructure. Green hydrogen, produced through water electrolysis powered by renewable energy, presents a
compelling pathway to address these dual challenges. It allows hospitals to decarbonize significantly while
enhancing operational self-sufficiency and resilience [1]. This document outlines the strategic integration of green
hydrogen systems in large hospitals, detailing key applications, financial considerations, and the long-term value
proposition for stakeholders and investors.

2. ON-SITE MEDICAL-GRADE OXYGEN PRODUCTION VIA ELECTROLYSIS
A primary output of water electrolysis, alongside hydrogen, is high-purity oxygen [2]. By installing a hospital-scale
electrolyzer, a facility can generate its own medical-grade oxygen, significantly reducing or eliminating
dependence on external bulk liquid oxygen suppliers. This offers a critical
advantage in supply security, particularly highlighted during crises like the COVID-19 pandemic, where hospital
oxygen demand surged by approximately 50%, straining conventional supply chains [2]. The Viamed San José
hospital in Spain, for instance, utilizes a solar-powered electrolyzer to produce hydrogen for heating and captures
the co-produced oxygen for medical use, enhancing self-sufficiency and reducing CO₂ emissions by 20 tons
annually [2]. To meet stringent medical standards (e.g., European Pharmacopeia mandates ≥93% purity for onsite generated O₂ [3]), the oxygen undergoes filtration and continuous monitoring. Quality control and
certification, adhering to medical gas regulations and Good Manufacturing Practices (GMP), ensure its suitability.
On-site generation can reduce oxygen supply costs by an estimated 50-90% [4], as it circumvents the costs
associated with production, liquefaction, transportation, and cylinder rental of bulk oxygen. These savings directly
offset a portion of the electrolyzer's operational expenses, effectively lowering the net cost of hydrogen when the
oxygen byproduct is valorized [5]. For investors, the electrolyzer becomes a dual-revenue (or dual cost-avoidance)
asset, improving the overall return on investment while providing an invaluable, life-saving, uninterrupted oxygen
supply.

3. HYDROGEN FOR MRI SUPER-COOLING: A FUTURE-PROOFING
Strategy Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) machines utilize superconducting magnets requiring cryogenic
temperatures (~4 K, -269°C), traditionally achieved using liquid helium, a scarce, non-renewable, and increasingly
expensive resource [6]. Global helium shortages have already impacted hospital operations, causing downtime

and escalating costs [6]. Hydrogen is emerging as a viable alternative cryogenic coolant. Research demonstrates
its efficacy, particularly with newer high-temperature superconductors like MgB₂ [7]. Engineering studies are
exploring MRI magnet designs incorporating hydrogen cooling loops, signaling a shift away from helium [7]. While
direct hydrogen-cooled MRI systems are still developmental, they could utilize liquid hydrogen or closed-cycle
hydrogen gas refrigeration. This transition promises to drastically reduce or eliminate helium consumption,
yielding substantial operational cost savings and mitigating supply chain vulnerabilities. From an investment
perspective, early adoption of hydrogen- based cooling technologies, synergizing with on-site hydrogen
production, positions a hospital as a leader in operational efficiency and reliability, addressing the critical and
worsening issue of helium scarcity [6].

4. HYDROGEN IN HOSPITAL RESEARCH AND ADVANCED MEDICAL
Applications Large hospitals are often centers for research and specialized treatments, creating diverse
opportunities for hydrogen utilization.
traditionally use helium as a carrier gas. Due to helium scarcity and cost, laboratories are
• Laboratory Operations: Many analytical instruments, such as gas chromatographs,
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · HYDROGEN FOR HOSPITALS

03

increasingly transitioning to hydrogen, which can be supplied reliably and cost-effectively from an on-site source,
eliminating bottled gas expenses and improving safety by enabling low-pressure piped supply [6].
with antioxidant and anti-inflammatory properties. Over 2,000 scientific publications and numerous human trials
are exploring its application in conditions ranging from cardiovascular and neurodegenerative diseases to tissue
injury treatment [7]. A hospital with integrated hydrogen infrastructure would be uniquely positioned to
participate in, or lead, such cutting-edge clinical trials, attracting research funding and enhancing its reputation as
an innovation hub. For investors, these applications represent significant upside. They leverage existing hydrogen
infrastructure at a low marginal cost for potentially high returns in research capabilities, grant attraction, and
pioneering new treatments.
• Therapeutic Potential: Hydrogen gas itself is being investigated as a therapeutic agent

5. HYDROGEN FOR ZERO-EMISSION BACKUP POWER AND ENHANCED
Resilience Uninterruptible power is non-negotiable in hospitals. Traditional diesel generators, while common,
suffer from emissions, maintenance burdens, and potential unreliability, as evidenced during major outages like
Superstorm Sandy where many failed [8]. Green hydrogen, through fuel cells or hydrogen-fueled generators,
offers a clean, highly reliable alternative. Several U.S. hospitals already employ fuel cells for critical backup power,
demonstrating superior resilience and eliminating local air pollution [8]. A hydrogen-powered backup system
typically comprises on-site hydrogen storage and fuel cell units sized for essential loads. These systems can
provide continuous power (cogeneration) during normal operations and instantly ramp up during outages, offering
extended runtime limited only by stored hydrogen volume. Financially, while CapEx for fuel cells may be higher
than diesel generators, long-term operational savings accrue from eliminated diesel fuel costs, reduced
maintenance, and potential carbon pricing avoidance. Furthermore, fuel cell installations have been eligible for
incentives like Investment Tax Credits and Renewable Energy Credits [8], improving project economics. The U.S.
Department of Energy's $9.5 billion program for clean hydrogen hubs signals strong policy support [9]. By
ensuring operational continuity and mitigating risks associated with power failures, hydrogen backup systems
protect a hospital's core mission and financial stability [10].

6. PEAK SHAVING AND ENERGY COST OPTIMIZATION WITH HYDROGEN
Hospitals, as 24/7 high-energy consumers, incur substantial electricity costs, including significant peak demand
charges. An integrated hydrogen system enables peak shaving: producing and storing hydrogen during off-peak
hours (when electricity is cheaper or surplus on-site renewables are available) and using it to generate power via
fuel cells during high-cost peak periods. This
flattens the hospital's demand profile, reducing energy procurement at premium rates and lowering demand
charges [11]. This strategy, proven in other sectors [11], can yield significant annual savings. For example,
shifting even a fraction of peak load consumption (e.g., from $0.20/kWh peak to $0.10/kWh off- peak equivalent
via hydrogen) can accumulate to hundreds of thousands of dollars saved annually for a large facility. The
hospital's energy management system can automate this process, responding to real-time pricing or participating
in demand-response programs for additional revenue. The round-trip efficiency of power-to-hydrogen-to-power is
lower than batteries, but hydrogen offers superior scalability for long-duration storage and multi-use versatility,
making it an active energy optimization asset that can reduce a hospital's estimated $6 billion annual energy
expenditure in the U.S. [12].

7. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS AND FINANCIAL VIABILITY
Integrating green hydrogen involves significant CapEx for electrolyzers, storage, fuel cells, and auxiliary systems,
potentially ranging from $5-10 million for a 1 MW scale system. However, this can be mitigated through phased
deployment or innovative financing like Power Purchase Agreements (PPAs) or Energy-as-a-Service models,
shifting CapEx off the hospital's balance sheet [12, 8]. The OpEx, primarily electricity for electrolysis, is countered
by substantial savings and revenue offsets:

saving hundreds of thousands annually and effectively subsidizing hydrogen production [5].
electricity purchases.

response revenue.
cells [8]), renewable energy credits, and potential future monetization of carbon reductions.
compared to diesel engines and avoidance of outage-related financial and reputational losses. While current
green hydrogen costs (2-3 times fossil-derived [13]) may suggest longer payback periods (e.g., 7-10 years), costs
are projected to fall significantly. IRENA forecasts green hydrogen below $2/kg within the next decade, and
electrolyzer CapEx to drop by 40% short-term and 80% long-term [13]. This trajectory ensures improving project
economics over its lifespan. The "value
• Oxygen Cost Savings: Up to 50-90% reduction in oxygen supply costs [4], potentially
• Fuel Displacement: Elimination of diesel fuel costs for backup generators and reduced grid
• Energy Cost Optimization: Tangible savings from peak shaving and potential demand
• Incentives & Carbon Credits: Access to grants, tax credits (e.g., U.S. 30% ITC for fuel
• Reduced Maintenance & Enhanced Reliability: Lower maintenance for fuel cells

utilization and return on investment.

9. CONCLUSION: INVESTMENT OUTLOOK AND STRATEGIC LONG-TERM
Value The integration of green hydrogen in large hospitals transcends a mere sustainability initiative; it is a
strategic investment in future-proof infrastructure that delivers energy security, operational cost control,
enhanced resilience, and potential new service avenues. For investors, this presents a compelling opportunity

H2 TRANSPORT
Industry Use Cases
Abstract: Hydrogen is rapidly emerging as a pivotal energy carrier in the
global transition towards a zero- emission future. Its versatility allows for

decarbonization challenges where direct electrification faces limitations. This
document highlights real-world examples of hydrogen utilization, from fuel cell
electric v

Version 2.0 · February 2026

Non-Confidential

T OBE ENERGY · H2 TRANSPORT

02

Abstract: Hydrogen is rapidly emerging as a pivotal energy carrier in the global transition towards a
zero- emission future. Its versatility allows for diverse applications across transportation and industrial
sectors, addressing decarbonization challenges where direct electrification faces limitations. This
document highlights real-world examples of hydrogen utilization, from fuel cell electric vehicles
(FCEVs) in passenger cars, buses, and heavy-duty trucks, to innovative hydrogen combustion engines
in various platforms, and specialized applications in rail, maritime, and industrial logistics. These case
studies demonstrate hydrogen's growing maturity and its crucial role in complementing battery
technology to achieve comprehensive emissions reductions.

1. INTRODUCTION
The imperative to decarbonize the global economy has spurred significant innovation in clean energy
technologies. Among these, hydrogen stands out for its potential to replace fossil fuels in sectors where emissions
are traditionally hard to abate. Whether utilized in fuel cells to generate electricity or directly combusted in
modified engines, hydrogen offers a pathway to zero or near- zero emissions at the point of use. This document
presents a survey of tangible, real-world applications of hydrogen technology, illustrating its expanding footprint
in creating a sustainable energy landscape.

2. HYDROGEN FUEL CELL ELECTRIC VEHICLES (FCEVS): ROAD-BASED
Mobility Hydrogen fuel cells convert hydrogen gas into electricity to power electric motors, with water vapor as
the only byproduct. This technology is gaining traction in various road vehicle segments.
Major automotive manufacturers have introduced FCEVs, offering consumers zero-emission driving with ranges
and refueling times comparable to conventional vehicles. o Toyota Mirai: Launched in 2014 as the world's first
mass-produced FCEV, the Mirai sedan boasts a range of 400-500 km, with over 10,000 units sold globally [1]. o
Hyundai Nexo: This hydrogen-powered SUV, launched in 2018, offers similar performance and is available in key
markets like California, South Korea, and parts
• 2.1 Passenger Cars:

of Europe [1]. While global FCEV sales remain niche (approximately 14,451 units in 2023, down from 20,700 in
2022 [2]), growth is steady in regions with developing hydrogen refueling infrastructure.
Fuel cell buses are increasingly deployed in urban environments to decarbonize public transit and improve air
quality. o Europe: By early 2023, 370 hydrogen buses were operational, with plans for over 1,200 by 2025 [3].
Notable deployments include London's fleet of 20 hydrogen double-decker buses and significant rollouts in
Germany, France, and the Netherlands, often supported by EU initiatives like JIVE, which aims to deploy 300 buses
across 22 cities [3]. o Asia: China, Japan, and South Korea are leaders in fuel cell bus adoption, with cities like
Seoul actively integrating them into their public transport networks. These buses typically offer ranges of 300-400
km and rapid refueling (around 10 minutes).
Hydrogen fuel cells are particularly promising for heavy-duty trucking, where battery solutions often struggle with
range, payload capacity, and charging times. o Hyundai XCIENT: Hyundai has deployed 47 XCIENT fuel cell trucks
in Switzerland, accumulating over 4 million kilometers by 2022. These trucks offer a ~400 km range with ~35 kg
of hydrogen. Hyundai aims for 1,600 units in Switzerland by 2025 and is expanding into other European countries
and conducting trials in California with a 30-truck deployment [4]. o Toyota & Kenworth Partnership: Successful
trials of fuel cell semi-trucks at the Port of Los Angeles, demonstrating ranges over 300 miles, have spurred
further development of hydrogen powertrains for freight. o H2Accelerate Consortium: A collaboration between
European truck manufacturers (Daimler Truck, Volvo, IVECO) and energy companies (Shell, TotalEnergies, OMV)
aims to deploy 150 fuel-cell trucks and associated refueling infrastructure in Europe by the late 2020s. Daimler
Truck, for example, plans to deliver 5,000 hydrogen trucks by 2030, supported by Shell's refueling network
development [5].
• 2.2 Buses:

• 2.3 Heavy-Duty Trucks:

3. HYDROGEN COMBUSTION ENGINES: A NEW PATHWAY FOR EXISTING
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · H2 TRANSPORT

03

Technology Beyond fuel cells, direct combustion of hydrogen in modified internal combustion engines (ICEs) offers
another route to decarbonization, leveraging existing manufacturing expertise.
o Toyota's Hydrogen Engine Development: Toyota has actively tested a hydrogen- fueled 1.6L turbocharged
engine in a Corolla race car, achieving significant performance improvements and rapid refueling (1.5 minutes).
This research has led to a prototype Corolla Cross H2 road car. Toyota has also demonstrated a liquid hydrogenfueled race car, highlighting the fuel's versatility [6]. o Cummins Hydrogen ICE for Trucks: U.S. engine
manufacturer Cummins is developing large-displacement hydrogen engines for heavy vehicles, including a 15liter engine targeting production in 2027, designed to offer power and torque comparable to diesel counterparts
but with zero CO₂ emissions. Cummins is collaborating with Daimler (Freightliner) to integrate these engines into
popular truck models [7].
Hydrogen and hydrogen-derived fuels like ammonia are being explored for marine engines. o Hydroville Ferry
(Belgium): Launched in 2017, this passenger ferry utilized a modified diesel engine to burn hydrogen (with diesel
pilot fuel), serving as an early testbed for marine hydrogen combustion. o MAN Energy Solutions: This major
marine engine manufacturer is developing ammonia-fueled engines, with the first delivery planned for 2024 and
commercial rollout anticipated by 2027. Ammonia (NH₃) allows for easier storage of hydrogen energy for long-haul
shipping.
Modified jet engines can potentially burn hydrogen, eliminating CO₂ emissions. o Rolls-Royce & easyJet: In 2022,
Rolls-Royce successfully tested a modern aircraft turbine (AE2100) running on 100% green hydrogen. They are
now advancing to test a full-size Pearl 15 business jet engine with hydrogen at a NASA facility in 2024 [8]. o
Airbus ZEROe: Airbus is developing concept hydrogen-powered commercial aircraft (ZEROe), targeting entry into
service by 2035. These designs envision hydrogen use in either fuel cells for electric propulsion or direct
combustion in modified turbine engines. Hydrogen combustion offers a potentially faster transition for industries
reliant on ICEs, though challenges include managing NOx emissions (which can be addressed with aftertreatment) and on-board hydrogen storage.
• 3.1 Automotive Applications:
• 3.2 Maritime Applications:
• 3.3 Aviation Applications:

4. HYDROGEN IN SPECIALIZED INDUSTRIAL TRANSPORT
Hydrogen is proving effective in various industrial transport modes that are difficult to electrify solely with
batteries.
Fuel cell trains offer a zero-emission solution for non-electrified or difficult-to-electrify rail lines. o Alstom Coradia
iLint (Germany): Since 2018, Germany has operated these hydrogen passenger trains. In 2022, a rail line in Lower
Saxony became the world's first to be fully powered by 14 hydrogen trains, replacing diesel units and saving an
estimated 4,000 tons of CO₂ annually [9]. These trains have also been demonstrated in Austria, the Netherlands,
and France. Globally, the UK (HydroFLEX prototype) and China (hydrogen trams) are also advancing hydrogen rail
technology.
Hydrogen fuel cell forklifts represent a significant, albeit less publicized, success story. o Large-Scale Adoption:
Companies like Amazon and Walmart have deployed tens of thousands of hydrogen fuel cell forklifts. Amazon
utilized over 15,000 units by 2022, with plans for 5,000 more by 2025. Walmart operated over 9,500 units by
2023 [10]. The key advantages are continuous power delivery throughout a shift and rapid refueling (around 3
minutes), significantly boosting productivity in 24/7 warehouse operations compared to battery-powered
alternatives.
The maritime sector is actively experimenting with hydrogen for cleaner operations. o MF Hydra (Norway):
Launched in 2023, this is the world's first liquid hydrogen- powered ferry, utilizing Ballard fuel cell systems. It
operates in Norway, aligning with the country's goal for zero-emission fjord transport by 2026 [11]. o Sea Change
(USA): This 75-passenger catamaran began operations in the San Francisco Bay Area in 2024, using compressed
hydrogen and fuel cells. It is the first commercial hydrogen ferry in the U.S. [12]. o CMB's Hydroville and
HydroBingo: Following the Hydroville pilot, CMB launched HydroBingo in Japan, an 80-passenger ferry with dual

hydrogen-diesel engines, showcasing a hybrid approach. Future projects include hydrogen fuel cell cruise ships
and cargo vessels, with ammonia also being a key focus for decarbonizing large ocean-going ships.
• 4.1 Hydrogen Trains:
• 4.2 Hydrogen Forklifts and Warehouse Vehicles:
• 4.3 Maritime Transport - Ferries and Ships:

5. FUTURE EXPANSION: INVESTMENTS AND STRATEGIC INITIATIVES
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · H2 TRANSPORT

04

The transition to hydrogen in transport and industry is supported by significant investments and strategic
planning from corporations and governments.
o Honda: Launching a CR-V Fuel Cell SUV in 2024 with a plug-in battery feature, produced in Ohio. The new fuel
cell system, co-developed with GM, is also intended for heavy trucks and stationary power. o Toyota: Beyond the
Mirai, Toyota is developing fuel cell buses, pickup trucks (Hilux prototype), and actively partnering to build
refueling infrastructure globally. o Hyundai: Plans to introduce next-generation, lower-cost fuel cell systems by
2025 for diverse applications including cars, trucks, construction machinery, and ships, backed by a $6-7 billion
investment by 2030 [13].
Traditional energy companies like Shell, TotalEnergies, and BP are investing in hydrogen refueling station networks
and green hydrogen production facilities (e.g., Shell's Holland Hydrogen I). Amazon has secured agreements for
green hydrogen to power its logistics fleet.
Companies such as Nikola Motors and Hyzon Motors are developing and deploying hydrogen fuel cell trucks,
contributing to the expansion of hydrogen-powered commercial fleets and supporting infrastructure.
o European Union: The "EU Hydrogen Strategy" includes ambitious targets for FCEV deployment and over 1,000
hydrogen fueling stations by 2030, supported by funding programs like IPCEI. o Japan & South Korea: Aim for
millions of FCEVs by the 2030s, with substantial subsidies and infrastructure development plans. o China: Leads in
hydrogen bus and truck deployment, with around 10,000 FCEVs by 2022 and a target of 50,000 by 2025 [14].
China became the largest market for new hydrogen vehicles in 2023 [2].
Beyond current trials, aerospace companies like Boeing are studying hydrogen, and startups like ZeroAvia are
testing hydrogen fuel cells in smaller aircraft. In shipping, Maersk is investing in green methanol-fueled ships as a
transition towards hydrogen/ammonia, and major ports are developing hydrogen bunkering capabilities.
• 5.1 Automotive Commitments:
• 5.2 Energy Sector Involvement:
• 5.3 Emerging Players:
• 5.4 Government Support and Targets:
• 5.5 Advancements in Aviation and Shipping:

6. CONCLUSION
The real-world examples presented herein underscore hydrogen's growing significance as a versatile and effective
solution for decarbonizing diverse transportation and industrial sectors. From passenger vehicles and urban buses
to heavy-duty trucks, trains, maritime vessels, and industrial equipment, hydrogen technologies - both fuel cells
and combustion engines - are demonstrating practical viability and tangible emissions reductions. Supported by
substantial private and public investment, and driven by increasingly ambitious climate targets, these pioneering
applications are paving the way for hydrogen to play a critical role in achieving a global zero-emission future,
complementing electrification efforts and extending clean energy solutions to the most challenging applications.
