USE HYDROGEN TO CUT
COSTS BY OVER 60%
In this detailed use case, we'll present real cost data from Duke Energy's off-peak pricing and compare
it to traditional battery storage, demonstrating how Tobe's hydrogen solution can save customers
more than 60% on their energy costs. We'll break down the numbers and show the tangible economic
and environmental benefits of leveraging green hydrogen for AI data centers.
Solving AI's Energy Crisis: Over 60% Cheaper, Zero-Emission Power AI power consumption is set to skyrocket, but
so are the challenges of global warming. As demand for energy grows, the gold standard for powering data
centers increasingly points toward renewable, low-emission sources. At the forefront of the conversation are
nuclear, solar, and wind energy. While all of these power sources face challenges - particularly with the need for
energy storage - today we're focusing on what many consider the most scalable solution for large-scale renewable
power generation: nuclear energy. You might be wondering, what about hydrogen? While hydrogen plays a pivotal
role, it's important to clarify that hydrogen isn't "harvested" like wind, solar, nuclear, or fossil fuels. In many ways,
hydrogen functions more like a battery than a traditional fuel source - but don't worry, we'll explain why that's
crucial. The Nuclear Landscape: Nuclear energy holds a unique place in the energy mix. Unlike other energy
sources, nuclear power plants have little tolerance for turning up or down based on demand. Humans, however,
tend to use the most energy during "peak" hours - typically when they come home from work - and almost none
during "off-peak" times, like while they sleep. This mismatch creates challenges. But nuclear plants need to run
consistently, and that's where the concept of "peak" and "off-peak" pricing comes in.
For example, Duke Energy, one of the nation's largest nuclear operators, drops its energy prices by more than
60% during off-peak times. On-peak pricing can be around 27.64 cents per kWh, while off-peak pricing falls to
10.47 cents per kWh. This begs the question: Why doesn't everyone bank energy during off-peak times when it's
cheapest, and then use it during peak hours when it's more expensive? The answer is simple: Batteries are
incredibly expensive. Two of Tesla's car batteries, for instance, can cost up to $15,000 each or $30,000 total, for
the equivalent energy storage that our Tobe Electrolyzer can generate for less than $2,500. Our system produces
10 kg of hydrogen per day - enough to fill two standard gas tanks - at a fraction of the cost. Not only does the
price of batteries make large-scale energy storage impractical, but relying on batteries also means we would
quickly run out of rare earth minerals if every home and business required battery backups. The high costs and
limited resources make batteries an unsustainable solution to energy storage at scale. Why Hasn't This Been
Done With Hydrogen Before?
The Facts

What Does This Have to Do
with AI and Data Centers? The connection between AI, data centers, and hydrogen energy storage is simple yet
revolutionary. As AI data centers continue to demand more power, energy efficiency and cost savings become
crucial considerations. Here's where Tobe's hydrogen technology steps in: because our system is a fraction of the
cost of a traditional battery system and produces true zero-emission fuel, AI data centers can store low-cost, offpeak power in a form that's flexible, storable, and usable at any time. Duke Energy Peak // Off-Peak Pricing Case
Study Take Duke Energy's pricing model, for example - during off-peak hours, electricity costs over 60% less. Tobe
Electrolyzers can produce green hydrogen during these off-peak hours, storing the energy for future use at a
fraction of the cost compared to batteries. This not only lowers utility bills for AI data centers but also reduces
their carbon footprint. When hydrogen is used, the only by-product is water, unlike traditional fossil fuels that
contribute to harmful emissions. Moreover, integrating hydrogen into AI data centers' energy mix significantly
reduces stress on the grid by distributing energy use more evenly, helping prevent costly overloads and outages.
Tobe's solution isn't just about short-term savings - it's about future-proofing the energy needs of AI data centers,
both financially and environmentally. Here are three key benefits that make our approach stand out: Cost Savings:
AI data centers can capitalize on significantly lower energy prices by storing energy as green hydrogen during offpeak hours and using it when energy prices spike. Efficiency: Our Tobe Electrolyzers are 75% cheaper to build and
45% more efficient to operate than traditional electrolysis systems, offering the most affordable and efficient
green hydrogen solution available. Sustainability: The combination of nuclear power and green hydrogen creates
true zero-emission energy, offering AI data centers a cleaner, more sustainable way to meet their growing power
demands.

Mitigating the Challenges of the AI Revolution
As the world races to accommodate the exponential growth in AI power demands, the energy infrastructure
supporting this transformation must evolve. Hydrogen, traditionally thought of as a fuel source, can play a pivotal
role in addressing the challenges posed by fluctuating energy demands and increasing global carbon emissions.
The Tobe Electrolyzer offers a game-changing solution by making green hydrogen as efficient and cost-effective to
store energy as batteries - but without the price tag or environmental strain of mining rare earth materials. This
isn't just about reducing utility bills for AI data centers. It's about building a resilient, future- proof energy model
that supports the expansion of AI without compromising on sustainability. With Tobe Electrolyzers, we're not only
reducing costs but also reducing carbon emissions and grid stress, paving the way for a more sustainable,
scalable, and green AI revolution.
The future of energy is green hydrogen
and the future of green hydrogen is Tobe. Whether you're looking to reduce operational costs, minimize
environmental impact, or be at the cutting edge of AI energy infrastructure, our approach is uniquely positioned to
help AI data centers succeed both today and tomorrow. We are excited to continue revolutionizing energy storage
and to partner with those looking to lead in this new era of innovation. As the demand for AI infrastructure grows
and energy usage intensifies, integrating cost- effective, zero-emission hydrogen storage solutions becomes
increasingly vital. By capitalizing on off-peak nuclear energy pricing, Tobe's electrolyzer technology enables AI
data centers to dramatically reduce operational expenses - over 60% in some cases - while shrinking their carbon
footprint. This forward-looking approach eases grid strain, ensures long-term energy availability, and establishes a
more resilient, sustainable, and economically sound future for large-scale data operations.

=== public/docs/market/case-studies/OU_Case_Study_Final_Report.pdf ===
Key Insights and Final Recommendations for Tobe Energy
The Ronnie K. Irani Center for the Creation of Economic Wealth
Energy Fall 2025
Consulting Associates
Nelly Komuhendo, Kaivalya Meduri, Anna Nilsson, Anoushika Singh
Team Leader: Kendall Cousins

Table of Contents
Executive Summary
This semester, the Ronnie K. Irani Center for the Creation of Economic Wealth has
provided five consulting interns with the opportunity to work with Tobe, an Oklahomabased startup focused on making green hydrogen feasible and affordable. Tobe
manufactures electrolyzer machines in three sizes (25kg/day, 250kg/day, 2500kg/day),
which operate at a 94.7% efficiency, and produce hydrogen at ~$446 per kWh. Tobe’s
electrolyzer requires an input of water and electricity, and outputs H₂. Through the
team’s research, including extensive market analysis and 7 interviews with industry
experts and professionals, the Energy Team has analyzed Tobe’s market viability,
business model, and growth needs. The team examined the demand for hydrogen as
a service (HaaS) versus electrolyzer sales. The team determined who Tobe’s main
competitors in the hydrogen space could be, and how Tobe can position themselves
within the market to provide services that no competitor is currently offering.
Tobe’s ideal customer is hydrogen users that are 150+ miles away from their hydrogen
source because cost is the highest contributing factor for companies making hydrogen
decisions.
Location: Transportation costs are the highest cost factor outside of 150 miles. Tobe
should concentrate on securing companies that are 150+ miles from their hydrogen
source to stay cost competitive.
Green Commitments: Tobe should also pursue companies with green commitments
regardless of location because cost is not the priority.

Tobe’s $9.81B serviceable market and 8 diversified segments, making up ~11% of the
total market and over 10 million kg/year of hydrogen, provide a focused pathway to
scale market share.
The 8 segments are heavy-duty fueling, urban fleet fueling, light & medium-duty
commercial fueling, industrial decarbonization, chemical production & synthetic fuel,
backup power & microgrids, energy storage, and already green customers. These
segments are defined by qualities like hydrogen volume requirements and established
infrastructure to establish use cases.
Tobe will benefit from 2 business models, Hydrogen-as-a-Service (HaaS) and
Electrolyzer Sales, that can address segments based on industry factors.
Heavy-duty fueling, industrial decarbonization, chemical production & synthetic fuels:
these companies are more likely to buy electrolyzers directly because they use large
amount of hydrogen and have pre-existing infrastructure to support it.
Light & medium-duty commercial fueling, backup power & microgrids, & energy
storage/grid balancing: these companies would more likely take the HaaS model
because of low hydrogen needs, seasonal hydrogen needs, or low capital funding.
Urban fleet fueling & already green companies: These industries have variable factors
across companies that will lead to a mix of HaaS and electrolyzer sales.

Tobe could attain $657.7 million in profit by 2033 by instituting a 150% profit margin on
electrolyzers and a 30% profit margin on hydrogen.
Tobe can charge a 150% profit margin on electrolyzers while staying cost competitive
compared to competitors.
Tobe can charge $3.51 per kg of hydrogen on the low end. If customer utility costs are
low, Tobe can charge up to their current cost per kg – utilities – 15%.
Tobe can achieve a target growth rate of 50% by increasing its labor force
proportionately to the number of electrolyzers needed.

Context
The hydrogen industry constituted 2.5% of the energy consumption in 2021. Less than
1% of the hydrogen industry is currently considered green. However, green hydrogen
is projected to account for 30% of all hydrogen in 2030 and 73-100% of all hydrogen in

2050. By 2050, it’s predicted that hydrogen will make up 13% of the energy industry
granted that the world remains under the 1.5 degrees Celsius increase limit.
There are significant differences between green and grey hydrogen in the amount of
carbon emissions released and the cost. Green hydrogen releases less than 1 kg of
carbon emissions per kghydrogen while grey hydrogen releases about 12 kgs of
carbon emissions. Green hydrogen costs range from $4.5 to $12 per kghydrogen.
Meanwhile, grey hydrogen costs range from $1 to $3 per kghydrogen. The cost of
hydrogen is driven by economies of scale; the current environment does not have
sufficient demand or supply to.

Problem
The green hydrogen market dynamic is characterized by a supply/demand dilemma.
Various common forms of hydrogen, most notably grey hydrogen, are significantly
cheaper than green hydrogen. Due to grey hydrogens cheaper cost, we see a supply/
demand dilemma within green hydrogen industry in which there are not enough
producers of green hydrogen to be cost-efficient, while at the same time there is not
enough demand to drive increased production.
Transportation in the hydrogen market is the largest driver of cost increases.
Though grey hydrogen is cheaper at first glance, transportation costs for users far from
hydrogen supply can eradicate grey hydrogen cost advantage. The difficulty of moving
hydrogen efficiently and safely is a major reason for the high transportation costs.
Pipelines tend to be the cheapest form of transportation ($0.2-0.5/kg H₂), but given
their limited presence of 1,600 miles, this is only utilized by large-scale hydrogen users
for in-house hydrogen transport. Tube trailers transport compressed hydrogen around
the 150-mile range and is expensive ($0.9-1.9/kg H₂). Liquid tankers are better suited
than tube trailers for transporting larger amounts of hydrogen over longer distances,
but it is significantly more expensive ($2.7-3.2/kg H₂ ) due to energy and equipment
requirements for the liquefaction process. This inhibitor to infrastructure alongside
transportation cost barriers discourages potential users from adopting hydrogen as a
fuel source.
Figure 2
When the cost factors of grey hydrogen are considered per kg, the factors such as
operating expenses, profit margins, cost of hydrogen, and cost of production of the
hydrogen stay stable as mileage increases, as depicted in Figure 1. However, as
transportation factor is broken down per mile transported, it does not stay stable but

rather drives the price increase from $5 for a city located 5 miles away to over $50 for
a city located 1000 miles away. We measure these cost factors that encompass the
total cost for hydrogen for cities located increasingly farther away from the nearest
hydrogen supply to depict the impact of transportation costs on overall grey hydrogen
cost.

Ideal Customer
Figure 3
Tobe’s ideal customers are companies located 150 miles away from hydrogen hubs
and production sites because transporting hydrogen becomes costly due to
convergence into liquid hydrogen. Tobe offers on-site production, a cost-effective
solution. Additionally, there has been a large increase in the number of companies
declaring carbon-emission reduction goals with 45% of the Fortune 500 declaring
emission goals and 79% of North American companies setting a net-zero target. A few
such companies and industries with strong green initiatives or carbon-reduction goals
are also willing to pay a premium for green hydrogen, highlighting the global
environment drive. Customers most burdened by transportation costs and customers
who have set decarbonization goals are Tobe’s ideal customers who will benefit from
cost-efficient and zero-carbon emission electrolyzers.

Market
Tobe’s $9.81B serviceable market and 8 diversified segments, making up about 11% of
the total market and over 10 million kg/year of hydrogen, provide a focused pathway to
scale market share.
Market advantage exists where hydrogen demand is rising but delivery costs make
grey hydrogen uneconomical, making on-site green hydrogen production the most
cost-competitive solution. Tobe’s total addressable market is $90 billion but this figure
includes large portions of demand irrelevant to Tobe’s commercial model such as firms
who produce and use their own hydrogen on-site. Eliminating this section yields a
more realistic Serviceable Available Market (SAM). From this refined market, we
further narrow the SAM to U.S. demand segments where green hydrogen adoption is
accelerating due to industrial decarbonization goals, transportation fuel alternatives,
and newly implemented federal incentives for clean energy production. This produces
a Serviceable Obtainable Market (SOM) of $9.81 billion. The SOM reflects sectors that
not only show strong hydrogen growth but also are a good fit for Tobe’s electrolyzers’
capabilities, and hardware-plus-service business strategy.

Within this $9.81 billion SOM, the market is then segmented into eight commercially
distinct clusters, each chosen because of its strategic fit with Tobe’s HaaS and
Electrolyzer Sales offerings. These clusters reflect differences in hydrogen usage
patterns, infrastructure needs, capital budgeting behaviors, and likelihood of green
hydrogen adoption. Together, they represent a combined market value of $9.81 billion
served by approximately 32,025 potential customers.
The U.S. green hydrogen market is still in its early stages but it’s now characterized by
accelerated growth and favorable government policies. This improves the bankability
of Tobe’s projects since it can now structure both its HaaS deployments and
Electrolyzer Sales offerings around predictable, performance-based credits, reducing
revenue uncertainty and making project financing more feasible. Examples of these
policies include hydrogen production tax credits, the most significant of which came in
January 2025, when the U.S. Treasury finalized the long-awaited Section 45V
production tax credit (PTC). This credit provides up to $3 per kg of clean hydrogen
produced, depending on verified lifecycle emissions. The final rules on emissions
clarify eligibility and reporting pathways for projects that begin construction before
2033, which gives investors and developers a predictable framework for financial
modeling.
Segments
Figure 3
Our $9.81B serviceable market is distributed across eight priority customer segments.
Industrial decarbonization is the largest at $3.47B, followed by heavy-duty fueling at
$1.98B. Chemical production and synthetic fuels, along with energy storage and gridbalancing, each represent $0.99B opportunities. Urban fleet fueling, light and mediumduty commercial fleets, and backup power/microgrids each contribute $0.50B,
reflecting strong demand from municipal fleets, last-mile delivery operators, and
critical-infrastructure users. The remaining $0.90B comes from “already green”
hydrogen users, where Tobe can undercut competitors by offering the most costeffective solutions. Together, these eight segments form the most actionable portion of
our market and guide our go-to-market strategy.

Geographic Market Analysis
Figure 4
The map is a tool for TOBE to visualize where to expand the business. It is
recommended that TOBE expands the business 150 miles away from grey hydrogen

projects. The recommendation is based upon where it is most cost-effective and
profitable for Tobe to operate considering locations of competitors.
The map includes locations of green and non-green hydrogen projects, potential rivals,
ideal customers, companies whose customers are ideal customers for TOBE,
hydrogen pipelines, green and non-green hydrogen projects under construction and
hydrogen fueling stations.
The ideal customers were identified based on distance to the closest grey hydrogen
project. It was calculated that customers outside the 150-mile radius benefit
economically from choosing TOBE. The map visualizes the 150-mile distance in the
form of purple circles around the non-green hydrogen projects. Ideal customers are
located everywhere in the country. Locations of competitors were found using multiple
sources. Most data come from Clean Energy Group’s Hydrogen Project Map along
with a few other sources. Some grey hydrogen projects were left out when creating
Tobe’s map as those hydrogen projects are producing for on-site use. A limited number
of non-grey and non-green projects were included (pink, blue, turquoise hydrogen) but
were categorized together with the grey hydrogen projects as they aren’t considered
green.
Many of the ideal customers use hydrogen for material handling equipment e.g.
forklifts. Some ideal customers use hydrogen in their transit systems for hydrogenpowered buses. Other customers use hydrogen for trucks and ammonia production.
Furthermore, companies producing products that require hydrogen were identified as
their customer base are potential TOBE customers. See Appendix K for a full list of
ideal customers and companies whose customers are ideal TOBE customers.
There are 1600 miles of hydrogen pipelines across the US. Those are marked out by
red lines with a yellow 150-mile radius. In contrast, there are 300 million miles of
natural gas pipelines. The hydrogen pipelines are located mostly in Texas, California,
and Illinois. All hydrogen pipelines are used for feedstock and are not delivered to
private customers. According to industry professionals, natural gas pipelines cannot be
used for hydrogen transportation without improvement. Transporting hydrogen in nonhydrogen pipelines would result in the pipelines becoming brittle, and the gaskets
would lose elasticity.
Hydrogen hubs exist in multiple states across the country and differ from hydrogen
projects. Hydrogen hubs can include multiple hydrogen projects, and some states are
involved in a hydrogen hub without having a hydrogen project located in their states.
Many of the hydrogen hubs across the USA received funding from the government,
however, many lost their funding in October 2025. While the future of the defunded
hydrogen hubs is unclear, this could give TOBE an advantage in expanding the

business. If hydrogen hubs are stalled or canceled, TOBE could expand in those
areas.

Business Model
Tobe will benefit from 2 business models, Hydrogen-as-a-Service (HaaS) and
Electrolyzer Sales, that can address segments based on industry factors.
Figure 5
Electrolyzer Sales:
Tobe manufactures and sells electrolyzers at the following costs to companies or
contractors who will be using their own utilities to power the machines. In this model,
Tobe will be installing the machines to the companies location.
25kg electolyzer price is 43,125.00 per device
250kg electolyzer price is 600,000.00 per device
2500kg electolyzer price is 4,312,500.00 per device
Customers purchase the machines directly and manage hydrogen production
independently.
Tobe profits at a 30% margin from the sale and can add optional servicing contracts for
further revenue add to it such as installation, servicing, and long-term maintenance.
Figure 6
Hydrogen-as-a-Service (HaaS Model):
Tobe installs electrolyzers at client sites and maintains ownership.
Clients pay $3.51 per kg of hydrogen produced via 3-year service contracts, which
include maintenance, calibration, and operational data analytics.
This model offers recurring revenue and builds long-term relationships with industrial
and transportation clients

Value Add
Tobe creates value by redesigning the full cost structure of green hydrogen rather than
improving only one part of it. Their electrolyzers reduce the energy required to produce
hydrogen, which directly lowers electricity expenses, but the financial benefits extend

much further. Tobe’s systems reduce upfront capital costs, simplify installation, and cut
the ongoing maintenance burden that often makes traditional electrolyzers expensive
to operate. Their modular units also reduce the need for extensive construction work
and site preparation, and by producing hydrogen on-site, customers avoid the high
transportation and storage costs that usually make hydrogen projects difficult to justify.
The technology also reduces carbon emissions, helping customers qualify for federal
clean-energy incentives, ESG benefits, and carbon credit revenue.
The value-add calculator allows companies to quantify these savings using their own
project assumptions. Users can enter their expected energy consumption, electricity
pricing, annual hydrogen production, and both Tobe and competitor capital and
operating costs. They can also include the value of avoided carbon emissions if they
participate in carbon markets or incentive programs. Once these inputs are added, the
calculator combines them to show the total economic advantage of choosing Tobe.
This gives each company a clear, data-driven understanding of how much money they
can save and how quickly a Tobe system would pay back in their specific situation.
This Tobe Energy value-add calculator shows how much money a customer can save
by switching from a competitor’s electrolyzer to Tobe’s system. It compares the
competitor’s energy consumption (45.8 kWh/kg) to Tobe’s lower usage (35.2 kWh/kg),
multiplies the difference by the customer’s own annual hydrogen production and local
electricity price, and calculates yearly energy-cost savings. It then compares capital
expenditure per kilowatt between the competitor ($4,351/kW) and Tobe ($446/kW)
using the user’s system size in kilowatts, revealing upfront installation savings.
Operating savings come from taking the competitor’s annual OPEX (e.g., $1,000,000)
and applying Tobe’s 45% reduction factor. The calculator also includes environmental
value by multiplying carbon dioxide avoided (9,125 tons) by a carbon price ($25/ton).
By changing these input values, production, electricity price, system size, CAPEX
numbers, OPEX numbers, and carbon price, any customer can plug in their own data
and instantly see their personalized Total Value Add, which in this example equals
$1,649,280.

Financial Model
Tobe could attain $657.7 million in profit by 2033 by instituting a 150% profit margin on
electrolyzers and a 30% profit margin on hydrogen.

Revenue Model
Tobe’s hybrid model (HaaS + Electrolyzer-Sales) guarantees predictable revenue:
HaaS captures recurring income from distance-sensitive users, while electrolyzer sales
monetize large industrial users with existing infrastructure.
The HaaS model provides the foundation for recurring revenue, which stabilizes cash
flow and ensures sustainable profitability as Tobe scales. Additionally, it generates
steady income by producing and selling hydrogen directly to end-users at their
facilities. According to Tobe’s financial outlook, each partner site is modeled with an
installed capacity of 2.65 MW, producing about 37,500 kgs per month, with the ability
to scale up to 5.72 MW (75,000 kg/month) as demand increases. Tobe’s hydrogen will
sell at $3.51/kg, with production costs near $3.96/kg, yielding a 30% gross margin. By
operating 330 days per year, Tobe will achieve profitability while keeping its services
competitively priced.
The HaaS model ensures that Tobe can increase revenue without increasing capital
risk, which is advantageous for a rollout aiming to gain traction in the early years.
Market assumptions project an initial reach of 20% of the ideal, targetable customer
pool in Year 1, expanding to 60% by Year 5, supported by a 10% annual market growth
rate. Customers pay through a blended service fee ($0.02 per day annually) combining fixed equipment uptime payments and variable per-kg charges based on
how much hydrogen is produced by Tobe‘s electrolyzers.
Prioritizing early deployment in mobility and distributed power segments enables Tobe
to acquire early adopters as their first customers and achieve volume-driven
profitability. This should give Tobe the benefit of economies of scale and lower per-kg
production costs faster than its competitors. The strongest HaaS opportunities are in
urban and fleet fueling, backup power, and energy storage, which together represent
about $2.35 billion in accessible market value and 21,000 potential customers. These
sectors have high energy needs (which translates to high need for hydrogen as a
potential fuel) and including them in the rollout plan should reduce revenue volatility
and simplify logistics management across multiple sites. Locking customers into longterm service contracts increases credibility and Tobe’s potential to attract investors,
both of which are key to funding future expansion. Each HaaS site would operate
under a 3-year contract, blending fixed lease payments with per-kg usage fees.
Clients would commit to monthly minimum volumes that ensure baseline revenue,
while uptime guarantees and integrated O&M would build trust.
Table 2

Structuring the model around a variety of products creates a balanced, scalable sales
model that enables early liquidity and lays the foundation for long-term relationships
with customers. Prioritizing high-consumption sectors ensures Tobe anchors its rollout
in markets which would deliver immediate revenue. The largest opportunities for
electrolyzer sales lie in industrial decarbonization (electronics, glass, and food
processing), chemical and synthetic fuel production, and light commercial fueling,
totaling about $6.56 billion in reachable market value and 10,870 potential customers.

Cost Structure
For the HaaS model, the capital expenditure (CAPEX) includes electrolyzer
equipment, compression and purification systems, RO water treatment, and facility
installation. Breaking down OPEX categories allows Tobe to control recurring costs
and maintain its 30% gross margin target even as electricity prices fluctuate. HaaS
operating expenses (OPEX) include electricity, compression and purification, RO water
consumables, and personnel costs estimated at $228,000 per year for a fully
operational customer facility - these costs would be incurred by the consumer. Direct
variable production costs are $3.96/kg of hydrogen, combining electricity,
maintenance, and operational overheads.
For the Electrolyzer Sales model, understanding per-unit CAPEX and OPEX clarifies
how sales revenue translates into profit margins and reinvestment potential for future
growth. Each electrolyzer unit carries base equipment costs (COGS) of $15,000 for 25
kg/day, $150,000 for 250 kg/day, and $1.5 million for 2,500 kg/day systems.
Installation, materials, personnel, and R&D costs are variable; they’ll scale with
production or customization level rather than fixed overhead. The model targets a 30%
gross margin, reflecting the difference between these COGS and the selling prices
listed in the revenue table ($19,500, $1.95M, and $2.535M, respectively). This cost
structure enables Tobe to adjust margins per system size while maintaining predictable
profitability as volumes increase.
Table 3
Across both business lines, fixed costs consist of long-term CAPEX (plant
construction, installed equipment, and depreciation), while variable costs include
electricity, consumables, labor, and materials. So, in initial expansion stages, there are
high fixed costs, but unit costs decrease as production scales. Under the HaaS model,
OPEX is primarily variable, supporting scalability without major new investment per
site. Under the Electrolyzer Sales model, costs remain flexible through contractorbased installation and third-party EPC channels. This cost structure makes Tobe’s
rollout scalable and capital-efficient, enabling expansion without proportionate
increases in overhead.

5-Year Financial Projections
Figure 7
The financial projections for Tobe are built on a five-year horizon, beginning directly
after the implementation of our proposed rollout plan. These projections model both
revenue streams - Hydrogen-as-a-Service (HaaS) and Electrolyzer Sales - to reflect
the company’s hybrid market entry strategy. Each model is derived from benchmarked
hydrogen pricing and equipment costs. For HaaS, revenues are tied to kgs of
hydrogen produced and sold per day and for Electrolyzer Sales, revenues are based
on unit sales of 25 kg/day, 250 kg/day, and 2,500 kg/day systems. Revenue models for
both streams have an imposed profit margin of 30% because typically, the majority of
electrolyzer manufacturers set their profit margin between 25% and 35% on system
sales before scaling up production.
Growth rates assume a 10% annual success rate for pitched sales (given that this is
just below typical for B2B sales), as well as a 10% market growth rate which reflects
the compounded annual growth rate of the green hydrogen market. The model
emphasizes that Tobe’s profitability can be achieved by scaling both service and
equipment volumes, balancing long-term recurring income (HaaS) with short-cycle
cash inflows (Electrolyzer-Sales).
Across both business lines, several common operating assumptions were applied to
ensure consistency and comparability. Production sites under the HaaS model are
assumed to operate 330 days per year to allow for scheduled maintenance and
downtime (Clean Hydrogen provides a KPI assumption of 8000 working hours of a
system which is about 333 days). Cost inputs - including labor, materials, and O&M are escalated at a 2.5% annual inflation rate, consistent with FED inflation targets.

Threats & Opportunities Analysis
Government incentives and rising industrial demand are accelerating the shift toward
green hydrogen, while regulatory uncertainty and infrastructure gaps require Tobe to
scale carefully to capture opportunities.
Government policy creates several major opportunities for clean hydrogen, especially
for smaller companies targeting rural or off-pipeline markets. Federal incentives, such
as the Inflation Reduction Act’s clean hydrogen production tax credits, significantly
reduce the cost gap between green and grey hydrogen, making decentralized
production more economically attractive. The Department of Energy and state-level
programs also offer grants, rebates, and support for clean energy projects, while the

$7 billion investment into Regional Hydrogen Hubs helps drive demand for new
hydrogen technologies. These policies encourage industries like ammonia production,
chemicals, metallurgy, and power generation to shift toward low-carbon fuels, creating
a growing customer base. Rural energy and resiliency initiatives further support local,
on-site hydrogen production, exactly the type of market segment that benefits from
modular electrolyzer systems.
Similarly, federal agencies are building the domestic hydrogen supply chain. In March
2024, DOE awarded $750 million to expand electrolyzer R&D, manufacturing, and
recycling across 52 projects. The DOE Loan Programs Office also issued a $1.66
billion loan guarantee to Plug Power for multiple green hydrogen plants, signaling that
the federal government is ready to support first-of-a-kind production at scale. These
moves are expected to reduce equipment costs, strengthen supply-chains, and
expand the U.S. manufacturing base needed for widespread deployment.
At the same time, government-related threats create real challenges. Policy
uncertainty around tax credit qualifications, renewable energy matching rules, and
carbon intensity measurements could make compliance expensive for smaller
producers. Water regulations also pose a risk, since electrolyzers require very clean
water and many states face restrictions due to drought. Large companies such as
Siemens, Plug, and Bloom have the scale and political influence to navigate shifting
regulations more easily, creating competitive pressure. Other threats include slow
development of hydrogen infrastructure, especially pipeline limitations due to hydrogen
embrittlement, and the high cost of materials like platinum, which current electrolyzers
rely on. Together, these factors make the market promising but also dependent on
careful navigation of regulatory and technological risks.
Reaching cost parity with gray hydrogen would be the green hydrogen industry’s
defining milestone. Today, gray hydrogen costs $1-2/kg, while green hydrogen costs
between $4-8/kg depending on electricity prices, among other factors. Analysts
forecast the price of green hydrogen to decrease by 40 to 80% between now and
2030, contingent on renewable power costs, electrolyzer scale-up, and continued
policy support. The combination of 45V credits and cheap renewable power could
bring effective green hydrogen costs down to $2–3/kg by the end of this decade. Tobe
should time its large-scale HaaS rollouts to coincide with these falling cost curves by
targeting anchor customers (refineries, fleet depots) by 2027–2030, when green
hydrogen is projected to achieve economic viability without heavy reliance on
subsidization.

Competitive Overview
By combining dual revenue models, low-cost green hydrogen production, and the
ability to serve markets unreachable by legacy players or emerging alternatives, Tobe
is positioned as the most flexible, affordable, and scalable solution for accelerating
distributed hydrogen adoption.
Figure 8
Tobe’s dual-market strategy, direct electrolyzer sales and Hydrogen-as-a-Service,
ensures revenue diversification while penetrating both capital-heavy and contractbased markets.
Tobe uniquely serves industries distant from hydrogen pipelines, including remote
manufacturing, fleet transport, and data centers. Through its focus on small-to-mid
scale adoption, data-backed operations, and zero-emission production, Tobe Energy
stands out as the most affordable, flexible, and sustainable hydrogen solution currently
available.
Tobe stands out from legacy players like Siemens, Rockwell, Bloom, Plug, and Nel
because they are not tied to decades-old infrastructure, slow upgrade cycles, or the
high-cost structures that come with traditional industrial companies and gray hydrogen
producers. Instead, they are building a modern and modular electrolyzer designed
from day one for lower cost per kg, higher efficiency, and real scalability. Siemens and
Rockwell focus on broad industrial automation rather than hydrogen, while Bloom,
Plug, and Nel often face high capital costs, complex maintenance requirements, and
inconsistent uptime across different deployments. Tobe offers a simpler and more
predictable system that replaces bulky and maintenance-heavy stacks with technology
that is easier to deploy and service. Their approach is also designed to integrate
seamlessly with partners such as Zeeco and Loves, allowing for immediate use in both
grid and off-grid environments. As many competitors continue to struggle with the
economics of green hydrogen and regularly miss their target production costs, Tobe is
intentionally engineered to meet competitive cost goals without sacrificing reliability,
giving them a strong advantage in new hydrogen markets that prioritize speed,
affordability, and clean performance.
A competitor to Tobe that stands out is HyVera. HyVera, a Canadian company that
recently expanded to the US has two offices located in California and Indiana. HyVera,
like Tobe, does not require expensive transportation which is one of the obstacles
keeping hydrogen prices high. HyVera’s technology produces fuel pellets that, when
combined with water, results in a reaction that produces hydrogen. The leftover
material can be reused a second time to produce more hydrogen.

Appendix
Appendix A
Email answers from Amish Patel, Business Solutions, Enterprise Products
What are the operations behind Hydrogen production?
The primary source of Industrial Hydrogen is from Natural Gas (CH4). Hydrogen is a
product of the SynGas (Synthesis Gas) process, which is decomposing hydrocarbons
into hydrogen and carbon monoxide gases. The most energy efficient way to create
pure hydrogen is “reforming” natural gas to H2 and CO.
Another industrial way of making hydrogen is via electrolysis, which is running
electricity through water (adding electrolytes improves the reaction) with a cathode
(where the H2 forms) and anode (where the O2 forms). It requires a lot of power and is
very corrosive on the electrodes. However, if you have a green energy source (wind,
solar, etc..), it is the only “green” way to produce hydrogen, so the renewed interest
recently.
How do Hydrogen contracts work?
I have not worked with the contracts too much, but as much of the oil and gas/
chemicals industry requires, a long-term deal is needed to support the large capital
investment required to build a hydrogen plant and associated pipeline. The key players
in the hydrogen business are the Industrial Gas companies (Air Liquide, Air Products,
and Linde are the major ones in the US). So, the customer(s) will typically pay a fee to
consume or will sell hydrogen to these Industrial Gas companies.
How is Hydrogen moved?
The bulk of hydrogen is moved by pipeline. Additionally, hydrogen can be moved on
multi-cylinder trailers or liquified bullet trailers by semi-trucks. Cylinders are also
delivered as you might find in the labs at OU.

What does Hydrogen end use look like?
Bulk usage is for chemical processes as a feedstock. In the refining industry, it is used
to remove sulfur from fuels. Sometimes it is used as fuel in facilities since it requires
further processing to purify. Very little is used for powering vehicles.
How is green hydrogen changing the industry?
Predominantly hydrogen is used to hydrotreat fuels in refineries, reducing drastically
the amount of sulfuric compounds in fuels, which when burned can produce SO2 and
SO3 that will turn in the atmosphere by mixing with water to making acid rain (H2SO4
– sulfuric acid). Hydrogen has tried to be commercialized as a fuel source for vehicles,
but the market has not supported it.

Appendix B
Interview with Amish Patel, Business Solutions, Enterprise Products
What are factors in contracts
Industrial customers primarily use hydrogen for chemical processes. These users
typically rely on long-term contracts due to the high capital expenses associated with
production and infrastructure. Natural gas feedstock costs can reach approximately
$300 million, and small-diameter hydrogen pipelines cost $3–4 million per mile.
Fee-based contracts are common; fee levels correlate directly with hydrogen
production costs, averaging roughly $0.70 per kg. Companies target a 12–15% hurdle
rate above cost to achieve acceptable profitability. Anchor customers are a critical
requirement for project viability.
The hydrogen market is dominated by major players including Air Liquide, Linde, Air
Products, Nippon Steel, and Messer (with Messer absorbed by Air Liquide in the U.S.).
Hydrogen transportation by truck is limited to roughly 1,800 lbs. in gaseous form. Only
two plants currently support liquid hydrogen transport (requires verification). Smaller
customers, such as school laboratories, purchase hydrogen in very small quantities.
Where are H pipelines/refineries?
On-site hydrogen production provides substantial operational value. Hydrogen
generation produces multiple by-products and requires significant heat inputs; these
outputs must be effectively managed. Integrating production within a refinery enables
the steam by-product to be absorbed and reduces environmental imbalance.

Large-scale production benefits from economies of scale to achieve acceptable energy
efficiency. Existing hydrogen pipeline infrastructure in the U.S. is concentrated along
the coast, particularly between Corpus Christi and New Orleans. There are three
underground storage wells along these pipelines, with a possible fourth in
development. Another potential storage project exists in Utah, tied to an electrolysis
and long-duration storage concept.
Pipeline routing and system information can be found on operator websites and
through mapping resources maintained by the Texas Railroad Commission. Globally,
the largest hydrogen pipeline systems are expected to be in the United States, Europe,
Saudi Arabia, and China. Tax incentives encourage placing hydrogen plants within
refineries and moving hydrogen by pipeline. Upfront costs include plant construction
followed by customer-specific pipeline investment.
What kind of niche or smaller industries is hydrogen moving into?
Hydrogen currently supports various chemical reaction processes, although this sector
is not experiencing significant growth. Enterprise-level users rely on hydrogen for
these applications. The strongest growth area remains hydrogen-fueled vehicles,
particularly in California. Japan is utilizing hydrogen for ammonia production.
Hydrogenation markets are not expected to expand, especially with political factors
such as RFK’s health initiatives.
The electronics and semiconductor sector represents a growing but small-scale use
case; as chip manufacturing expands, hydrogen demand increases accordingly.
Certain automotive manufacturers, such as Honda, demonstrate potential synergies
between hydrogen and electric vehicles. Hydrogen may also see niche residential
adoption for hydrogen-powered cars. Opportunities exist in energy generation and in
server applications (e.g., Bloom).
What is the difference between small and large electrolyzers?
Electrolyzers require substantial electricity. Small electrolyzers can operate effectively
because their power demands remain manageable. Large-scale electrolyzers,
however, require extremely high electricity input, raising concerns about sourcing
adequate and reliable power. In Texas, the ERCOT grid provides a mixed energy
supply but experiences measurable power loss. Both grey and green hydrogen
production at scale involve trade-offs related to electricity requirements and efficiency.
Are there any markets you see transportation costs being a limiter?
Transportation costs can limit hydrogen market expansion. Historically, hydrogen
growth has been tied to cleaner fuel applications. Fuel cells present a possible

pathway for residential power. However, regions highly sensitive to CO₂ emissions are
generally unsuitable for hydrogen production due to process-related impacts.
What would you say is the most important for H use: reliability, price, sustainability?
The hydrogen market presents a unique dynamic. Although hydrogen is marketed as a
green energy solution due to its lack of downstream pollutants, industrial buyers
prioritize cost above sustainability or reliability, provided production does not release
harmful toxins. The environmental benefit of hydrogen depends significantly on the
energy source used during production.
Hydrogen remains a low-margin industry, where profitability depends heavily on
volume. A key recommendation is to review the historical growth trajectory of hydrogen
to understand long-term market behavior.
Amish's advice for us was to look at how hydrogen has grown historically and look for
the indicators for where it will grow next.

Appendix E
Interview with Steve Szymanski, Vice President of Sales, and Marketing, Nel
How much does an electrolyzer cost?
The cost of an electrolyzer itself is relatively small compared to the total cost of
producing hydrogen. Approximately 70% of total production cost comes from
electricity, underscoring the importance of efficiency. Water and capital expenses
contribute minimally to overall cost. As a result, cheap power directly translates into
cheap hydrogen, and access to green energy is essential; without renewable inputs,
the environmental value proposition of hydrogen is diminished.
A major industry benchmark is the goal of building large-scale electrolyzer plants for
$1,000 per kW, a price point regarded as highly attractive. Utilities are treated strictly
as operating costs, as electrolyzer manufacturers do not produce hydrogen, they only
sell equipment. In all cases, the plant owner, not the equipment company, is
responsible for utility expenditures.
“If you can build an electrolyzer plant (large scale) for $1000/kW a lot of people would
be really excited about that.”
When you approach customers with assumptions, how do you sell? How do you find
them?
When approaching customers, assumptions about costs and operating conditions
influence how the value proposition is communicated. In an unsubsidized market, grey
hydrogen remains cheaper than green hydrogen. Bridging this cost gap requires
demand-side incentives, such as pay-for-difference programs or carbon taxes. While
many companies pursue ESG goals, the cost differential between green and
conventional hydrogen remains challenging to justify commercially.
Can you describe some of the markets you have seen reach out to Nel?
Companies reaching out to electrolyzer suppliers tend to be conventional hydrogen
users, particularly those who rely on hydrogen as an input chemical. Typical sectors
include semiconductor manufacturing, steel treatment, and glass processing. Demand
for green hydrogen within these traditional industries is growing, and these markets
remain core opportunities for equipment providers.

Customers frequently evaluate whether it makes more sense to receive delivered
hydrogen or produce hydrogen on-site. Many prefer self-reliance and are hesitant to
enter long-term supply contracts. While industrial decarbonization attracts more public
excitement, traditional markets still represent steady, reliable demand.
Have you noticed that customers are far away? What’s the main motivation?
Transportation logistics strongly influence customer decisions. Compressed hydrogen
has a limited transportation radius of roughly 150 miles, making it impractical for longdistance delivery. Liquid hydrogen travels further but is more expensive and
susceptible to boil-off losses. In some regions, hydrogen is delivered from Canada,
especially along the East Coast.
The guiding principle is that “the best place to put an electrolyzer is at the point of use.”
This reduces transportation costs and dependency on delivered fuel.
Central vs decentralized plant?
Certain customers prefer fully owning and operating their electrolyzer systems. For
example, a steel manufacturer in West Virginia purchased equipment to remain selfsufficient.
A notable example of centralized production is a planned 60-ton/day electrolyzer and
liquefaction plant in Ardmore, developed by Woodside Energy. The plant would sell
hydrogen to companies fueling trucks, but the project is currently delayed due to
failures among hydrogen truck startups. Many customers in similar projects are large
oil and gas companies who view hydrogen as an additional project type and a pathway
into decarbonization markets.
Would HaaS be a feasible model?
Hydrogen-as-a-Service (HaaS) models carry significant risk. Cost exposure is a key
concern: if hydrogen is sold for $5/kg but actual production costs land at $6/kg, the
provider incurs direct losses. Manufacturers often consider it “cleaner” to simply sell
electrolyzer equipment rather than sell hydrogen itself.
Leasing models have been explored since leasing is categorized as an operating
expense for customers. However, categorizing the sale of hydrogen molecules within
such a model introduces complications.
Industrial gas suppliers often impose penalties and force majeure terms that place
heavy burdens on customers, reinforcing the appeal of customer-owned systems.
Even with on-premise hydrogen generation, selling hydrogen to the customer still
creates exposure similar to traditional gas supply models.

How do contracts work?
Electrolyzer sales typically follow a structured payment schedule. Once equipment is
installed, manufacturers require full payment. To maintain long-term engagement and
recurring revenue, suppliers also provide long-term service agreements, covering
maintenance and support for the installed equipment.

Appendix F
Email correspondence with Steve Szymanski, VP of Sales, and Marketing, Nel
The length of the sales cycle and the “win rate” varies widely depending on the size of
the electrolyzer system. For example, we have some smaller machines (just a few kW
of input power) that we can sometimes sell in just a few months from our initial
engagement with the customer. We also don’t have a lot of competition in that size
range, which helps. As I was telling you in our call, the real competition is delivered
hydrogen. So, if a customer has already made up their mind that they want to produce
their own hydrogen, our win rate is quite good. Even so, these are still capital
purchases, as even the smaller systems cost $100K. Customers typically do capital
budgeting on an annual basis. So, if you start talking to them in 2025, and they don’t
have money approved in their 2025 capital budget, they will end up deferring the
purchase to 2026. Some customers prefer to pay for hydrogen as an operating
expense, and that is where delivered hydrogen is their preferred solution.
For larger electrolyzer equipment (MWs of power), the sales cycle can be very long
(2+ years in some cases), and many projects don’t end up happening because the
client can’t get financing. In these types of projects, the success rate can be quite low,
even though our equipment is preferred by the customer. I can’t put a specific number
on it, but anecdotally I would guess that less than 10% of larger projects we quote end
up turning into a sale of some kind. Again, these are quotes for millions of dollars’
worth of electrolyzer equipment, and they will often require significant third-party
financing. The low success rate is more a function of the project not being “bankable”
than the client not liking our technical solution. Hopefully, that makes sense.

Appendix G
Interview with Dipankar Ghosh, ConocoPhillips Chair in Energy Accounting, University
of Oklahoma
According to Dipankar Ghosh, the cost of green hydrogen is currently not viable. The
central issue is that electrolyzers remain too expensive, though companies like Tobe

aim to reduce this barrier. Green hydrogen’s competitiveness relies heavily on
subsidies, which play a major role across the hydrogen industry.
A recurring theme is the importance of lowering electricity costs. Cheap electricity
directly reduces hydrogen prices, making overall production more feasible. Companies
often use excess electricity—particularly medium-sized power plants—to generate
hydrogen. Storage represents another significant cost, and reducing the cost of green
electricity is viewed as essential to improving market viability.
The primary question companies ask is: “How much carbon are you releasing?”
Environmental impact is central to green hydrogen adoption. Many companies are
adopting CO₂ reduction goals, and consumer awareness is driving interest in greener
alternatives.
Green hydrogen’s output is oriented toward green or low-carbon applications only,
limiting its market reach compared to grey hydrogen. India is currently the largest
investor in green hydrogen, and global adoption is growing more rapidly outside the
United States.
Transporting hydrogen is both cost-intensive and logistically complex. This serves as
one of the hidden and often underestimated costs of hydrogen supply. Storage adds
further expense, reinforcing the need to locate hydrogen production near the point of
use.
Dipankar noted meaningful potential for on-site electrolyzers, particularly for smaller
businesses. On-site generation helps avoid transportation challenges and reduces
exposure to hidden costs associated with delivery and storage. Certain sectors stand
to benefit from on-site production, especially those prioritizing self-reliance and
sustainability.
There is more global growth in green hydrogen than in the U.S., driven by national
sustainability targets and consumer expectations around environmental impact. The
industry’s long-term trajectory will depend heavily on electricity affordability, continued
subsidies, and companies' ability to meet their green and CO₂-based performance
goals.

Appendix H
Interview Notes with Dr. Runar Nygaard, University of Oklahoma
Hydrogen

It’s important to know that hydrogen is not an energy source, it’s an energy carrier. It
can also be used for storing energy. On-site production could be better if we know how
to produce on-site, but current production is large-scale and industrial complex.
Hydrogen Pipelines
There are researchers at OU that are looking at how much hydrogen can be added
into ordinary pipelines without having to build a new pipeline system. There are
companies right now that are adding about 5% of hydrogen into their natural gas
stream. They think that up to 20% of hydrogen could be added into natural gas
pipelines but they don’t know and there are no standards yet. He said that the question
is whether it might be better to take hydrogen and add carbon dioxide to it to create a
synthetic fuel. He said it’s much more like natural gas or gasoline; it looks like a
hydrocarbon which makes it easier to transport.
The current hydrogen pipelines go between manufacturers to other petrochemical
plants that use hydrogen. He explained that if we all go in for hydrogen, the current
pipelines would need to be replaced. However, current pipelines could be improved by
putting liners in every pipeline. The reason hydrogen can’t be transported in natural
gas pipelines is because the stainless steel used for natural gas pipelines is not
resistant enough for hydrogen. Hydrogen causes the stainless steel to become brittle
and the gaskets that are made from rubber lose their elasticity.
Hydrogen and location
He said that hydrogen uses a lot of fresh water. A lot of places where hydrogen can be
beneficial are in areas with excess electricity production, e.g. Oklahoma, Texas, and
Western USA. Such areas have a lot of wind and solar power which already uses a lot
of water. Thus, it will create a strain on the water supply. He explained there’s a
mismatch between where the cheap and excess electricity is and where it would be
best to produce hydrogen. Producing hydrogen in the Great Lakes area would be good
considering the amount of fresh water.

Appendix I
Interview with Lance Lobban, Professor of Chemical Engineering, University of
Oklahoma
1. Cleanliness and Cost of Hydrogen Pathways
Professor Lobban emphasized that green hydrogen is only clean when powered by
solar or wind electricity. Grey hydrogen remains significantly cheaper because it relies

on natural gas (methane) as its feedstock. Transitioning to green hydrogen requires
clean water, excess renewable electricity, and customers who prioritize low emissions.
Electrolysis remains a major inefficiency in the hydrogen supply chain. Electrolyzers
also require very clean water, adding an additional cost burden. High-pressure
electrolysis could help with storage needs but would make equipment more expensive.
2. Transportation and On-Site Production
Transporting hydrogen is expensive, making on-site production especially valuable—
particularly in rural or off-pipeline regions, which align well with your target market.
Hydrogen pipelines cannot simply be repurposed because the gas causes metal
embrittlement, which compromises pipeline integrity.
Hydrogen can be stored chemically in liquids, but this approach requires large
chemical plants and results in energy loss during each conversion step.
3. Storage Challenges
Storage remains one of the largest unknowns and most significant challenges for the
hydrogen industry. Professor Lobban noted that batteries make more sense for smallscale storage, while hydrogen is more viable for mid- to large-scale energy storage
applications.
4. Industrial Users and Applications
The largest current users of hydrogen include:
Ammonia production
Metallurgy
Chemical manufacturing
High-heat industrial processes
Power generation via fuel cells
Hydrogen for vehicles is technically feasible, but costly, because fueling systems and
gas tanks require redesign.
5. Market Fit and Customer Considerations
For smaller, rural customers with modest hydrogen demand and limited pipeline
access, on-site electrolyzer solutions are practical and logical. Leasing models—like
solar panel leasing—may accelerate adoption by lowering upfront costs and allowing

technology upgrades over time. Selling machines outright risks locking customers into
outdated technology.
Selling hydrogen directly is less attractive due to the high cost of transportation.
6. Technology, Materials, and Industry Structure
Electrolyzers often rely on platinum catalysts, increasing capital cost; research into
cheaper alternatives is ongoing. High-pressure electrolyzers may aid storage but
would bring higher upfront equipment costs.
Industry structure is likely to favor large companies such as Siemens, which have the
scale to integrate production systems, fuel cells, and equipment. Smaller companies
(e.g., Plug, Bloom) may remain niche players or become acquisition targets.

Appendix J
Interview with Louis Mousney, Co-founder of Tobe
1. Background and Expertise
Louis Mousney is based in Paris and currently works at Arkema, a major French
chemical company. He leads the firm’s green hydrogen strategy, covering both
technical and commercial dimensions. He possesses extensive knowledge across the
entire hydrogen value chain, including global markets, policies, electrolyzer
manufacturers, production economics, transportation, storage, and industrial endusers.
He recently submitted his three-month notice to leave Arkema and will join Tobe
Energy full-time as CFO and Co-Founder alongside Colby. Mounsey has been
involved with Tobe since its early days due to his close relationship with Colby.
Tobe’s near-term focus is the U.S. domestic hydrogen market, with no immediate plans
for international expansion.
2. Overview of the U.S. Hydrogen Market
The U.S. hydrogen market is currently 95–99% gray hydrogen, produced via steam
methane reforming (SMR). Green hydrogen adoption is limited almost entirely by the
high cost of renewable electricity and the high cost of hydrogen transportation and
storage.
Hydrogen is extremely expensive to move. For example:
Production cost may be ~$3/kg

Delivered cost in places like Tulsa or Seattle can reach $25/kg due to compression,
trucking, and handling
This transportation premium is one of the biggest structural inefficiencies in the market.
3. Tobe Energy’s Value Proposition
Tobe’s model centers on placing electrolyzers directly at the customer’s site,
eliminating transportation costs. This allows Tobe to be cost-competitive even without
subsidies.
Ideal initial markets include regions with:
High industrial hydrogen usage
No local hydrogen production
High delivered hydrogen prices Examples include Tulsa and Seattle.
4. Partnership With Zeeco
Zeeco is a major global player in combustion engineering and testing, working with
clients such as Chevron to develop hydrogen-capable burners. Their testing facilities
handle:
100% hydrogen combustion
100% natural gas
Mixed hydrogen/gas blends
Their customers seek flexible burners capable of running on multiple fuels.
Why Zeeco Needs Hydrogen
Zeeco already consumes hydrogen for burner R&D. Currently, they source hydrogen
via tube trailers from Houston, which is expensive.
Tobe will replace this supply with on-site green hydrogen produced using their
electrolyzer.
Simplified Supply Chain for the Partnership
Tobe installs electrolyzer at Zeeco’s site.
Zeco provides renewable electricity to power it.
Electrolyzer splits water to produce green hydrogen.

Hydrogen is used directly on-site in combustion systems.
No downstream distribution is needed.
Financial Mechanics
Zeeco currently buys delivered gray hydrogen at high cost.
With Tobe, they stop buying delivered hydrogen.
Instead, they pay Tobe for on-site green hydrogen or enter a structured partnership.
Zeeco lowers operational expenses, while Tobe remains price competitive.
Why Zeeco Is Considered a “Partner”
Zeeco positions itself as a partner to its clients. For Tobe, this partnership provides:
Industry visibility
Credibility and validation
A major testing environment
Access to clients who need hydrogen test infrastructure
5. Business Models: Equipment Sales vs. HaaS
1. Equipment Sales (Electrolyzer Sales)
Tobe sells the electrolyzer directly.
Customer owns, operates, and maintains it.
Lower risk for Tobe; simpler contracting.
Revenue is traditional and upfront.
2. Hydrogen-as-a-Service (HaaS)
Tobe owns and operates the electrolyzer.
Tobe sells hydrogen generated on-site.
Recurring, higher-upside revenue model.
Significantly higher operational and financial risk.
Risks and Constraints of HaaS

Liability for leaks, explosions, and safety incidents
Compliance obligations: EPA, DOT, and permitting
Operational staffing and monitoring needs
Need for external financing (banks, investors)
Revenue exposure to hydrogen price fluctuations
Customer demand risk if usage drops
Because of these risks, HaaS requires careful contract design.
6. Contract Length Expectations
Industry-standard hydrogen contracts run 3–5 years, regardless of green or gray
hydrogen. Customers hesitate to sign longer agreements due to emerging technology
uncertainty.
Traditional SMR plants require long-term contracts because their financing depends on
predictable offtake. Tobe’s modular electrolyzers do not require long financing cycles,
so contract lengths can remain flexible or shorter.
7. International vs. U.S. Market Differences
Factors That Do Translate Internationally
Hydrogen cost drivers: electricity, electrolyzer capex, O&M
The global need to reduce hydrogen cost
Major market players (Air Liquide, Linde, Air Products) operate worldwide
End-uses and technology trends are globally similar
Factors That Do Not Translate
Policy stability
The U.S. has volatile hydrogen policy (administration changes)
The IRA sunsets in 2026
Europe offers long-term, stable support
China and the Middle East follow predictable state-driven roadmaps
U.S. policy uncertainty is a major barrier to long-term project planning.

8. Cost Modeling Guidance
Louis recommends modeling hydrogen cost using the following drivers:
Renewable electricity cost (largest factor)
Electrolyzer capital cost
Operations & maintenance
Purification requirements
Compression needs
Storage needs
Your model should include:
A with-subsidies case (IRA + state incentives)
A no-subsidy case (post-IRA)
Regional consumption adjustments by industry density
Comparison of delivered gray hydrogen prices to demonstrate savings
9. Hydrogen Use in Data Centers
Hydrogen is emerging but not yet widespread in data centers. Current applications
include:
Backup power
Peak shaving to avoid expensive daytime electricity
Adoption is slowed by storage cost, and commercial-scale implementation is not yet
common.

Version 2.0 · February 2026

Confidential

T OBE ENERGY · C OMPETITOR & AC QUIRER OVERVIEW

02

Siemens Energy is a major player in the global energy sector, pulling in around €29 billion ($31 billion
USD) in revenue. They've been really ramping up their hydrogen portfolio, especially with their Silyzer
series for industrial electrolysis. Siemens has a habit of scooping up or partnering with companies that
can help them scale effectively or cut costs, making them a strong candidate for acquiring new tech
that aligns with their decarbonization goals. Cummins Inc. / Hydrogenics Cummins is a big name,
generating about $28 billion USD in annual revenue. They've been putting serious money into
hydrogen technologies lately. Their buyout of Hydrogenics in 2019 was a clear move to expand their
electrolysis capabilities. They're still actively looking for ways to strengthen their hydrogen production
and distribution systems.
Thyssenkrupp Uhde Chlorine Engineers
Thyssenkrupp is a heavyweight, with €44 billion ($47 billion USD) in revenue. They've been growing their
industrial hydrogen and chemical engineering footprint through smart acquisitions and joint ventures. Recently,
they've been zeroing in on scaling up large alkaline electrolysis systems, making them a strong potential buyer for
any tech that can optimize large-scale hydrogen production. Prepared Exclusively for 46VC Pag e 1
Plug Power
Plug Power reported $700 million USD in revenue in 2023, but their market cap sits around $4 billion USD thanks
to a strong growth outlook. They've been snapping up companies like Giner ELX and United Hydrogen to vertically
integrate and cement their position in the hydrogen supply chain. Their strategy is all about building a
comprehensive hydrogen ecosystem, so they're keen on new technologies that can boost production efficiency.
NEL Hydrogen NEL is a more specialized player in the hydrogen market, with revenue around $130 million USD
and a market cap of about $1.5 billion USD. While they tend to favor partnerships over direct acquisitions, NEL
has shown interest in picking up niche technologies that complement their PEM and alkaline electrolysis
platforms. They're smaller than some of the other players, but they've carved out a solid position in the industry.
Tobe Energy stands out in the hydrogen space by delivering a next-generation electrolysis technology focused on
efficiency and scalability. With a clear path to commercial deployment and a competitive edge in production costs,
we are well-positioned to partner with industry leaders seeking innovative solutions for a decarbonized future.

PUBLIC MASTER PLAN
Strategic Roadmap
moves hydrogen - and by doing so, help usher in a future free of fossil fuels
and carbon emissions. Traditional hydrogen is produced from fossil fuels (SMR)
hundreds of miles away, liquefied for transport, and then driven by trucks to
its final destination, all at enormous environmental and financial costs. We
believe that's wildly inefficient and unsustainable. It's time we produced clean
hydroge

Version 2.0 · February 2026

Non-Confidential

T OBE ENERGY · PUBLIC MASTER PL AN

02

moves hydrogen - and by doing so, help usher in a future free of fossil fuels and carbon emissions.
Traditional hydrogen is produced from fossil fuels (SMR) hundreds of miles away, liquefied for
transport, and then driven by trucks to its final destination, all at enormous environmental and
financial costs. We believe that's wildly inefficient and unsustainable. It's time we produced clean
hydrogen locally, at ultra-high efficiency, using low-cost electricity and water. But how do we get
there? Our plan is straightforward and unfolds in three phases: Phase I: Build Close-Proximity Green
Hydrogen Plants Goal: Undercut costly, emission-heavy delivered hydrogen by producing it in regional
facilities near industrial users. Today, much of the hydrogen used in places like Tulsa, Oklahoma comes
from Houston, where it's manufactured with fossil fuels, then liquefied and trucked hundreds of miles.
This process is expensive, energy-intensive, and carbon-heavy. Our approach is to build smaller-scale
green hydrogen plants right where the demand is - close enough that transportation and liquefaction
costs basically vanish. Thanks to our 90+% efficient electrolysis technology, we can thrive anywhere
electricity costs are under $0.13/kWh (where we achieve cost parity with SMR hydrogen). The lower
the price of electricity, the stronger our advantage. And by avoiding the complexities of liquefaction
and helium refrigeration, we reduce both cost and carbon footprints. In short, our plants deliver clean
hydrogen at a lower price than SMR - without any carbon emissions. Phase I starts us off by capturing
local markets, proving that green hydrogen can beat fossil-based hydrogen on both cost and
convenience, right now. After Oklahoma, we expand to other markets with high hydrogen usage, and
also a high hydrogen cost, low electricity cost, and a large amount of renewable energy on their
electrical grid. Think regions like the Pacific Northwest, the Great Plains, and the Desert Southwest.
Phase II: Replace Existing SMR with Electrolysis Goal: Convert industrial hydrogen producers - currently using
Steam Methane Reforming - to a greener, more efficient method. Once we've demonstrated that local green
hydrogen makes economic sense, the next step is bigger: helping major hydrogen producers (the ones behind oil
refineries, petrochemical plants, steelmakers, and more) replace their aging SMR units with Tobe Energy Corp.
electrolyzers. Phase II moves us from hydrogen sales to equipment sales. Why would these big operators ditch
their tried-and-true natural gas methods? Because the world is shifting rapidly toward decarbonization, regulators
are applying more pressure, carbon pricing is making fossil-based production increasingly expensive, and our
electrolyzers make hydrogen just as cheap as SMR hydrogen. With our plug-and-play, modular electrolyzer
systems, these industries can phase out SMR gradually - starting small, scaling up, and seeing immediate cuts in
CO₂. Over time, entire SMR plants can be swapped out for a combination of electrolyzers. The result? Less
dependence on natural gas, a smaller environmental footprint, and newfound eligibility for clean energy
incentives or premium markets that demand truly "green" hydrogen. Phase II is about taking this technology from
regional hubs to the industrial heartland, tackling the biggest sources of hydrogen-related emissions and turning
them into zero-carbon powerhouses. Phase III: Integrate Hydrogen with Renewables for 24/7
Clean Energy
Goal: Use hydrogen as a powerful storage medium for solar and wind, eliminating intermittency and enabling a
resilient, zero-carbon grid. The final piece of the puzzle is also the most exciting: linking large-scale renewables
with hydrogen storage. Our vision is a world where solar farms, wind turbines, and other clean energy sources
feed any surplus electricity into Tobe Energy Corp. electrolyzers. That hydrogen is stored for hours, days, or even
months - only being converted back to electricity when the sun isn't shining or the wind isn't blowing. In this way,
hydrogen becomes the battery for the grid: infinitely scalable, longer-lasting and cheaper than chemical batteries,
and perfectly suited to smooth out renewable intermittency. By integrating with renewables, we achieve a true
24/7 clean-energy cycle. Excess power from wind or sun never goes to waste. Communities and industries get
round-the-clock access to zero- carbon electricity. And for critical sites - hospitals, data centers, remote facilities hydrogen backup can replace diesel generators entirely. Phase III makes hydrogen the linchpin of a fully
renewable energy system, driving us ever closer to a future without fossil fuels.

Putting It All Together
Our three-phase plan may sound ambitious, but it's grounded in real economics and real technology:

1. BUILD CLOSE-PROXIMITY GREEN HYDROGEN PLANTS TO OUT-COMPETE
DELIVERED FOSSIL HYDROGEN
in targeted markets.

2. REPLACE SMR AT LARGE INDUSTRIAL SITES, CUTTING CO₂ AND ALIGNING
WITH GLOBAL
decarbonization trends.

3. INTEGRATE WITH RENEWABLES TO CREATE 24/7 CLEAN ENERGY SYSTEMS AND
PUSH THE WORLD
closer to net zero. At every step, our high-efficiency, patent-pending electrolyzers are the key. They allow us to
tap into relatively cheap electricity (under $0.13/kWh) and convert it into hydrogen at cost levels that can beat
SMR - without the existential risk of carbon emissions. We believe energy should be cleaner, cheaper, and
produced closer to where it's used. We're building that future right now. The end goal? A world in which green
hydrogen is as commonplace - and invisible - as electricity is today. Each phase of our plan helps us realize that
vision, while fueling growth, innovation, and real-world impact. We invite you to join Tobe Energy Corp. on this
journey. By executing this plan, we won't just transform the hydrogen supply chain - we'll transform entire
industries, accelerate the clean energy transition, and do our part to preserve the planet for future generations.
Let's get there together.
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

NSF IdeaLaunch | 2025

BUSINESS THESIS
Who are your customers:

Utilities interested in using hydrogen as an energy storage medium, combustion companies searching for a natural gas
replacement, hospitals seeking pure H2 for diagnostics, and heavy industries seeking hydrogen as a chemical feedstock

What is your product or service:
Modular hydrogen electrolyzers and compressed green hydrogen

Why will your customers buy:
To achieve emissions targets by divesting from SMR hydrogen, realize significant financial savings by transitioning from legacy
electrolysis methods, improve supply chain infrastructure by installing modular, on-site green hydrogen production facilities

We’ve pioneered a groundbreaking innovation in Hydrogen Electrolysis that is 45% more
efficient and 75% cheaper than legacy electrolysis methods. Our product validates longstanding theories on whether Green Hydrogen can achieve cost parity with SMR-derived grey/
blue hydrogen, and our customers, from data center operators to steel and ammonia producers
to combustion specialists, will reap the benefits of reducing costs, emissions, and supply chain
headaches.
www.tobe.energy

Our Modular Electrolyzer and Compressed Green Hydrogen solutions

And significantly reducing delivered green hydrogen expenses

NSF IdeaLaunch | 2025

WHAT DID WE LEARN?
Hypothesis: If our value proposition offers cost savings, reliable functionality, supply chain stability, and operations
decarbonization, more decision-makers and operations personnel would be open to adopting hydrogen solutions
or switching suppliers.
Experiments: Interviewed candidates from multiple sectors/positions to gauge if various customer segmentations
would prioritize or show interest in similar or different aspects of our value propositions.
Results: We validated our hypothesis and then some. All interviewees were either excited or interested in learning
more about our product & service offerings, even if they had never considered hydrogen in the past. We took note
that separate segmentations do prioritize different aspects, e.g., some customers valued our modularity and
reliability and were not as price-sensitive, while others were only concerned with price. However, all interviewees
valued decarbonization and consider it a net positive. Additionally, our interviews introduced us to hydrogen
applications we had not initially considered, which widened our segmentation pool and added elements to our
business model canvas.
Iterate: As we transition into customer prospecting and sales, we’ll leverage insights from these interviews to be
highly targeted in our marketing and sales campaigns. These interviews have painted a detailed portrait of the realworld problems hydrogen users consider before making decisions. We’re better equipped to approach future
conversations with highly tailored questions, a clearer understanding of business contexts, and a solutions
playbook that addresses complex and specific user needs.
www.tobe.energy
