Version 2.0 · February 2026

Non-Confidential

T OBE ENERGY · HYDROGEN USE IN NORTH AMERIC A

02

Hydrogen (H₂) is a versatile energy carrier and industrial feedstock with a significant, albeit often
unseen, presence in the North American economy. This document outlines the primary current
applications of hydrogen, categorized by its role as an industrial feedstock, a fuel, an inert or carrier
gas, and in specialized analytical and technical functions. It details major consuming industries such as
oil refining, ammonia production, and methanol synthesis, alongside uses in transportation, power
generation, metallurgy, semiconductor manufacturing, and laboratories. Furthermore, it explores the
current hydrogen production landscape, dominated by steam methane reforming, and discusses the
emerging applications poised to expand hydrogen's role, particularly in decarbonization efforts across
heavy industry, energy storage, and transportation sectors.

1. INTRODUCTION
Hydrogen is the simplest and most abundant element in the universe, and while it rarely exists in its pure
molecular form (H₂) naturally on Earth, it plays a crucial role in numerous industrial processes and is increasingly
viewed as a key component of a future low-carbon energy system. In North America, hydrogen is already integral
to several established industries. This document provides an overview of its current applications, examines the
production landscape, and highlights emerging uses that signal a potentially transformative shift in hydrogen's
economic and environmental significance.

2. HYDROGEN AS AN INDUSTRIAL FEEDSTOCK
Hydrogen is widely utilized as a reactant in key North American industries to produce essential chemicals and
fuels.
hydrogen with atmospheric nitrogen to synthesize ammonia (NH₃), is the single largest global consumer of
hydrogen, accounting for over 30% of worldwide demand [1]. In North America, fertilizer plants employ this
process extensively, using vast quantities of hydrogen to manufacture ammonia, the primary component of
nitrogen-based fertilizers essential for modern agriculture.
representing approximately half of the U.S. hydrogen demand [2, Internal Ref. Note 1]. Hydrogen is critical in
processes such as hydrotreating (to remove impurities like sulfur) and hydrocracking (to break down heavy
hydrocarbons), enabling the conversion of crude oil into valuable transportation fuels like gasoline and diesel.
• 2.1 Ammonia Production (Fertilizers): The Haber-Bosch process, which combines
• 2.2 Oil Refining: Petroleum refineries are another dominant hydrogen consumer,

carbon monoxide or carbon dioxide. This chemical pathway relies heavily on hydrogen feedstock and constitutes a
significant portion of industrial hydrogen consumption [1]. North American methanol plants, producing this
versatile chemical for applications in plastics, solvents, and other derivatives, are therefore considerable
hydrogen users.
of organic compounds. For instance, the food industry hydrogenates vegetable oils (adding hydrogen to
unsaturated fats) to produce margarine and shortening. Chemical manufacturers employ hydrogenation to
convert hydrocarbons (e.g., benzene to cyclohexane) and other materials, treating hydrogen as a feedstock to
alter chemical bonds and create desired products.
• 2.3 Methanol Production: Methanol (CH₃OH) is synthesized by reacting hydrogen with
• 2.4 Hydrogenation Processes: Numerous industries utilize hydrogen for the hydrogenation

3. HYDROGEN AS A FUEL

03

Hydrogen can also be consumed for its energy content, either through combustion or electrochemical reaction in
fuel cells.
generate electricity onboard. North America has a growing fleet of hydrogen fuel cell electric vehicles (FCEVs),
with over 7,600 FCEV cars in the U.S. and numerous fuel cell buses, complemented by over 25,000 fuel cell
forklifts operating in warehouses [3]. These vehicles emit only water vapor. Hydrogen is also under investigation
for heavy-duty trucks, locomotives, and maritime applications.
turbines. Several North American facilities utilize large fuel cell units for applications like backup power or
distributed generation. Furthermore, utilities are advancing hydrogen- capable gas turbines; a notable example is
an 840 MW power plant in Utah, planned to initially operate on a 30% hydrogen/70% natural gas blend, with
ambitions to transition to 100% hydrogen [4]. The use of hydrogen in turbines and engines is considered a
promising method for storing and utilizing renewable energy [1].
heat. Petroleum refineries, for instance, often burn hydrogen-rich by-product streams in furnaces and boilers [2].
Similarly, off-gases from chemical processes like chlorine production or catalytic reforming are used as fuel in
chemical plants. Pilot projects are also exploring the use of hydrogen in high-temperature industrial furnaces (e.g.,
steel reheating, cement kilns, glass manufacturing) to displace natural gas and reduce CO₂ emissions.

4. HYDROGEN AS AN INERT OR CARRIER GAS
In specific applications, hydrogen is valued for its chemical properties (e.g., reducing environment, low density)
rather than its energy content or role as a primary reactant.
processes to prevent oxidation and achieve desired material properties. Bright annealing of
• 4.1 Metallurgy and Materials Processing: Hydrogen is used in metal heat-treating

stainless steel, for example, often occurs in a hydrogen or hydrogen-nitrogen (forming gas) atmosphere to
maintain a clean, oxide-free metal surface [5]. Brazing, particularly of copper and silver, may also employ a pure
hydrogen atmosphere to ensure an oxygen-free environment, facilitating high-quality joints.
hydrogen as a carrier and process gas in integrated circuit fabrication. In silicon wafer processing and epitaxy,
hydrogen transports doping gases and creates a protective reducing atmosphere [6]. Its low density, high thermal
conductivity, and oxygen-scavenging capabilities make it ideal for processes demanding extremely clean and
controlled environments [6].
gases in modified atmosphere packaging (MAP), hydrogen serves a niche role as a tracer gas for leak detection. A
small percentage of hydrogen (e.g., 5% H₂ in N₂) can be included in the package atmosphere, allowing sensitive
sensors to detect escaping hydrogen and identify micro-leaks, thus ensuring packaging integrity without exposing
food to harmful substances [7].

5. HYDROGEN IN CALIBRATION AND ANALYTICAL USES
Hydrogen's unique properties make it well-established in North American laboratories and analytical
instrumentation.

rely on FIDs with hydrogen flames to measure total hydrocarbons in exhaust streams [9].

analytical instruments.

7. CURRENT HYDROGEN MARKET AND PRODUCTION IN NORTH AMERICA
The North American hydrogen market is dominated by a few key industrial sectors: o Oil Refining: Approximately
65-70% of hydrogen demand. o Ammonia Production: Roughly 20-25%. o Methanol Production: Around 5-7%. o
Other Industrial Chemical Uses (e.g., hydrogenation): Collectively about 2-3%. o Specialty Uses (Steel & Metals,
Electronics, Food, Analytical, Transportation, Power, Aerospace, etc.): Each of these sectors individually accounts
for less than 1% of current demand, though they are critical for their respective operations.
o Steam Methane Reforming (SMR): This is by far the predominant production method, accounting for
approximately 90% or more of hydrogen in North America. Methane, primarily from natural gas, is reacted with
steam at high temperatures to produce hydrogen and carbon dioxide (CO₂). o Partial Oxidation (POX) or
Gasification: Utilized in some refineries (gasifying heavy residual oils) and chemical plants, this method is less
common than SMR but remains a notable source. o By-product Hydrogen: Some industrial processes, such as
chlor-alkali production, generate hydrogen as a by-product, which can be used on-site or sold. o Merchant vs. Onsite Production: Large consumers like refineries and ammonia/methanol plants typically produce hydrogen on-site.
Merchant hydrogen is produced by industrial gas companies at centralized facilities or via pipeline networks and
delivered to a diverse range of end-users.
• 7.1 Market Share Overview:
• 7.2 Primary Production Methods:

Presently, a very small fraction of North American hydrogen is produced via electrolysis (using renewable or grid
electricity, termed "green" or "pink/yellow/purple" hydrogen, respectively) or SMR equipped with carbon capture,
utilization, and storage (CCUS, termed "blue" hydrogen). The vast majority is "gray" hydrogen, derived from SMR
without CO₂ abatement. However, policy initiatives, such as the U.S. Inflation Reduction Act's hydrogen production
tax credits, are designed to accelerate the growth of low-carbon hydrogen production.
• 7.3 Current Low-Carbon Hydrogen Share:

Driven by decarbonization goals, a range of new applications for hydrogen are being explored, leveraging its
potential as a clean energy carrier.

reduce iron ore, significantly lowering CO₂ emissions compared to traditional coke-based methods [14]. The
cement and concrete industry is also testing hydrogen as a supplemental fuel in kilns [14].

H₂) and later converted back to electricity via fuel cells or turbines [4]. Blending hydrogen (5-20%) into natural
gas pipelines is also being explored [1].
a zero-carbon fuel through both fuel cells and direct combustion. In 2023, a significant milestone was achieved
with the first successful flight of a 40-passenger regional airliner retrofitted with a hydrogen fuel cell powertrain
[15]. Major aircraft manufacturers are also developing hydrogen combustion concepts for future aircraft.
cells offer advantages like fast refueling and long range. Pilot projects in North America are deploying fuel cell
semi-trucks, locomotives, and mining vehicles.
being explored for maritime shipping. Additionally, hydrogen fuel cells are increasingly being installed for backup
power at data centers, telecommunication towers, and hospitals as cleaner alternatives to diesel generators.
• 8.1 Decarbonizing Heavy Industry: Hydrogen is being trialed as an alternative to coal and
• 8.2 Advanced Energy Storage: Hydrogen is increasingly viewed as a viable solution for
• 8.3 Hydrogen-Powered Aviation: The aviation industry is experimenting with hydrogen as
• 8.4 Zero-Emission Heavy Transport: For heavy-duty ground transportation, hydrogen fuel
• 8.5 Maritime and Backup Power: Hydrogen and hydrogen-derived fuels like ammonia are

9. CONCLUSION
Currently, hydrogen consumption in North America is overwhelmingly concentrated in industrial feedstock
applications, particularly oil refining and ammonia production, with most of this hydrogen
generated from natural gas via SMR. While specialized uses in metallurgy, electronics, and analytics are smaller in
volume, they are vital to their respective fields. The landscape of hydrogen use is poised for significant evolution.
Emerging applications in heavy industry decarbonization, large-scale energy storage, and zero-emission
transportation (including aviation, heavy trucking, and maritime) are gaining traction. If these innovations,
currently at pilot or early commercial stages, are successfully scaled, supported by growth in low-carbon hydrogen
production (green and blue hydrogen), North America's hydrogen demand and infrastructure could expand
dramatically. This transition would see hydrogen evolve from a specialized industrial input into a more
mainstream energy resource, playing a pivotal role in achieving deeper decarbonization across multiple sectors of
the economy in the coming decades.

H2 UTILIZATION SURVEY
OKC & Tulsa Metro Area
Introduction: Hydrogen plays a vital, albeit often unseen, role in a diverse
array of industries and research activities across Oklahoma's major

Introduction: Hydrogen plays a vital, albeit often unseen, role in a diverse array of industries and
research activities across Oklahoma's major metropolitan areas. Companies in manufacturing, energy,
and technology, alongside prominent research institutions, hospitals, and universities in the Oklahoma
City and Tulsa regions, routinely purchase hydrogen from industrial gas suppliers. This document
provides an overview of these entities, detailing their sectors, contact information, and specific ways
in which hydrogen is integrated into their daily operational workflows.

critical process for aerospace and industrial components. (Source: bodycote.com)

efficient electrolysis technology in U.S. regions characterized by low electricity costs and exceptionally
high prices for delivered hydrogen. This document outlines a phased strategic expansion plan,
commencing with an initial flagship facility in Oklahoma City, followed by replication in Tulsa, OK, and
subsequent entries into high-potential markets including Washington, New Mexico, South Dakota, and
Iowa. The plan leverages a modular facility design, enabling rapid deployment and scalability,
targeting industrial, energy, healthcare, and research sectors currently reliant on expensive (often
$20-25+/kg), logistically constrained hydrogen supplies. This approach positions Tobe Energy for
accelerated growth, market leadership, and offers a compelling investment opportunity in the
burgeoning green hydrogen economy.

1. INTRODUCTION: THE TOBE ENERGY VALUE PROPOSITION
Tobe Energy has developed an advanced, ultra-efficient electrolysis process capable of producing green hydrogen
at costs significantly below current market rates for delivered hydrogen, particularly in regions underserved by
existing production infrastructure. Our mission is to make green hydrogen economically competitive, thereby
accelerating the energy transition. This market expansion plan details a pragmatic, phased approach to
establishing a network of decentralized hydrogen production facilities across strategically selected U.S. markets
where incumbent delivered hydrogen costs are exceedingly high.

2. PHASE 1: INITIAL MARKET ENTRY & VALIDATION - OKLAHOMA CITY,
OK (Year 1)
due to a compelling confluence of factors: o Low-Cost Electricity: Industrial electricity rates average approximately
6.3¢/kWh. o Significant Renewable Penetration: Approximately 35% of Oklahoma's electricity is wind-generated,
offering a pathway to green hydrogen. o Incumbent High Hydrogen Prices: Local industries, particularly smaller
volume or specialized users, currently pay premium prices, often in the range of $20-25+/kg, for hydrogen
trucked in from distant production hubs or supplied in cylinders.
with established hydrogen demand:
• Strategic Rationale: Oklahoma City (OKC) has been selected as the optimal launch market
• Target Customer Segments: The initial OKC facility will serve a diversified customer base

o Industrial/Manufacturing: Bodycote Thermal Processing (metal heat treatment). o Energy Technology & Utilities:
Baker Hughes Energy Innovation Center (R&D for hydrogen-blended turbines), Oklahoma Gas & Electric (OG&E)
(generator cooling). o Healthcare & Research: Oklahoma Medical Research Foundation (OMRF), OU Health, Integris
Baptist Medical Center, Mercy Hospital (analytical instrumentation, medical research).
o Validate Tobe Energy's production cost model and operational efficiency at commercial scale. o Secure anchor
offtake agreements, demonstrating market demand and de-risking further investment by offering substantial
savings against current delivered prices. o Establish a replicable operational blueprint for subsequent facilities.

T OBE ENERGY · INVESTORS GUIDE TO 4 5 V

02

The Section 45V Clean Hydrogen Production Tax Credit (PTC), a cornerstone of the Inflation Reduction
Act (IRA), offers a powerful, decade-long incentive of up to $3.00 per kilogram, fundamentally
reshaping the economics of U.S. hydrogen projects. Final regulations from the U.S. Treasury and
Department of Energy have now provided a clear and robust framework for implementation, de-risking
key aspects of project development. However, a significant legislative challenge has emerged. A
House-passed bill in mid-2025 proposes to accelerate the "begin construction" deadline for 45V
eligibility from December 31, 2027, to December 31, 2025. While its passage in the Senate remains
uncertain, this development has introduced a critical timing risk for the entire project pipeline. This
note provides an analysis of the 45V credit mechanism, the current regulatory landscape, and the
strategic implications of the legislative headwind. Our key takeaways for investors are:
Investment Tax Credit) makes the U.S. one of the most attractive markets globally for clean hydrogen investment.
verification, and the use of Energy Attribute Certificates (EACs) provide a stable operating framework. The "GREET
model safe harbor" is a crucial feature, locking in emissions pathways for a project's entire 10-year credit life.
after 2025 creates immense urgency. Developers and investors must now operate under the assumption that this
accelerated deadline could become law.
ensure projects "begin construction" before December 31, 2025, by either initiating significant physical work or,
more commonly, by incurring at least 5% of the total project cost under binding written contracts. Doing so would
grandfather the project's eligibility for the 45V credit, irrespective of the legislative outcome.
• The Opportunity is Unprecedented: The 45V credit (as a PTC or an elective 30%
• Regulatory Clarity is Achieved: Final IRS rules on emissions modeling (H₂-GREET),
• A Critical Deadline Looms: The proposal to sunset the credit for new projects starting
• Strategic Imperative is to "Safe Harbor": The primary risk mitigation strategy is to

1. THE 45V CREDIT: A GENERATIONAL INCENTIVE
Section 45V of the IRA provides a technology-neutral tax credit for the production of qualified clean hydrogen.
Project owners can choose one of two incentive structures:
volume of hydrogen produced and its lifecycle carbon intensity. The maximum credit is $3.00/kg (inflationadjusted) for projects meeting Prevailing Wage and Apprenticeship (PWA) labor standards.
• Production Tax Credit (PTC - §45V): A 10-year, per-kilogram credit based on the

percentage of the facility's eligible capital investment. The maximum credit is 30% of the project cost for facilities
designed to produce the cleanest hydrogen and meeting PWA standards. The choice is irrevocable and strategic:
the PTC favors projects with high production volumes and lower capital intensity, while the ITC provides significant
upfront capital relief, benefiting capital-intensive projects and simplifying tax equity financing.
• Investment Tax Credit (ITC - §48): A one-time, upfront credit calculated as a
© 2026 Tobe Energy Corp.

tobe.energy

T OBE ENERGY · INVESTORS GUIDE TO 4 5 V

03

2. CREDIT TIERS AND ECONOMIC IMPACT
The credit value is tiered based on lifecycle greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions, measured on a "well-to-gate" basis
using the DOE's 45VH2-GREET model. To qualify, a project's emissions must be below 4.0 kg CO₂e per kg of H₂.
Table 1: Section 45V Credit Tiers (Assuming PWA Requirements are Met) Tier Lifecycle Emissions (kg CO₂e per kg
H₂) PTC (Full Rate) ITC (Full Rate) 1 < 0.45 kg $3.00 / kg 30% 2 0.45 - 1.5 kg $1.00 / kg 10% 3 1.5 - 2.5 kg $0.75 /
kg 7.5% 4 2.5 - 4.0 kg $0.60 / kg 6%
> **Note: Base rates are 20% of the full rate if PWA standards are not met. All PTC values are**
indexed to inflation. The 5x multiplier for meeting PWA requirements makes compliance a near-universal goal for
economically viable projects.

3. THE CRITICAL DEADLINE: NAVIGATING LEGISLATIVE HEADWINDS AND THE
2025 SAFE HARBOR
While current law provides a generous runway, recent legislative action has become the single most important
variable for near-term project planning.
to December 31, 2025. The Senate's position is the pivotal factor, with significant industry lobbying to preserve
the original timeline. However, investors cannot afford to wait for political resolution. Prudent strategy dictates
action before year-end 2025. The Safe Harbor Solution: To mitigate this legislative risk, projects can be
grandfathered under current law by establishing that they have "begun construction." The IRS provides two wellestablished methods:
• Current Law: A qualifying facility must begin construction by December 31, 2027.
• Proposed Legislation (House-Passed Bill): This deadline would be accelerated

1. PHYSICAL WORK TEST: INITIATING PHYSICAL WORK OF A SIGNIFICANT
NATURE, EITHER ON-SITE (E.G.,
pouring foundations) or off-site for custom-ordered project components under a binding contract. 2. 5% Safe
Harbor: Incurring at least 5% of the total project's capital cost. This is the more common path for large projects
and can be satisfied through milestone payments for long-lead equipment (e.g., electrolyzers, reformers) under
binding written agreements. Once construction has begun, projects are typically afforded a four-year window
(e.g., by Dec. 31, 2029 for a 2025 start) to be placed in service to satisfy continuity requirements automatically.

1. Executive Summary
Tobe Energy Corp has developed a membrane-free, catalyst-free hydrogen
electrolyzer that achieves 70–72% LHV wall-to-plug efficiency across all tested
conditions — competitive with or exceeding PEM electrolysis at a fraction of the capital
cost. Measured performance data from 22,103 data points across four test
configurations establishes a system-level specific energy consumption of 46.08 kWh/
kg at the electrolyzer outlet, with a demonstrated stack-level SEC of 42.2 kWh/kg
using optimized power electronics.

Headline LCOH Results (at Electrolyzer Outlet)
All three scenarios fall below the DOE/NREL PEM electrolysis benchmark of $4–7/kg
(unsubsidized).
45V Production Tax Credit impact (scenario-dependent):

The On-Site Advantage
The comparison that matters to customers is delivered cost, not production cost. DOE
data shows 75–85% of delivered hydrogen cost is transport and storage:
Tobe produces on-site, eliminates delivery entirely for low-pressure industrial
customers, and sells at $15–30/kg with 81–91% gross margins validated by real
contracts and LOIs across seven industry sectors.

Key Differentiators
Wall-to-plug efficiency: All reported efficiencies are true AC-in → H₂-out
measurements. Most competitors report only stack efficiency (DC → H₂), which is
10–15% higher. Tobe's 70–72% LHV wall-to-plug exceeds typical PEM wall-to-plug of
57–67% LHV.
No membrane: Eliminates Nafion ($500–800/m²) and associated degradation-driven
replacement cycles.
No rare earth catalysts: Uses stainless steel construction. No iridium/platinum
($1,000–3,000/kW for PEM precious metals eliminated).
No cooling system: Operates at 27–28°C near ambient. Eliminates heat exchangers,
pumps, and coolant loops.
TDS tolerance: Operates across 25–10,000+ ppm TDS, enabling standard RO instead
of expensive deionization.

with published benchmarks. Delivered-cost adders by end-use application are shown
separately in Section 6.4.

3. Technical Model
3.1 Measured Performance Data
All efficiency data derives from laboratory testing of the 6 kW and 15 kW prototype
electrolyzers, documented in federal grant submission (Navy SBIR N242-070) and
internal test records.
Source: ENG_EfficiencyCalculations.xlsx;
ENG_PreCommit_SampleOperatingData.xlsx

Four Test Configurations
\ 15 kW High TDS test ended early (2 hours) due to diode failure. Daily rate
extrapolation not meaningful. SEC derived from measured LHV efficiency (33.33 ÷
0.6915). Efficiency calculated over actual test duration.*
Source: ENG_EfficiencyCalculations.xlsx; ENG_GRANT_NavySBIR_N242-070.pdf
Critical context:

These are true wall-to-plug efficiencies (AC mains → H₂ gas out). The test setup
measures AC power input at the wall outlet and H₂ mass output, capturing all
conversion losses including the DC bench power supply. Most electrolyzer
manufacturers report stack-only efficiency (DC → H₂), which omits power electronics
losses of 10–15%. Tobe's reported numbers are directly comparable to the DOE/NREL
"system" efficiency metric.
Tests used a standard DC bench power supply, not an optimized power topology. The
bench supply introduces additional rectification losses. Tobe's production units use a
resonant LLC power topology that has demonstrated 94% HHV efficiency in controlled
testing. The measured bench results represent a conservative floor, not optimistic
projections.

Operating Data Summary (6 kW Low TDS Run)
The primary test dataset contains 22,103 data points at 5-second intervals from the 6
kW Low TDS run (August 18, 2024).
Source: ENG_PreCommit_SampleOperatingData.xlsx
The data shows remarkable operational stability: stack temperature holds within ±1°C
of ambient across the entire test run, with no evidence of transient oscillations, thermal
drift, or performance degradation over 30+ hours of logged data.

3.2 Heat and Material Balance
Source: ENG_HMB_HeatAndMaterialBalance.xlsx;
NOTES_heat_material_balance.md

Single T-25 Unit (25 kg/day Design Basis)
Energy inputs:
Electrolyzer stack: 1,154 kWh/day (48.1 kW continuous)
Booster pump: 2 kWh/day (negligible)
Total: 1,156 kWh/day → 46.2 kWh/kg H₂
Water consumption: 15.6 kg city water → 9.4 kg RO permeate per 1.042 kg/hr H₂ = 9.0
kg water per kg H₂ (vs. PEM requiring 9–10 kg ultra-pure DI water per kg H₂)

3.2.1 Efficiency Boundary Clarification
The T-25 system has two distinct efficiency levels that must be clearly distinguished:

Stack SEC (42.2 kWh/kg): Energy consumed by the electrolyzer cells alone, validated
by the Zeeco PFD design basis (TOBE-PFD-001) and the Pulse Holdings equipment
quote. This is the electrolyzer-only efficiency — the metric most PEM manufacturers
report.
System SEC (46.0–46.5 kWh/kg): Total AC energy from service entrance to hydrogen
at the outlet flange, including all BOP parasitic loads: RO/DI water treatment pumps,
gas separation and purification, dual-bed dryer train, industrial PLC/HMI controls,
safety interlock systems, and enclosure HVAC. This is the true wall-to-plug metric used
throughout this TEA.
The ~4 kWh/kg difference between stack and system SEC represents the BOP
parasitic load. This is confirmed by two independent measurements:
Bench testing: Measured 46.08 kWh/kg at the AC wall outlet with all conversion losses
captured (ENG_PreCommit_SampleOperatingData.xlsx)
Heat & material balance: 1,154 kWh/day ÷ 25 kg/day = 46.2 kWh/kg (ENG_HMB)
The base case SEC of 46.5 kWh/kg used in this TEA is a system-level number,
deliberately set slightly above the best measured system result (46.08 kWh/kg) to
provide conservatism. It should be compared to other TEAs' system-level SEC values
(e.g., DOE PEM benchmark of ~57.5 kWh/kg system-level), not to stack-only metrics.

Multi-Unit Deployment (Zeeco Phase 1: 12× T-25)
The H&MB file contains a 15-unit sheet; however, Zeeco Phase 1 was revised to 12
units per the FEED package. At 12 units:

Tobe equipment cost ($500/kW) is 37–63% below PEM equipment-only cost
Tobe installed cost ($1,000/kW) is below PEM uninstalled (equipment) cost

5. OPEX Model
5.1 Electricity — The Dominant Cost Driver
Electricity represents ~67% of total LCOH at base case, consistent with the 55–70%
range documented across nine TEA exemplars in the deep research report.
Electricity price basis: Tobe facilities qualify for the PSO (Public Service Company of
Oklahoma) large-power industrial tariff at $0.04/kWh. PSO also publishes a renewable
incentive rate of $0.035/kWh, used in the favorable/upside scenario. For reference, the
EIA 2024 Oklahoma industrial average is $0.0584/kWh; Tobe's tariff is below average
due to large-power classification and direct industrial service.
For sensitivity, electricity price is modeled from $0.025/kWh (favorable PPA) to $0.055/
kWh (approaching EIA state average).

5.2 Fixed O&M
3% of CAPEX is at the low end of the 2–5% benchmark range, justified by Tobe's
simplified BOP (no cooling system maintenance, no membrane monitoring, no catalyst
management).

5.3 Water
5.4 Stack Replacement and Degradation
Tobe's membrane-free architecture fundamentally changes the stack replacement
calculus:

Modeled assumption (conservative):
Replacement interval: 80,000 operating hours
At 85% capacity factor: ~10.7 years between replacements
Replacement cost: 10% of installed CAPEX ($5,000 per T-25)
Annualized replacement provision: $0.06/kg H₂
Over 20-year economic life: approximately 1 replacement event
This is conservative because:
Stainless steel electrodes do not suffer the chemical degradation that limits PEM
membrane life
No rare earth catalyst depletion or poisoning occurs
The system operates at near-ambient temperature (27°C), reducing thermal stress on
all components
Electrode fouling can be addressed through cleaning rather than full replacement

Degradation Model
A simple linear degradation model is applied following the European Hydrogen
Observatory LCOH calculator methodology:
At base case SEC of 46.5 kWh/kg, the life-average SEC is 46.5 × 1.02 = 47.43 kWh/
kg. The $0.04/kg degradation impact is noted but not incorporated into the headline
LCOH to maintain comparability with benchmark TEAs that typically report BOL SEC.
The all-in LCOH including degradation is $2.83/kg.
Long-duration degradation testing will commence with the Zeeco ARC deployment in
Q4 2026 — Tobe's first commercial installation. Until validated lifetime data is available
from sustained commercial operation, the 80,000-hour stack replacement interval and
0.5%/10,000-hour degradation rate are conservative engineering estimates based on
the known degradation characteristics of stainless steel electrodes in near-ambient
electrochemical environments. These assumptions will be updated as operational data
accumulates.

7. Financial Model / DCF
7.1 LCOH Calculation Methodology
LCOH is calculated using the Capital Recovery Factor (CRF) method, following the
approach recommended by Agora Energiewende and the European Hydrogen
Observatory LCOH Calculator:
LCOH = (CAPEX_ann + OPEX_fix + C_replacement + C_electricity + C_water) /
H₂_annual
Where:
CAPEX_ann = Installed_CAPEX × CRF
CRF = r(1+r)^n / ((1+r)^n - 1)
H₂_annual = Rated_output × 365 × Capacity_factor

7.2 Base Case LCOH Calculation (Single T-25 Unit)
Step-by-step calculation:

LCOH Cost Breakdown (Base Case)
Figure 3: LCOH Cost Breakdown (Base Case)
Electricity dominates at 67% of LCOH, consistent with the 55–70% benchmark from
the deep research report. Including the degradation adjustment (+$0.04/kg, Section
5.4), the all-in LCOH is $2.83/kg.

7.3 Three-Scenario LCOH Summary
All three scenarios remain well below the PEM electrolysis benchmark of $4–7/kg.

7.4 WACC Sensitivity
LCOH is relatively insensitive to WACC because CAPEX is only ~24% of total cost. A
4-percentage-point swing in WACC changes LCOH by only $0.19/kg.

7.5 45V Production Tax Credit Analysis
Tobe qualifies for the Section 45V Clean Hydrogen Production Tax Credit. Final rules
under 45VH2-GREET determine the credit tier based on lifecycle emissions. Three
scenarios are modeled reflecting compliance uncertainty:

45V Credit Scenarios
Temporal matching requirements: EAC-based claims require annual matching through
2029 and hourly matching starting 2030. Tobe's grid-connected model in Oklahoma
requires an explicit electricity attributes strategy (EACs or direct PPA) for Tier 1
qualification.
Compliance costs ($0.05–0.10/kg) include verification, reporting, EAC procurement,
and prevailing wage/apprenticeship administration. These are modeled as a direct
LCOH adder rather than absorbed into O&M.
The subsidy value per facility under the Full Tier 1 scenario (based on financial model
projections):
$27M per eligible facility over 10-year PTC window
4 eligible facilities identified (Tulsa, OKC, Seattle, Spokane)
Total 45V value: $108M cumulative
Source: Fin_FinancialModel_v4.3.0.xlsx; Tobe_TEA_Narrative_v1.md
Note: 45V PTC qualification requires compliance with prevailing wage and registered
apprenticeship requirements under IRA Section 45V(e). The begin-construction
deadline is currently 2027 under OBBBA, with a 10-year production credit window. The
negative effective LCOH under the Full Tier 1 scenario reflects the PTC's intended
policy outcome — incentivizing domestic clean hydrogen production — and should be
interpreted as a margin enhancement rather than a sustainable long-term production
cost.

7.6 Zeeco Phase 1 Deployment Economics (Worked
Example)
The Zeeco ARC deployment provides a concrete validation of the LCOH model against
a real contracted project with FEED-level engineering.
Source: TOBE FEED Package – Zeeco ARC; TOBE-PFD-001;
TEA_PIPELINE_NARRATIVE.md
This worked example validates that the unit-level LCOH model scales predictably to
multi-unit deployments, and that the contracted Zeeco price of $30/kg produces
exceptional margins even at the facility level.

7.7 DCF / Revenue Projections
The financial model projects seven-year revenue growth driven by facility deployment,
equipment sales scale-up, and the expanding customer pipeline:
Revenue mix shifts from H₂-production-dominant (61% in Year 1) to equipment-salesdominant (66% by Year 7), reflecting the transition from operated hydrogen-as-aservice deployments to a scaled equipment manufacturing business. Equipment
services revenue (recurring maintenance contracts at 75% attach rate) emerges as a
growing third stream from Year 2 onward.
Fundraising trajectory:
Exit valuation framework: $2.6–3.3B at 8–10× revenue multiple

8. Sensitivity Analysis
8.1 Tornado Chart — Single-Variable LCOH Sensitivity
Each variable is varied independently while holding all others at base case ($2.79/kg
at $0.04/kWh).
Figure 4: Single-Variable LCOH Sensitivity (Tornado Chart)
Key finding: Electricity price is the dominant sensitivity driver, consistent with the deep
research report's finding across all nine exemplar TEAs. A swing from $0.025 to
$0.055/kWh changes LCOH by $1.40/kg. Even at the most expensive electricity
modeled ($0.055/kWh), Tobe's LCOH ($3.49/kg) remains below the PEM benchmark
floor of $4/kg.

8.2 Multi-Variable Scenario Analysis
Tobe's LCOH exceeds PEM benchmarks only in an extreme worst-case scenario
combining the highest electricity price, lowest utilization, highest CAPEX, worst
efficiency, and highest cost of capital — a scenario that is unlikely given the signed
offtake pipeline and Oklahoma's industrial electricity rates.

8.3 Capacity Factor Sensitivity
The signed pipeline of 2,165 kg/day validates high utilization targets. However, LCOH
is moderately sensitive to capacity factor because fixed costs (CAPEX annuity, O&M)
are spread over fewer production kilograms at lower utilization:
Even at 30% capacity factor — a level typical of intermittent renewable-only systems
— Tobe's LCOH ($4.35/kg) remains within the PEM benchmark range. Gross margins
exceed 82% at all utilization levels when selling at $25/kg.

8.4 Hydrogen Selling Price Sensitivity
At all contracted/LOI price points, the T-25 unit pays back its installed capital ($50,000
at base case) in under 12 months.

9. Competitive Comparison
9.1 Production Cost Comparison
9.2 Wall-to-Plug Efficiency Comparison
This comparison requires careful framing. Most published PEM efficiencies are stack
efficiencies (DC → H₂), not wall-to-plug (AC → H₂). Tobe reports wall-to-plug
exclusively. See Section 3.2.1 for the stack vs. system SEC boundary clarification.
Source: ENG_EfficiencyCalculations.xlsx (Tobe); TOBE-PFD-001 (stack SEC); DOE
2024, NREL 2024, IRENA 2020 (PEM)
Tobe's system-level wall-to-plug efficiency of 70–72% LHV exceeds the typical PEM
wall-to-plug range of 57–67% LHV. Tobe's stack-level SEC of 42.2 kWh/kg is
comparable to the best PEM stack-only numbers — but Tobe achieves this with
stainless steel at a fraction of the cost.

9.3 Delivered Cost Comparison (The Comparison That
Matters)
For end customers, the relevant comparison is total cost of hydrogen at point of use,
not production cost:
Tobe's value proposition: customers pay $15–30/kg for on-site green hydrogen, which
is at or below their current delivered grey hydrogen cost, with zero CAPEX
commitment, zero operational burden, and the environmental benefit of emission-free
production.

9.4 Structural Advantages Summary

10. Conclusions
10.1 Tobe's measured efficiency already beats PEM
benchmarks
Across four test configurations spanning two power levels and two water quality
conditions, Tobe's electrolyzer delivers 70–72% LHV wall-to-plug efficiency (system
SEC of 46–48 kWh/kg; stack SEC of 42.2 kWh/kg). This exceeds the typical PEM wallto-plug range of 57–67% LHV, using conservative bench power supply conditions. The
demonstrated upside (94% HHV with resonant LLC topology) suggests significant
headroom for further improvement.
Source: ENG_EfficiencyCalculations.xlsx;
ENG_PreCommit_SampleOperatingData.xlsx; TOBE-PFD-001

10.2 On-site production eliminates 75–85% of delivered
hydrogen cost
DOE data and the Airgas market quote ($121/kg delivered) confirm that transport,
storage, and distribution dominate hydrogen cost for end users. Tobe's on-site model
eliminates these costs entirely for low-pressure industrial applications, allowing
production-cost LCOH of $2.79/kg to translate directly into customer value at $15–30/
kg selling prices.
Source: MKRT_CASE_CostAnalysis_OnSiteAdvantage.pdf;
TEA_PIPELINE_NARRATIVE.md

10.3 Real contracted pricing validates >81% gross
margins
Gross margins of 81–91% are calculated from real contracted prices ($30/kg Zeeco)
and signed LOIs ($15–25/kg across four customers) against a measured production
cost basis. The weighted average selling price across signed commitments is ~$19/kg.
Source: TEA_PIPELINE_NARRATIVE.md; Fin_FinancialModel_v4.3.0.xlsx

10.4 Technology advantages drive structural CAPEX
advantage
Equipment cost of $500/kW (Pulse Holdings quote) is 37–63% below PEM equipmentonly cost of $800–1,350/kW (NREL 2024). Fully installed at $1,000/kW, Tobe remains
below the PEM uninstalled cost. This advantage stems from elimination of the
membrane, rare earth catalysts, and active cooling system, combined with commodity
stainless steel construction.
Source: Pulse Holdings quote (Jan 2026); TOBE-FEED-PKG-001; NREL 2024 (deep
research report)

10.5 $100M+ pipeline across 7 sectors proves
horizontal market applicability
With 2,165 kg/day of signed capacity across industrial, manufacturing, academic,
mobility, e-fuels, transit, and EPC, Tobe has demonstrated that the on-site hydrogen
production model addresses a broad, horizontal market — not a single niche
application.
Source: TEA_PIPELINE_NARRATIVE.md

10.6 Investment Thesis
Tobe Energy offers investors:
Production economics that beat all current electrolysis benchmarks ($2.79/kg vs. $4–7/
kg PEM)
Gross margins of 81–91% validated by real commercial contracts
A $100M+ pipeline across seven industry sectors with geographic diversity

Technology differentiation that is structural (no membrane, no rare earths, nearambient operation) rather than incremental
Policy tailwinds via the 45V PTC (up to $108M cumulative value across four facilities
under Full Tier 1)
Capital efficiency with sub-12-month payback on deployed units

Definition
Balance of Plant — auxiliary systems (power electronics, water treatment, thermal
management, controls)

CF

Capacity Factor — fraction of rated output achieved annually

CRF

Capital Recovery Factor — annualization factor for CAPEX

FLH

Full Load Hours — equivalent hours at rated capacity per year

HHV

Higher Heating Value of hydrogen (39.41 kWh/kg = 142 MJ/kg)

LCOH

Levelized Cost of Hydrogen — annualized total cost per kg produced

LHV

Lower Heating Value of hydrogen (33.33 kWh/kg = 120 MJ/kg)

LLC

Inductor-Inductor-Capacitor — resonant power converter topology

PEM

Proton Exchange Membrane — conventional electrolysis technology

PTC

Production Tax Credit — Section 45V of the Inflation Reduction Act

RO

Reverse Osmosis — water purification technology

SEC

Specific Energy Consumption — kWh per kg H₂ produced

SMR

Steam Methane Reforming — conventional hydrogen production from natural gas

TDS

Total Dissolved Solids — measure of water impurity (ppm)

WACC

Weighted Average Cost of Capital — discount rate for DCF analysis

TOB E EN ERGY · TEC H N OLOGY OVERVIEW

02

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
Tobe Energy has developed a fundamentally different approach to water electrolysis that achieves >92%
electrical efficiency while eliminating the exotic materials, complex thermal management, and degradation
mechanisms that constrain conventional electrolyzer technologies.

94.7%

~75%

1,000+

80k+

ELECTRICAL EFFICIENCY (LHV)

CAPEX REDUCTION

HOURS TESTED

EST. STACK LIFE (HRS)

KEY DIFFERENTIATORS
ATTRIBUTE

CONVENTIONAL ELECTROLYZERS

TOBE APPROACH

Operating Temperature

60–80°C (PEM), 70–90°C (Alkaline)

Near ambient (<30°C)

Critical Materials

Platinum, Iridium, Nafion membranes

304 SS, commodity components

Electrolyte

Concentrated KOH (25-40%)

Dilute solution (pure water compatible)

Primary Failure Mode

Membrane degradation, catalyst poisoning

Standard industrial wear items

Thermal Management

Active cooling required

Minimal (isothermal operation)

THE CORE INSIGHT
Conventional electrolysis generates significant waste heat because it operates continuously at voltages well above
thermodynamic minimum. Tobe's pulsed approach works at lower effective overpotentials, converting electrical energy to
chemical energy with minimal thermal loss.

© 2026 Tobe Energy Inc.

tobe.energy

TOB E EN ERGY · TEC H N OLOGY OVERVIEW

03

THE PROBLEM
Green hydrogen remains expensive primarily because electrolysis is inefficient. Electricity accounts for 6080% of levelized hydrogen cost, yet conventional electrolyzers convert only 60-75% of input energy to
hydrogen.

WHY CONVENTIONAL SYSTEMS ARE STUCK
PEM Electrolyzers

Alkaline Electrolyzers

Achieve good current density but require platinum-group metals
and expensive proton-exchange membranes. Membrane
degradation limits lifetime. Iridium scarcity constrains scale-up—
global production cannot support terawatt-scale deployment.

Avoid PGMs but require concentrated caustic electrolyte (25-40%
KOH), creating safety, handling, and corrosion challenges. Limited
turndown capability makes them poorly suited for variable
renewable power.

THE THERMAL PENALTY
Both technologies operate at elevated temperatures (60-90°C) and generate substantial waste heat. This isn't incidental
—it's fundamental to how they work.
Conventional Approach

Tobe Approach

Continuous DC operation at 1.8–2.2V

Pulsed waveform operation

High overpotentials → waste heat

Lower effective overpotentials

Active cooling infrastructure required

Near-isothermal operation

65-75% system efficiency typical

>92% system efficiency demonstrated

Balance of plant adds cost and failure modes

Simplified system architecture

THE SCALE CHALLENGE
Meeting global decarbonization targets requires electrolyzer capacity measured in terawatts. Current PGM-dependent
technologies face fundamental material constraints. A new approach using commodity materials isn't just cost-competitive—it's
necessary for scale.

© 2026 Tobe Energy Inc.

tobe.energy

TOB E EN ERGY · TEC H N OLOGY OVERVIEW

04

WHAT TOBE IS
Tobe is a category-level rethink of water electrolysis. Rather than incremental improvements to PEM or
alkaline systems, we've developed a fundamentally different electrochemical architecture that eliminates the
constraints those technologies accept as given.

CORE ARCHITECTURE
SUBSYSTEM

FUNCTION

KEY INNOVATION

Power Electronics

Converts input to controlled pulse waveforms

Proprietary pulse patterns for electrochemical kinetics

Electrolysis Module

Houses electrode stacks and manages electrolyte

Membrane-free design using 304 SS construction

Gas Handling

Separates, dries, and pressurizes H₂ and O₂

Simplified separation (no membrane → cleaner streams)

WHAT MAKES IT DIFFERENT

No Membranes

No Caustic

No PGMs

Near Ambient

ZERO PROTON-EXCHANGE
MEMBRANE

WORKS WITH DILUTE OR PURE
WATER

ZERO PLATINUM OR IRIDIUM

OPERATES <30°C, NO ACTIVE
COOLING

WHERE IT FITS
Behind-the-meter installations — Industrial facilities with variable renewable generation
Hydrogen hubs — Centralized production serving multiple offtakers
Data center backup power — Converting excess renewable capacity to stored hydrogen
Distributed production — Small-scale on-site generation for fleet fueling or industrial processes

TOB E EN ERGY · TEC H N OLOGY OVERVIEW

05

PERFORMANCE
We report efficiency using clear definitions to enable direct comparison. All figures represent measured
system performance, not theoretical or stack-only values.
LHV VS HHV BASIS
Hydrogen's lower heating value (LHV) is 33.33 kWh/kg. The higher heating value (HHV) is 39.41 kWh/kg. We report LHV
efficiency as the industry standard for combustion and fuel cell applications.

MEASURED PERFORMANCE
PARAMETER

VALUE

NOTES

94.7%

~42.23 kWh/kg H₂

Maintains efficiency across operating range

Up to 350 psig

Reduces downstream compression

Operating Temperature

<30°C

Near-ambient, minimal thermal management

Ambient Range

-30°C to +50°C

Outdoor installation without climate control

H₂ Purity

>99%

Higher purity available with standard PSA

WHY THIS MATTERS
At typical industrial electricity rates ($0.04–0.06/kWh), improving efficiency from 70% to 94% reduces the electricity cost
component of hydrogen from ~$2.38/kg to ~$1.77/kg. Over the lifetime of a facility producing 1,000 kg/day, this
represents millions in savings.

© 2026 Tobe Energy Inc.

tobe.energy

TOB E EN ERGY · TEC H N OLOGY OVERVIEW

RELIABILITY
Tobe's architecture eliminates the primary failure modes that limit conventional electrolyzer lifetime. Nothing
in the stack gets "used up" during operation.

DESIGN PHILOSOPHY
Commodity materials — 304 stainless steel uses globally available supply chains

MANUFACTURING PHILOSOPHY
Current State

Production Projection

Prototype systems built using standard fabrication. No specialized
equipment or exotic materials required. Current cost includes NRE
and low-volume inefficiencies.

At MW-scale volumes, cost converges toward commodity
manufacturing. Primary drivers are power electronics and
structural steel—well-understood supply chains.

Prototype cost: ~$446/kW

Projected at scale: ~$177/kW

CAPEX ADVANTAGE
Conventional PEM electrolyzers cost $700–1,200/kW at scale. Alkaline systems range $500–800/kW. Tobe's projected $177/kW
represents ~75% reduction from PEM and ~65% from alkaline.

SUPPLY CHAIN CONSIDERATIONS
No PGM dependency — Eliminates platinum/iridium price volatility and supply constraints
No membrane supply chain — Removes Nafion sourcing as bottleneck
Standard industrial components — Leverages global 304 SS and power electronics base

MEASUREMENT METHODOLOGY
Power measurement: INA260 precision power sensors (±1% accuracy)
Flow measurement: FS1015 mass flow transmitters with NIST-traceable calibration
Purity verification: KE-25 electrochemical O₂ sensors for cross-gas detection
Safety monitoring: TGS821 hydrogen leak detection throughout facility
